# P1_S03 – A Remarkable T eacher’s Legacy

# A Remarkable Teacher’s Legacy
**Story ID:** P1_S03
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Mid-20th century through 1990s (1950s graduates referenced; death in 1994; property transactions in 1998), Education, Balancing rigorous, sometimes unpopular academic demands against students' resistance in a community with limited educational opportunity., High standards and disciplined practice produce la, Private, Consistent intellectual curiosity and exposure to, Communities can preserve and extend a leader's leg

## Summary

A devoted, intellectually curious high-school English teacher in a rural community demanded rigorous standards and memorization, profoundly shaping students' lives and inspiring alumni to preserve her legacy.

## Full Text

A Remarkable
T eacher’s Legacy
Calhoun City is a back woods farming community with limited opportunity for young people who want to reach out beyond the basics. In the midst of this environment, there was a gentle, erudite woman who taught English to the local students for decades. Rather quiet and serious, with a dab of wit about her, Miss Frances Mallory lived a simple life in this rural community. She never married. Family and friends surrounded her, and she had an abiding allegiance to her students. She dabbled in oil painting, but never displayed much of her work. Legend has it she once wrote a novel, but no one seems to have ever seen or read it. She possessed a voracious appetite as a reader and was a student of classical literature, rather rare traits in this community.
Miss Frances lived at 400 North Jackson Street, just one block from Calhoun City High School. Her 1905 home was one of a handful of original residences in the town. Her family owned the property for seventy-five years, and she lived there throughout that period. Like Willy Loman, she “never made a lot of money” and “her name was never in the paper” . She lived a private life, one that was dedicated almost entirely to her students.
Miss Frances’ life was dominated by an unusual intellectual curiosity, continually poking around like a thorn looking for a so ft spot. She managed to arouse in me, and probably in many others,
some of that same curiosity. T o some, she undoubtedly was nothing more than a stern task master who made unreasonable demands and passed out stiff grading sentences for substandard performances. She was tough; there was no doubt about it. Many former students can remember sitting on her front porch (voluntarily or involuntarily),
while Miss Frances sat on the porch swing, providing private tutoring. Perhaps a few of them despised her, but the overwhelming majority recognized her innate fairness and her genuine caring. They also respected her good-natured goading and calm firmness. She once said to me, upon handing back a poor grade, “I’m not going to tell your mother about this, Keith, because you and I both know you can do be tter. ” She had a knack for reasoned argument, as well as for personal humiliation, that made it difficult to quibble with her verdicts. For me, there was more than ample motivation to meet her expectations.
It was Miss Frances who introduced me to memorization. She believed in memorization, and it was an annual April ritual for the senior English class. Learning to recite the prologue to Chaucer’s
Canterbury T ales in Old English was a requirement, which seemed to most students to be a ridiculous exercise in futility and a cause for great consternation. After all, when would one ever find such an abstruse thing useful? Despite all the groaning, Miss Frances was brutal in her demands that each student stand before the class and recite this meaningless collection of arcane words. As testimony to the stickiness of this exercise, at the fiftieth reunion of the 1959
graduating class, a half-dozen students stood in the living room of
Miss Frances Mallory the Mallory House and jointly recited from memory the first twenty lines of this work—in Old English no less—without a single hiccup!
“Whan that Aprill with his shoures soote, the droghte of march hath perced to the roote, and bathed every veyne in swich licour… ” .
Upon her death in 1994, her home, which was her only asset of any signi ficant value, was donated to the First Baptist Church.
Fred Nabors, a local builder and long time friend, subsequently acquired the property in 1998 and meticulously restored it to its original appearance. On an impulse of nostalgia, I purchased the property from Fred and vowed to keep it as a historical memory of this remarkable lady. I think I sort of looked upon myself as a metaphorical Clym Y eobright (in Return of the Native) reconnecting with my roots. The home stands today in pristine condition complete with white picket fence, rose bushes and a manicured lawn. It would qualify as a cover picture for Life magazine. Although the Cobbs have owned it as a second home since 1998, it appropriately sports a bronze plaque labeled “Mallory House” .
Miss Frances was quietly buried in the local cemetery. Her family never acquired a grave marker for it. A few years later, a group of loyal, respectful alumni, admirers of the legendary teacher, chipped in to fund a gravestone. The inscription is telling:
Honorary gravestone for Miss Frances
We truly treasured her legacy. In addition to working together to fund the gravestone, we launched a campaign to establish a scholarship fund in her memory. Those funds now reside with the
Create Community Foundation, which administers the scholarship program. Perhaps this fund will continue to enable a few local students to benefit from this outstanding woman’s legacy.
For me, Miss Frances was indeed remarkable. I have bene fitted from the solid grounding of her tutelage for the past fifty-plus years.
She has prodded me and nudged me for years, in more ways than fundamental English grammar and composition. She opened my mind to countless new thoughts as well as new words. She motivated me to aspire and look beyond my immediate environment, to wonder what might be out there on the other side. I remember sitting in the porch swing (which I still do, from time to time) wishing that I could be as knowledgeable and worldly as she was. Actually, I also longed to be as firm and tough as she was, because I admired her resolve and determination. Without a doubt, I am a better and more responsible person because of her leadership.

## Principles

- High standards and disciplined practice produce lasting knowledge and character.
- Private, firm encouragement motivates improvement more effectively than public scolding.
- Consistent intellectual curiosity and exposure to literature expand students' horizons in limited-opportunity settings.
- Communities can preserve and extend a leader's legacy through collective action (memorials and scholarships).

## Decision heuristics

- When a student underperforms, address them privately and set a higher expectation instead of immediately escalating to caregivers.
- Implement rigorous, repeatable practice rituals to create durable learning (e.g., annual memorization exercises).
- When honoring a mentor, mobilize peers and alumni to fund tangible memorials and scholarships to continue their impact.

---

# P1_S04 – On My Honor

# On My Honor
**Story ID:** P1_S04
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Mid-20th century (experiences include 1957); later reflections through 2000, Youth organization (Boy Scouts of America), Balancing personal tendencies to procrastinate and seek recognition with the disciplined, demanding requirements needed to achieve Eagle Scout and internalize Scout values., Leaders should model the behaviors they expect fro, Require genuine effort before granting rewards to, Consistent mentorship during formative years shape, Use structured milestones and visible recognition, Embed charity and service as routine practices to

## Summary

A personal memoir of Boy Scout experiences emphasizing mentorship, discipline, structured progression, charity, and the lasting influence of Scout values and leaders such as Dr. Carter Dobbs.

## Full Text

On My Honor
There is no finer organization for young people in this country than the Boy Scouts of America. I’m certain of that. My scouting journey began as a Cub Scout at about age ten. I worked my way up to become an Eagle Scout several years later. Scouting represented years of adventure, fun, learning and personal character development. The fundamental fiber of the Boy Scout organization and the quality of the leaders who shepherded me through these years would be di fficult to exaggerate.
My earliest scouting memories begin in the living room of my home, where a small den of Cub Scouts gathered periodically.
Mother was the den mother and guided a handful of guys through various cra fts and games. The young Cub Scouts all had small scouting books with pictures of various animals on the cover. We were issued individual score sheets that kept track of exercises and projects. An added plus was the spiffy blue uniforms we wore to the meetings. The activities taught us teamwork, sportsmanship and a variety of other physical and character traits. We were challenged to advance through the various levels —Bobcat, Wolf, Bear, etc., with an ultimate goal of reaching something called Webelos. At each level, achievement badges were awarded, and I proudly sewed them on my blue uniform as they were awarded. I instinctively liked this scene. The meetings were always fun. The activities were challenging and invariably ended with a sense of achievement. The badges and awards fed my personal desire for recognition. There was a sense of order and structure that appealed to me, not the least of which was that formal uniform that I wore so proudly.
When the time came, at about age twelve, I moved up to the official Boy Scout organization and became a member of T roop
40 as a T enderfoot, the base level of ranking. Mother took some of us on a trip to Reed’s Department Store in Tupelo, where we each acquired a complete uniform with the entire trappings —khaki outfit with cap, scarf, belt, etc. As the years went by, I outgrew the uniforms, and we made other trips for new pants and shirts. As we became designated Explorer Scouts, somewhere along the line, the uniforms morphed into dark green outfits. I have a sharp memory of one fundamental character lesson learned in this uniform migration.
With Mother’s guidance, we always tried to find a less fortunate kid who could not afford a uniform and we would donate my outgrown outfit. I always had a warm feeling about this. This act of kindness and charity seemed to resemble some of the citizenship lessons embedded in the Boy Scout program.
T roop 40 met regularly in the Scout Hut, an old one-room building on a hill, near the football field where community tennis courts stand today. The building was a dark brick-red color, like an old country barn. It was a one-room structure that was empty inside except for an old wood-burning stove that served as a heater. More than likely, it was built for some other purpose and perhaps used for something else at the time, but for us the Scout Hut was our place.
If I were led blindfolded back to this building, the nostalgic smell of the place would be immediately recognizable.
Some extraordinary volunteers led our troop from time to time.
George Swindle comes to mind first. George was a young adult who devoted hundreds of hours to the local scouting program. He was a former scout himself and seemingly knew everything there was to know about the subject. He was a skinny, gangly fellow, a relatively weak guy physically, with several physical limitations, and he had been in ill health much of his life. Nonetheless, his mind was razor sharp and he was undaunted in his devotion to the troop. George later became a lawyer, married and then unfortunately died at a young age. We all owe him a debt of gratitude for his tutelage. There were several others who provided quality leadership and guidance along the way. And then there was Dr. Carter Dobbs, a terrific role model for character, citizenship, patriotism and personal development —
the fundamental things for which the Boy Scouts of America stands.
More about him later.
My Boy Scout Handbook became my virtual Bible at this juncture in my life. It was a three-hundred-plus page book, packed with fascinating information about all phases of personal development—
camping, hiking, first aid, life saving, personal health, citizenship and many other subjects. Mine was ragged around the edges from persistent use. The Handbook contained requirements and guidelines for progressing through the achievement ranks —Second Class,
First Class, Star, Life and Eagle. It also included several symbols of the scout creed that the program required us to memorize, such as the Boy Scout oath, law and mo tto. But it was more than rote memorization; we were expertly guided to learn what it all meant,
and we could explain it in terms we all understood. Sixty years later,
I can still recite the law without faltering: “ A Scout is trustworthy,
loyal, helpful, friendly, courteous, kind, obedient, cheerful, thri fty,
brave, clean and reverent. ” I still know what all that means! T oday,
it means the same thing it meant sixty years ago. The Boy Scout
Handbook was designed to accomplish the avowed purpose of the organization: to produce a “well-developed, well-informed boy” . As far as I am concerned, the members of T roop 40 achieved that goal,
thanks to the Handbook and extraordinary, dedicated leaders.
Over time, I accumulated a lot of Boy Scout paraphernalia,
including all sorts of camping gear. The inventory included knives,
hatchets, cooking gear, first-aid kits and water purification pills—all kinds of stu ff. My personal sleeping bag was a makeshift thing that
I fashioned out of an old quilt by sewing two sides together with fishing line. The crude, homemade appearance slightly embarrassed me, but the sleeping bag served its purpose. I even had a beat-up antique tent that we used a few times. The tent dated back to World
War I and had been handed down in the family from my dad’s Uncle
Vernon, who had been in the Army.
Our camping excursions were terri fic experiences. They were lots of fun and had the added benefit of learning all sorts of new and useful outdoor skills.
I took the ranking system seriously and plodded through the various levels steadily. Steadily, that is, until I arrived at the Life level.
The steps required significant learning and skill demonstration, and much testing along the way. I found the hurdle from Life to Eagle to be difficult. I squa tted at that Life level for a couple of years. Hugh
Steve Swink and I both seemed to be stuck there. By that time, we had other things on our minds. I found myself procrastinating on accomplishing the rigorous achievements in a twenty-one merit badge program. Hugh Steve and I were not alone hanging in this suspension. This is the level where many well-intentioned scouts falter and never make it over the hump. As a ma tter of fact, I have read that less than 5 percent of scouts become Eagle Scouts. The desire for recognition as an Eagle Scout was there for me, but I found my discipline and tenacity to be weak. I felt badly about that and had a sense of shame about my procrastination. Eventually, I buckled down though and got it all done. It was indeed a proud moment for me to stand in the First Baptist Church and receive that sterling silver eagle with the red, white and blue ribbon. It hangs today in a beautiful frame in a prominent spot in my study. The inscription says, “Be Prepared” , an apt message.
I attended Boy Scout camps at Camp Palila in Louisville on two or three occasions and eventually served as a counselor there one year. I supervised a cabin of about a dozen kids and taught lifesaving at the lake. One of my perks was my own private canoe that was used to tool around the large lake. Also, my crowning personal achievement that summer was swimming a mile nonstop —four lengths of the lake. My last signi ficant camp excursion was a trip to the National Boy Scout Jamboree at Valley Forge, Pennsylvania in
1957. It was an unbelievable experience, sprinkled with dozens of larger-than-life memories.
The mentor and motivator-in-chief behind my Boy Scout days was Dr. Carter Dobbs. Dr. Dobbs was a local dentist with an excellent reputation as a superb professional and a respected health care provider in our community. But he also had a second job. He was the local scoutmaster. He took on this responsibility with the same degree of vigor and serious expertise that he applied to his dental practice. He wore the uniform, talked the talk and walked the walk of the Boy Scouts of America. He spent untold hours with T roop 40. He instructed, guided, supervised, disciplined and prodded guys of all ages and all backgrounds as though they all were his sons. He made all the meetings, participated in the hikes and slept on the ground with the troop on the campouts. And he monitored the development and progress of each scout individually.
In his book, one had to do the work—all of it—to achieve the goals before basking in any rewards. Dr. Dobbs didn’t believe in breaks,
gimmies or mulligans. Without a doubt, he was a tough guy and a stern taskmaster. But his rigor never got in the way of us having fun along the way.
There were eleven young boys in T roop 40 who a ttained the
Eagle Scout award under Dr. Dobbs’ direction in those years. That’s an unusually large number for such a small community! Thanks to his son Jimmy Gage, I now have in my memorabilia a treasured photograph of all eleven guys posed together in uniform with their
Eagle Scout medals.
Dr. Dobbs was a serious man who hardly ever laughed—at least as I recall—but a shadow of a smile sneaks out in this photograph. It is the smile of an intensely proud man. It is a smile of accomplishment.
It is a smile that says, “Don’t look at me; look at these guys. ” His pride was certainly well deserved. And I doubt that he ever looked at anyone’s teeth and smiled like that!
Eagle Scouts mentored by Dr. Carter Dobbs
As I look back over my life, I can’t think of a more signi ficant teacher and role model than Dr. Dobbs. His positive leadership helped keep me grounded during these formative stages of my life.
He kept my feet on a positive pathway. I owe him an extreme debt of gratitude for this personal leadership. I stayed in touch with him over the years. I attended his funeral back in Calhoun City a few years ago and stood sadly beside his casket with tears welling up in my eyes.
The lessons of my Boy Scout experiences have followed me throughout my life. As a matter of appreciation and duty, I have tried to keep alive some level of fidelity to the organization over the years.
That allegiance has waxed and waned from time to time, but my heart has always been there with the program. In college, I volunteered to be an assistant scoutmaster with a troop in Ha ttiesburg. Y ears later,
when I was living in Philadelphia, I served as an executive member of the regional Boy Scout Council. I have contributed money to various Boy Scout programs over the years and I have belonged to the National Eagle Scouts Association for many years. In the year
2000, I was honored with a presentation of the prestigious national
Distinguished Eagle Scout Award at a dinner in Miami. In my acceptance remarks, I made sure to mention Dr. Dobbs and pay tribute to the significant role he played in my life.
I am a be tter person today because of the Boy Scouts of
America. I invested heavily in the program as a teenager, but others invested more heavily in me during those years. The returns on these investments cannot be measured in numbers, badges, medals or awards —or even good memories. The returns are represented by the ethical and moral choices that one makes throughout life. In my case, these choices have been heavily in fluenced by the values instilled in me during these impressionable years. The Boy Scout
Oath still lives with me and, I hope, has been a catalyst in keeping me “ …physically strong, mentally awake and morally straight” .

## Principles

- Leaders should model the behaviors they expect from others.
- Require genuine effort before granting rewards to instill discipline and competence.
- Consistent mentorship during formative years shapes long-term character and choices.
- Use structured milestones and visible recognition to motivate progress and learning.
- Embed charity and service as routine practices to reinforce citizenship.

## Decision heuristics

- Model desired behavior by consistently participating and visibly following the same standards as the group.
- Require completion of substantive work before awarding recognition to build discipline.
- Create clear ranks, badges, and score-keeping to turn progress into tangible, motivating milestones.
- Monitor each person's development individually to tailor guidance and accountability.

---

# P1_S05 – A Would-be Music Man

# A Would-be Music Man
**Story ID:** P1_S05
**Part:** One
**Tags:** childhood through high school, music / education, Having natural musical ability but repeatedly avoiding the sustained, technical practice required for mastery, leading to missed formal opportunities and lifelong regret., Lifelong engagement can come from enjoyment even w, Mentor demonstration and support can compensate fo, Be honest with yourself about willingness to inves, Regret follows neglected natural gifts when discip

## Summary

An individual with clear musical talent but limited discipline recounts childhood and high-school musical experiences, mentors, missed opportunities, and lifelong love of music despite not pursuing mastery.

## Full Text

A Would-be Music Man
The story of my musical career is a composition without a finale,
an aspirant’s career that actually never happened. I had grand visions of such a career at an early age, but musical mastery never developed.
Based on the evidence, it was not because of a lack of talent. More likely, it was because of a lack of discipline. Was I just lazy? Or was it all the distractions, fueled by my curiosity about so many things?
Whatever the cause, it is clear that I had some level of talent that I
never fully developed. Although mastery simply didn’t seem to be in the scheme of things for me, I have tinkered with music and musical instruments all my life, and have developed an enduring love for music.
My earliest musical experimentation began with a harmonica at six or seven years old. Blue Cole, our next-door neighbor and self-appointed surrogate father, bought a small Hohner Marine Band harmonica, gave it to me and challenged me to learn to play “Old
Black Joe” . I was fascinated with the instrument and sat under the big oak tree in our front yard for hours playing with it. Before too long, I figured out the basics and learned to play the melody. Blue was ecstatic. When I would play a tune, he would roar with a loud belly laugh and slap his legs. He then put me on display, taking me down to his Standard Oil depot and asking me to play “Old Black
Joe” for his friends and customers. I became his personal monkey and organ grinder!
I do not remember when piano lessons began and I honestly do not recall how many years I took lessons. Not too many, consistently,
I think. My best guess would be that I started in about fourth or fifth grade. My mother recognized that I might have some talent. She enrolled me for private lessons and bought a new Bradbury piano on a rental/purchase plan, a big financial commitment on her part. I
was in and out of piano lessons for a few years, but I never performed well—or even moderately well. Piano lessons were a painful experience for me. I floundered around in a world of dichotomy. I
loved the piano, but I despised practicing all the scales and required exercises in the John Thompson’s method books. When it came to piano practice, I was a certi fiable goof-o ff. My instructors would always report my dalliances to Mother and o ffer up comments like,
“It’s such a waste. He clearly has the ability. ” Mother was distraught,
having sacri ficed financially to buy the piano and then me not performing up to her expectations. There was one embarrassing time when I failed to learn my recital piece and was not allowed to participate, even though my name had been printed in the program.
Mother was humiliated and said, “That’s it. No more piano lessons. ”
Under my breath I said, “Thank you God!”
An interesting development occurred along the way, however.
As I had dutifully sat on the stool supposedly practicing, I began to learn that I could play by ear many of the songs I actually liked. So,
I would spend all the allo tted practice time tinkering with familiar melodies and chords. I have been tinkering ever since.
The Calhoun City schools decided to form a band the year I was in the sixth grade. In order to gin up some interest and populate the band, school officials commissioned an aptitude-testing program for all students above the fifth grade. Approximately a dozen students popped out of this study as having exceptional natural music ability,
and a campaign was launched to recruit them for the band. I was the youngest among that group. Others I recall being singled out included James Bailey Caviness, Hal Jr. Reese, Jerry Sue Stewart,
Martha Ford Harrelson, Jan Cutrell and Nancy Wall. James Bailey was a phenomenal find. He was a macho guy and a popular football player who considered all male musicians sissies. He vigorously resisted the pressure to become a member of the band. The school authorities eventually prevailed on his parents, however, and he reluctantly began playing the trombone. His progress was nothing short of astonishing. Within a year, he was the first chair trombonist at the prestigious Mississippi State Band clinic. It was later determined that he was one of those rare individuals possessing perfect pitch.
The clarinet was my instrument of choice, and I spent the next six years as an active member of the school band. The band was a thoroughly enjoyable experience. Much like my piano experience,
I didn’t spend much time on technical practicing and sight reading,
so I was extremely slow at it. But over time, I sounded good, and after Jerry Sue graduated, I moved up to first chair clarinetist. I was selected to attend the State Band clinic on the Gulf Coast, ostensibly because of my musical talent. Predictably, I had great difficulty with the sight-reading portion of the tryouts upon arriving at the clinic and I was relegated to the last chair in the clarinet section. I was embarrassed to no end, certain that they all regarded me as a fraud and were wondering how I had managed to qualify for the clinic in the first place. I managed to stumble through the first hours of the clinic, memorizing my part as we moved along. Before the clinic ended, I was back on a par with the other clarinet players and felt much better about myself.
Throughout high school I was a regular participant in all sorts of organized music activities. I sang in the Glee Club and the church choir and was a regular in school musical productions. My favorite production was Lelawala, where I had a singing and acting role as an
Indian brave.
When I was in the tenth grade, a small group from the band formed our own Dixieland jazz band. In addition to me, the members included Hal Jr. Reese on trumpet, Phil Ray on trombone, Sonny
Y oung on bass and John Edmondson on drums. Given our meager backgrounds and dearth of formal training, we were actually pre tty good! We began playing our Dixieland gig at various events and talent shows. In due course, we found ourselves competing in the
Mid-South Fair T alent Competition in Memphis, where the winner was promised a spot on the T ed Mack Amateur Hour in New Y ork.
We didn’t win, but we were featured on a local Memphis TV show.
An even more important highlight of this event was the opportunity we had to meet Elvis Presley!
Dixieland band at Mid-South Fair Talent Competition
We also formed a ten-piece dance orchestra in which I played both the clarinet and saxophone. We actually played for a few local dances.
During my junior year, Luther Castleberry, our new band director, suggested I should try out for the Lion’s All-State Band,
a well-known hallmark of success among the music cognoscenti. I
agreed, so he prepared the application for me. The organization sent copies of the required music and requested a tape recording for the audition process. When I first looked at the music, I was staggered by the complexity and tried to beg off. Mr. Castleberry, who played the clarinet fairly well himself, sat down and played the music for me. I
listened. And then he played it again. Before long, I was saying, “Hey,
that’s pretty music. I can do that. ” I learned the pieces from listening to him, without any meaningful reference to the sheet music. With his supervision, I then made the recording. He submi tted the tape,
and a couple of weeks later —Bingo! I was in! That is the good part of the story. When the band assembled at the University of Southern
Mississippi for rehearsals before a trip to New Y ork City, we all sat down for seating tryouts. Once again, I was an abject failure because of my pitiful sight-reading ability. Nevertheless, I managed to learn the music fairly quickly and ultimately emerged as a respectable member of the band.
There is an interesting sidebar to this anecdote. Because of my inadequacies, the director of the All-State Band sent me over to the
U.S.M. fine arts department and stationed me in a practice room with instructions not to come out until I learned all the music. As
I was slaving away in my solitude, I heard a knock on the door. The visitor was Dr. Gomer Pound, professor of clarinet at U.S.M. He approached me and said, “Who are you?” I told him and he said,
“Where did you learn to play like that?” I explained my situation and my awkward predicament and he remarked, “Don’t worry about the sheet music—would you please just play that piece again for me?”
He then asked me to play something —anything—from memory. I
fumbled, then conjured up one of my Dixieland pieces. When I had finished, he said to me, “Y ou are a very talented young man. Surely you know that. But you obviously are not applying yourself very well. Y ou need to come down here to this college so I can teach you. ”
When I returned home from the New Y ork trip, there was a le tter waiting for me from Dr. Pound offering me a $300 scholarship. And that’s how I first began considering a ttending U.S.M.! I never used the scholarship, because it required playing in the college marching band, which I considered too much work.
I had terrific guidance in all my high school music experiences.
Freddie Cox didn’t impart a lot of piano or music theory, but I
learned a lot about music appreciation from him. I absolutely loved to hear him play the piano. He was a true virtuoso. I tried to imitate him, but found it impossible to replicate his virtuosity. Despite his many unusual personality quirks, Freddie was a great mentor.
Libba Jane Harrelson, the church organist, was also a dedicated professional who gave us unusual a ttention and instruction. When
I was sick one summer, she came to my home to visit me and brought a gi ft, the Van Cliburn stereo recording from the famous
Rachmanino ff piano competition in Moscow. I cherished that album and played it hundreds of times. I did have a beef with Libba
Jane, however. She kept the church organ locked, with the key on a ribbon around her neck. I desperately wanted to play the organ and see how it worked, but she was resolute in not le tting me touch it. I
suppose I should forgive her for that!
Perhaps the best music leader in town was Mrs. Minnie Lee
Pryor, the consummate sophisticated lady who made sure everything we did at the church, music-wise, was first class and professional in every respect. I learned a lot from her.
I am indebted to all these people for their leadership.
The outgrowth of all these high school experiences was an intense love and appreciation for music. I liked all types —classical,
folk, rock & roll, blues, gospel, Broadway, marches, jazz, et al. Thanks to the mentioned mentors and others, I did indeed learn about all styles of music. I played in bands, sang in the choir and glee club,
listened to the radio, collected albums and generally centered much of my life in musical themes. That love and appreciation has remained with me for the rest of my life. We have both a grand piano and a Yamaha keyboard in our home today, and I still tinker —just for my own personal enjoyment.
In addition to the activities and mentors, I am grateful for the natural gifts that God gave me. I clearly don’t have perfect pitch or any other virtuoso talents, but I have been blessed with an unusual ear for music. Throughout my life I have failed to develop certain gifts I was blessed with, and regret not being more diligent in mastering musical skills at a young age. Perhaps there was too much going on or perhaps I was lazy insofar as musical development was concerned.
I still have a Hohner Chromatic harmonica today. I pull it out from time to time and play a tune or two, which o ften will include a stanza of “Old Black Joe” . When I do, I always think of Blue Cole,
slapping his legs, pa tting his feet and laughing. I won’t ever forget that he was the one who set me off on this long sentimental journey.

## Principles

- Lifelong engagement can come from enjoyment even without technical mastery.
- Mentor demonstration and support can compensate for formal-skill gaps and create opportunities.
- Be honest with yourself about willingness to invest time; declining commitments may be preferable to half-hearted participation.
- Regret follows neglected natural gifts when discipline is lacking.

## Decision heuristics

- When written music is overwhelming, learn by listening and rehearse with a knowledgeable mentor to create an audition-quality performance.
- If an opportunity requires a commitment you are unwilling to sustain, decline it rather than participate half-heartedly.
- Use allotted practice time to focus on personally motivating material (play by ear) to maintain engagement when technical drills are aversive.

---

# P1_S06 – Bayne Cobb, Exemplar of a Dad

# Bayne Cobb, Exemplar of a Dad
**Story ID:** P1_S06
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Mid-20th century through 2009 (formative years 1940s–1950s; events through 2009), grocery wholesale / trucking / retail (Justice Company, State Cash Wholesale, Rupert’s Supermarket), Reconciling the outward perception of a dominant, micromanaging mother with the father’s quiet but decisive leadership; maintaining family stability and values through economic shocks and personal challenges., Lead by example rather than by instruction, Maintain calm composure under stress; model steadi, Prioritize financial responsibility and avoid debt, Respect and trust others; assume positive regard, Diligent work ethic and consistent service define

## Summary

A son reflects on his father's quiet, steady leadership across family, work, and adversity, highlighting lessons learned by observation over decades.

## Full Text

Bayne Cobb,
Exemplar of a Dad
All during my formative years in the 1940s and 1950s, I
considered my mother the dominant force in our family. She was the most outspoken, developed all the chores and assignments, and micromanaged our daily lives. It was my mother who set the bar and then persistently raised it above our reach. She was also the one who invariably dished out the punishment for misbehavior or generally not living up to her standards. So, I grew up thinking that my mother ruled the roost, so to speak.
What I failed to realize at the time, however, was that there was a gentle guiding force in the background—a quiet man with a strong work ethic who was the basic provider for the family. He was also a man who was grounded in solid principles. It wasn’t until many years passed that I slowly began to realize and appreciate the fundamental character and gentle spirit that formed the backdrop and foundation for our family over the years.
A bit about my father’s background. Bayne Cobb was born and raised in Banner, a tiny, unincorporated, non-descript community in northeast Mississippi. He was a twel fth-generation descendent of the Cobbs who moved from North Carolina to this small locale in the 1839. His father was the local postmaster, an accomplished gardener and an expert fisherman; Papa Cobb was an intelligent man with an easy-going and gentle spirit, a personality trait that he no doubt handed down to his son.
We don’t know a lot about Daddy’s formative years, except that he started working at a young age in sawmills. He went to Bruce
High School, about eight miles away from his home, and played basketball there. That was in the midst of the Great Depression —
tough economic times in Mississippi. I can remember stories of how he walked three miles from his home to catch the school bus each day. After high school, he attended Draughon’s Business College in
Memphis. He then returned home and found a job as a truck driver for The Justice Company in Calhoun City, where he worked for the next thirty-five years.
Daddy was a handsome blade in his young years, judging by some good photographs from that era. He met and married Frances
Clements, a hairdresser from a nearby community, in 1940. They set up housekeeping on South Madison Street in a house deeded to Daddy in lieu of a bonus at The Justice Company. For the rest of their lives they lived at this spot, in this original house and then another on the same property.
Over the years, Daddy moved from truck driver to shipping clerk and then to route salesman, a position he held for about twenty-five years. He drove around Calhoun and Grenada counties each week calling on grocers and country stores, taking orders for groceries that would be delivered the next day. The hours were long, but he loved it.
He thoroughly enjoyed visiting with the store owners, listening and telling tall tales, and cajoling them into buying more merchandise.
Then, abruptly on Christmas Eve in 1965, Mr. Justice appeared at the
Grenada warehouse and announced that the business was closing at the end of the year. My father received a $25 Christmas bonus coupled with a one-week notice of his termination. There was no severance pay. After thirty- five years of faithful service! Our entire family was devastated and frantic, except for Daddy, who seemed amazingly unflustered by it all. Fortunately, in a matter of days, State
Cash Wholesale came along, purchased the warehouse in Grenada and opened a new business there. As if a miracle sent directly from heaven, they asked my father to manage the warehouse. So, he never skipped a beat—not even a day of work —and was employed there for the next ten years. After that, he worked part time for several years at Rupert’s Supermarket, once again in the grocery business that he loved—a business where he had spent the rest of his adult life.
Daddy had many admirable qualities, which I gradually came to appreciate over the years. Although he was not a super intellect or highly educated, he was wise about much of the ways of the world.
T ravelling from store to store and dealing with simple country folk over the years gave him remarkable insight into human nature and a unique understanding of people. He genuinely respected people —
almost everyone —and rarely ever u ttered a negative comment about anyone.
As I have come to realize over time, I learned many valuable life lessons from observing my father. My mother taught me speci fic skills and pushed me into myriad learning environments in school,
church and elsewhere. Notwithstanding her constant hovering and close supervision, however, it was my father who instilled in me the overarching values that have guided much of my life. A few things come to mind quickly. First, he was simply a decent guy. He was an extremely conscientious and responsible man, taking care of his family first and thinking of himself more as an afterthought. He was trustworthy and honest to the bone. He was frugal and very careful with his money. He had an admirable work ethic, diligently applying himself both at his job and at home. He was a nice guy with an abiding good spirit.
I learned from my father mostly through observation. Thinking of him, I am reminded of a business management exercise I saw where the moderator of a large group provided detailed oral instructions on how to perform a task in three minutes. No one could do it. He then passed out detailed wri tten instructions, which yielded the same result. He then said, “Let me show you how it’s done. ” Everyone in the audience then completed the task in less than two minutes.
Daddy wasn’t one to deal out a lot of instructions or spend time in an official teaching mode. Rather, he just plodded along demonstrating his life according to his own personal golden rule. We were always along to watch and learn from his exemplary example.
I have told many people over the years that I never once saw my father really angry. In fact, in all my years, I never heard him raise his voice. Not once. At his funeral in 2009, Reverend Jimmy Vance related this fact to the a ttendees and asked for a show of hands by anyone who thought their family or friends would be able to say the same thing about them. No hands went up. Even in times of normal family stress, Daddy was always cool, calm and collected. I remember once I wrecked the family car coming home from an errand to the grocery, turning it over completely on its side. While Mother was focused on the drama of the situation and my recklessness, Daddy simply rose from his chair, walked over to the accident with me,
called on some friends to help turn the car back over and told me to drive it home. His only comment to me was, “Son, you need to be a little more careful on those dirt roads, especially when the gravel is piled up in the center as it was in this case. ” I got the point! And
I also got a valuable lesson in dealing thoughtfully with adversity.
As a result of this incident and other similar ones, I developed what
I think is an uncommon resilience to hardship. In situations where many other people panic or let excitement overwhelm them, I
usually experience the opposite. An odd sense of calmness seems to overtake me, to the point that I can feel my body relaxing. I attribute this unique trait to a childhood environment where my father was never rattled or discomposed.
Daddy was frugal to a fault—a trait passed down from his parents,
no doubt. He was extremely careful about all things financial. He disliked debt and didn’t want to owe anyone anything. When the new house was built in 1955, there was quite a bit of stress in the family over the costs. He calmly but strongly resisted Mother’s sweet tooth for all the nicer things and ultimately guided the process to a reasonable compromise. They borrowed $7,500 on a twenty-year mortgage from a savings and loan in Grenada. Haunted by the debt,
Daddy immediately began to make double payments each month and ultimately paid o ff the mortgage in seven years. They never owned a car until he saved $1,200 to buy a new Plymouth for cash in
1951. Every car he ever owned after that was paid for in cash. I don’t think he ever bought a new tire in his lifetime. He always had his old ones recapped or bought used recapped tires from Funderburg’s garage. He even bought used shoes from Cumberland’s Shoe Repair
Shop, claiming he liked them because they were already “broken in” .
Sandy and I have kibitzed and joked about Daddy’s parsimony over the years, observing that he was “tight as the bark on a tree” , as he would say. Nevertheless, we both learned valuable lessons from him about financial responsibility. I seriously doubt that I would be in the financial position I am today, were it not for his leadership in this area.
One particularly poignant moment in the many memories of my father occurred during my junior year in college. I came home at Christmas time and announced that Humble Oil & Re fining
Company had o ffered me an internship in New Orleans for the following summer at a salary of $425 per month. Sitting in his recliner
Daddy seemed unusually moved by this announcement. Big tears began to drift down his face (one of the few times I ever saw such an emotion). Mother promptly called me aside and reprimanded me,
saying that my bragging had embarrassed my father because that was more than he had ever earned in his life. I actually didn’t think that was the case, even at the time. He probably did, in fact, earn less than that, but I didn’t think that was the cause of his emotion. I thought those were tears of pride and I still prefer to believe that.
I also learned some valuable lessons in how Daddy treated my mother. As mentioned, Mother was a dominant force in our family.
She inserted herself into every nook and cranny of our lives with her exceptional intelligence and industriousness. Y et she was in ill health much of her life, with one serious ailment after another, both physical and emotional, as I will describe later. In this challenging environment, my father was nothing short of a saint! He readily took a back seat to all of the hustle and bustle that always surrounded my mother, particularly in the smaller, more insigni ficant matters, and was always unflappable. He invariably kept a cool head and focused stubbornly on doing what was right and prudent, quietly and without fanfare. He treated Mother with respect and seemed to have a patient insight into the emotional rollercoaster she was on. In the final years before her death in 2001, he was the consummate doting husband, tending to her every need and desire. I thoroughly admired his faithfulness and commitment for the sixty-one years they were married, and gleaned some truly priceless lessons from his example.
Again, not because he told me anything speci fically; I just watched him.
I also learned valuable lessons in how he treated Sandy and me.
The word that comes to mind when I think of this is respect. For as far back as I can remember, he treated us both like real people, not as little children, pets or inferior subordinates. I spent many hours trailing along behind him in his garden or squatting in the back yard working on cranky lawn mowers. I felt like his partner. Watching and learning all the time. From time to time, he would take one of us along on his sales route. During these long days, he never lectured us or even overtly showed us many things. He merely allowed us to hang around watching him ply his trade. Those were long days, but
I can still remember them vividly. During the tough teenage years,
he seemed to somehow understand me. And Sandy as well. Mother seemed to take a stronger hand with Sandy than me for some reason,
perhaps because she was a girl and more vulnerable. Or perhaps it was because she was simply two and a half years younger and, due to the age gap, couldn’t seem to measure up to what I was doing.
Anyway, Daddy was always uniquely protective of Sandy and defended her rigorously. While at the time, I wondered about the apparent discrimination, I still admired Daddy’s defense of my sister and his watching her back.
Daddy’s life changed dramatically when Mother died. Although he was passionately devoted to her well-being and was bere ft when she passed away, the change in his personality was palpable. It was as if someone switched on the lights! Almost immediately, he morphed in to a more vibrant personality, spread his wings and reached out to the world around him. He had taken care of virtually everything for the last few years, so Mother’s passing really left no vacant gaps or holes to deal with. He mixed and mingled with the town folk in every venue he could think of, with frequent trips to the post office,
grocery stores, drug stores, etc. He was always at church whenever the doors opened. He would go to the grocery several times a week,
usually to multiple stores. He claimed to be shopping for the best prices, but an underlying reason was probably to see people and visit with them. He loved people and they loved him. Universally,
all who knew him considered him friendly, likable, humorous and remarkably fit for his age. Without a doubt, he was a changed man in these final years, I think mostly due to being unshackled from the responsibilities of the previous years.
Daddy’s health was excellent most of his life, up until his last year, when his heart began to fail him. He took good care of himself,
healthwise. He didn’t smoke or drink, and he generally indulged in a moderate diet. I believe the most important factor in his good health,
however, was attributable to the calm, settled way in which he lived his life. He dealt with stress be tter than anyone I have ever known.
Until his final years, when his body began to falter, he was u tterly unshakable in his demeanor. Clearly, his was not a body ravaged with stress. He retired from his grocery work at about age seventy- five,
but remained active, pu ttering around with home repairs, tending his garden and taking care of Mother. He was a master gardener and maintained a full vegetable garden until he was ninety years old —
peas, bu tter beans, string beans, corn, okra, squash, etc. —and of course his prize tomatoes. He drove his car around town every day until he was ninety-one, when Sandy and I insisted that he give it up. This was indeed a painful transition for him, since he had been driving himself places for seventy- five years! He lived alone for the last eight years of his life and took care of himself until he was ninety-two, at which time we had him admitted to a local retirement home.
Although he mildly resisted the move, he found himself immediately happy for the three or four months that he lived there. The reason for this happiness: there were lots of people there. He passed away in the retirement home rather abruptly and without any pain or su ffering on January 4, 2009, two days after his ninety-third birthday. That was a very sad day for me.
There was a time in my life when I would have said I didn’t have much of a relationship with my father. That’s because I didn’t have a full appreciation of what the relationship really was. My admiration and appreciation for him grew steadily over time, mainly because of my own maturity. As I grew older and more introspective, I began to realize the enormous impact he had on my life. I did, in fact,
become closer to him as he aged, staying in regular contact with him,
particularly in the last ten years. I only regret that it took me so long.
But I will be eternally grateful for the relationship and his in fluence on me as a person.
Bayne Cobb was a good man. No, he was a great man! A
tranquil, gentle giant who lived an exemplary life and set an example of citizenship for everyone he touched. I deeply miss him, but feel his presence every day.
Bayne Cobb, “fit as a fiddle” at age ninety
Keith and Dad

## Principles

- Lead by example rather than by instruction.
- Maintain calm composure under stress; model steadiness.
- Prioritize financial responsibility and avoid debt.
- Respect and trust others; assume positive regard.
- Diligent work ethic and consistent service define leadership.

## Decision heuristics

- When faced with sudden job loss, remain composed, assess immediate local opportunities, and accept stable roles that preserve continuity.
- When financing major commitments, prioritize saving and accelerated repayment to eliminate debt sooner.
- Teach through demonstration: show the behavior you want others to learn instead of lengthy instruction.
- Respond to mistakes with calm, practical corrective actions and succinct guidance rather than drama.
- Avoid unnecessary debt for consumer goods; delay purchase until you can pay cash.

---

# P1_S07 – Frances Cobb/u2014.dA Spool of Blue Thread

# Frances Cobb—A Spool of Blue Thread
**Story ID:** P1_S07
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1921–2001 (notable events mid-20th century, e.g., 1940s–1950s), sewing / retail / service, Frances's strong ambition and drive for improved status and education conflicted with limited family finances, declining health, and the emotional cost of her exacting, punitive parenting., Lead through industriousness and skill mastery to, When constrained, Teach practical skills through hands-on instructio, Maintain very high standards and enforce them cons

## Summary

A memoir sketch of Frances Cobb, a hardworking seamstress and mother whose ambition, exacting standards, and health struggles shaped her family's economic life and her children's upbringing.

## Full Text

Frances Cobb—A Spool of Blue Thread
The hill country of Lafaye tte and Pontotoc counties in north
Mississippi was the 1921 birthplace and childhood home of
Mary Frances Clements, one of seven children of Jim and Beulah
Clements. The family was relatively poor, with Jim working in sawmills and farming at various times, struggling to provide for the family and make ends meet. Frances struggled through those years,
trying to make the best of her environment and plight, but at the same time yearning for something better.
After finishing high school at rural T occopola, she set up shop as a beautician in Sarepta, a small community about fifteen miles to the south. Along the way, she met Bayne Cobb, from nearby Banner, and married him in 1940. Bayne and Frances moved to Calhoun City,
where Bayne was working as a truck driver for the Justice Company.
Her entire life took place within a radius of about thirty miles; for the next sixty-one years, it took place mostly on one parcel of property in Calhoun City.
Mother was exceptionally bright and industrious, and when
Sandy and I werevery young, she poured all her energy into our care.
Then, when we were in elementary school, she began to find ways to help supplement the family income and still be home to take care of us. An excellent seamstress, she launched a home-based business as a seamstress. Before long, she was the go-to seamstress for making and tailoring clothing, taking on some of the most di fficult sewing projects in the city.
When Sandy and I were not in school, Mother enlisted us as helpers. I was probably nine or ten years old when she taught me how to match colors and regularly sent me uptown to the Ben
Franklin store with fabric samples to buy matching spools of thread.
I remember one time when she was trying to produce a contrasting seam and didn’t have a color sample. She sent me to the store with a Noxzema container to try to match its dark blue color. If I
did not secure a perfect match, I was immediately sent back to the
Ben Franklin store to get it right. Since the walk there and back was about a mile, I learned fairly quickly. Over time, we accumulated a large inventory of sewing thread, almost as many shades as the store’s inventory. I constructed a couple of racks with finishing nails to accommodate the spools, and regularly kept them organized by color. Using a special tool she purchased, I took over the production of the custom-covered bu ttons and belt buckles that accompanied some of the dresses and other articles of clothing that she made. I
also learned to cut fabric according to the complicated-looking
Simplicity and Butterick patterns; she coached me on how to arrange the pa tterns so as to follow the fabric’s grain and pa ttern without wasting anything. She was exacting. I was expected to be the same.
I can close my eyes and in my mind see Mother working over the Singer sewing machine as it clattered away. Because her eyesight was poor, she would tilt her head to the right, lean over the machine to get as close as she could to the fast-moving needle, and carefully
Frances Cobb, industrious mom push the fabric through. She was always intense at her work, as she was in practically everything. I anxiously listened to the racket made by the sewing machine and watched as the garments took shape.
When the ladies came to the house and oohed and aahed about their new, tailored or repaired garments, I would sit on the sidelines, proud of the work that had been done. One year, she made all the majorette uniforms for the high school band. I was especially fascinated and interested when the girls came over to try those on! Y eah, I peeped!
Same thing for the many fancy evening gowns she made.
When I was about fifteen, Mother began working outside the home. Her first job was at Lunceford’s Bargain Store, south of town,
where she worked six days a week for several years. I worked there with her on Saturdays and during the summer for three years. Her wages were modest, her hours were long and the work was hard. She moved from there to Chandler’s Drug Store on the square, where she was a store clerk and had be tter hours. After several years there,
she was a secretary at Beasley Insurance Agency for several years.
She was diligent at all these jobs and considered each one a step up from the previous one. She was enterprising, incredibly industrious and conscientious all her life.
However, she seemed embarrassed about having to work outside of the home, this being an era when the ideal wife and mother stayed home and devoted herself to family. She was insecure in many ways and seemed to su ffer from a sort of persecution complex and a feeling that she had been denied the life she deserved. She repeated incessantly—it was like a drumbeat—that she had always wanted an education but couldn’t afford it. She was also ambitious and resented that she could not a fford the finer things in life. She was driven to prove her worth to herself and to those around her.
One example was her quest for a new home in the early 1950s.
We lived at the time in a five-room frame house that Daddy had acquired in lieu of a bonus just before she married him. Mother wanted a big brick house with all the amenities. She marveled at the finer homes in town —the Mounger house, the Clint Hardin home, the T .J. Mallory place. They were new and modern, and she desperately wanted a house like one of them. She had an obsession about elevating her status and saw an expensive new house as a status symbol. The ne ttlesome issue naturally was the finances. Daddy’s salary was modest and her income was meager.
I remember si tting on the sofa while she labored for hours over books of floor plans, searching for a solution to the quandary of how to build a big new house with very little money. She finally compromised on something financially reasonable, surrendering the desired brick exterior, air conditioning, enclosed garage and some other features.
Mother’s health was a constant issue for as long as I can remember. It seemed as though she had incessant ailments, literally from head to toe. She underwent numerous surgeries—eye surgery to correct vision problems, a hysterectomy in her early thirties,
stomach surgery, an appendectomy, hip replacements and perhaps others that I don’t recall. She took medications for illnesses, some of them real and some of them perhaps imagined, and became dependent on prescription drugs. Eventually, she resorted to doctor-shopping in order to obtain prescriptions for all of her medications.
Although there is no doubt that she was not a healthy person, I
always felt that the emotional stress that she inflicted on herself was a strong contributor to her ailments. I see her now as a victim of her own mental or emotional disorders. There is also no doubt that drugs and stress dulled her mind and acuity as the years passed. The sharp, intelligent person she was as a younger woman atrophied over the years. It was sad to watch.
When Sandy and I were children, Mother was a strict disciplinarian. From as early as I can remember, she watched over us like a hawk, demanding that we behave and perform up to her towering standards. The slightest misstep was met with swi ft and sure punishment. I remember her sending me out to the willow tree in our yard numerous times to clip my own switch, and then whipping me with it until huge whelps bulged on my legs. Usually,
the instrument of choice was a belt, a fate far worse than the switches.
Mother believed that I should focus completely on academics,
music and working. I loved sports, but she nixed my participation in athletics. Even though I was a dutiful son, this was too much for me. I remember slipping o ff and joining a local baseball team when I was about twelve years old. One time, I was playing second base in a game at Vardaman and made an unassisted triple play, a rare and extremely lucky phenomenon one could only dream of experiencing in a lifetime. There have only been a few such plays in the history of the Major Leagues. I caught a line drive, stepped on the bag at second base, then ran down and tagged the runner from first base. I was walking on air when my ride dropped me off at home,
only to face my Mother. Mother was livid that I had disobeyed her proscription against participating in sports. Punishment was swi ft and intense, as usual. I never told her about the triple play; she would not have been impressed in the least. Over time, I managed to accumulate an incredible baseball card collection that included many famous players and the entire St. Louis Cardinals team. I
spent many happy hours trading cards, analyzing my holdings and reading about the players. Alas, Mother threw them all away when
I was away at college. In her mind, they were a worthless waste of time and space. I was distraught. T oday that collection would be valuable.
Mother was also a taskmaster when it came to chores. As far back as I can remember, in addition to being a seamstress helper, I
had chores and household responsibilities. Besides assisting Mother with wallpapering, painting and other household projects, I worked in the garden, raised chickens, kept the yard mowed and all sorts of other things. Dinner was my responsibility nearly every day during my teenage years and I learned to cook most everything —
vegetables, meats, corn bread, cobbler pies, you name it. I washed clothes and did most of the ironing; back then almost everything was ironed, including jeans, work pants and even the sheets and pillowcases. Mother directed all these activities. She taught me how to do everything and then supervised me to be sure I did them perfectly. Of course, I was never perfect, but I certainly learned a lot in the process.
She made sure we lived in a beehive of activity. There was never a dull moment. As a matter of fact, I do not remember a single instance where I was not hustling to get everything done. I have a mental file cabinet full of memories of school projects, homework, church programs, birthday parties, Cub Scout programs, music lessons,
choirs, dancing lessons, band practice, summer camps, as well as the chores and never-ending jobs—all engineered by Mother. I cannot even begin to describe all the things she taught me and all of the things she had me do, all of the environments she created to ensure that I would learn as much as possible.
I have o ften wondered about her motivation, however. Was it because of her deep love for me? Was it some destiny she envisioned for me? Was it to enhance her own sense of self-worth—and bragging rights? Was she trying to achieve honors and recognition through me? I just know that she was obsessed with me being perfect and was continually disappointed with my imperfect performance.
Unfortunately, there were too many times when I was imperfect and disappointed her. I wasn’t tall enough to reach her high standards no matter how hard I tried. T oo often, it seemed that I could never measure up. Nothing was good enough, no matter how satisfactory my achievements seemed to me. Being selected for a prominent role in the school musical wasn’t enough; it wasn’t the lead role. Being one of nine clarinetists for the All-State Band was not good enough;
I wasn’t first chair. Achieving the rank of Eagle Scout wasn’t cause for celebration; it took me too long. In my first year of college, when I
was awarded a trophy and received widespread praise for achieving the highest academic grade point average among 970 students in our class, I called home to announce the news to my parents. Mother brusquely responded that this was “interesting” and then abruptly changed the subject to begin bitterly complaining about the money
I was spending.
These experiences and others seared me deeply, and I have only recently begun to see them in perspective. Re flecting on my life, I
have become increasingly aware of my mother’s enormous influence on me, positive as well as negative —the realization that there were many positive influences has settled in. I am indebted to her for much of my education, both formal and informal, my strong work ethic,
my fundamental value system and ethical makeup. I am indebted to her for instilling self-discipline in me. Despite all the demons in her head, she paid close attention to her children and went to enormous lengths to motivate and guide us. I shall be forever grateful for all of this.
I feel the need to add that Mother also contributed to some characteristics of mine that might or might not be so noble. For example, I have often felt that my persona was inexorably hardened by having to deal over the years with her fundamental unhappiness and unrest.
Perhaps there is something I could have done to relieve her bitterness, pain and suffering, but I was never able to figure out the formula. I truly wish I had been able to do that. It was sad to witness her insecurity and feelings of persecution, and to see her suffer from so much physical and psychological distress. We all loved her and wanted her to be happy, but we honestly did not know how to help.
Mother lived to be eighty years old, but the yearning and hunger she experienced as a young woman never seemed to subside. If anything,
it worsened with time.
I recently read a novel entitled A Spool of Blue Thread by Anne
Tyler. It is a poignant and insightful story, sensitively written, about the inner workings of a complex Baltimore family. The family drama reminded me so much of my mother and my childhood that I
decided to plagiarize the title for this chapter. It seemed ironically congruous, even though the Ben Franklin store isn’t mentioned.
In September of 2001, Sandy and I stood together beside
Mother’s casket and commented to each other that she looked peaceful for the first time that we could ever remember—the change in her face was miraculous. That powerful thought made the normal grief a little more bearable. The idiomatic phrase “rest in peace” was certainly appropriate for Frances Cobb.

## Principles

- Lead through industriousness and skill mastery to create economic opportunity.
- When constrained, pursue practical compromises rather than ideal solutions to make progress.
- Teach practical skills through hands-on instruction and close supervision to build competence.
- Maintain very high standards and enforce them consistently to instill discipline.

## Decision heuristics

- When resources are limited, prioritize financially reasonable compromises so progress can continue.
- Build capability by involving learners in real tasks early and assigning responsibility.
- Enforce standards through direct supervision and correction to accelerate skill acquisition.
