# P1_S08 – Blue and Ruby Cole

# Blue and Ruby Cole
**Story ID:** P1_S08
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Mid-20th century through early 21st century (WWII years to 2008), Tension between parental authority (mother's strict discipline and resentment) and the Coles' surrogate parenting/meddling, and later the responsibility of caring for and managing Ruby's affairs culminating in an unexpected estate distribution., Consistent generosity of time and small acts build, Long-term devotion and caregiving can lead to unex, Protecting a child may justify shielding them from, Small

## Summary

Neighbors Blue and Ruby Cole acted as surrogate parents, offering time, protection, and practical help across decades; their long devotion culminated in Ruby leaving her estate to Sandy and Daddy, a surprising reward for years of care.

## Full Text

Blue and Ruby Cole
Sandy and I were blessed in our childhood years to have some wonderful next-door neighbors. Blue and Ruby Cole lived in a small frame house next to ours on South Madison Street. They worked hard and steadily for most of their entire lives, well into their seventies. They labored together on the factory floor at Harrelson
Manufacturing Company during the World War II years. Later, Blue operated the local Standard Oil gasoline depot and Ruby did sewing and odd jobs at Ruth’s Draperies.
These people were the salt of the earth, at least from my vantage point. Oddly, they regularly attended different churches. Blue was a
Methodist and sang in the choir at Lewis Memorial Methodist. Ruby attended First Baptist, across the street, where she was responsible for the flower arrangements every Sunday for as far back as I can remember.
Blue’s real name was Dewi tt T . Cole. I never actually knew where he acquired his nickname or what it signified, but “Blue” was what everyone called him. Blue was a Freemason and Ruby was a member of Eastern Star. These were always mysterious organizations that aroused my curiosity, with all sorts of secrets and such. I never could figure out what the Masons were all about. But I always heard that Blue was high up in the organization and considered a unique authority on the organization in our little part of the world. He was said to be a thirty-second or thirty-third degree Mason, whatever that meant. Based on the hints they dropped occasionally, I had the notion that both organizations had something to do with good works and the promise of rewards in the afterlife based on those good works. But that may have just been my imagination. I simply know that the organizations werevery important in their lives.
Blue’s health deteriorated as he aged, but he never lost his good humor or the twinkle in his eye. He died in 1995 at the age of ninety,
leaving Ruby to live alone for the next thirteen years.
Blue and Ruby never had children, though they always seemed to have a ffection for kids. They practically adopted Sandy and me,
and appointed themselves as virtual surrogate parents. They gave us both nicknames —I was “Bud” and Sandy was “Dune” —when we were small children and continuing practically all our lives. I
do not know how they arrived at these nicknames, but that’s what they always called us. They both were extremely generous with their time, playing with us and teaching us things as we hung out at their house. They would occasionally catch themselves interfering with our upbringing (or meddling in our family a ffairs, as Mother would characterize it). They were o ften openly critical of the strict discipline that we lived under and didn’t hesitate to choose sides when we were being punished.
I remember once when I was playing baseball in the front yard with Blue and accidentally swung the bat the wrong way, hi tting
Ruby in the head and in flicting a big gash. Blue rushed her to the doctor, who repaired the injury with several stitches. When they returned from the doctor with Ruby’s head all bandaged up, Mother happened to be outside and gasped, “Gracious alive, Ruby. What on
Blue Cole, self-appointed surrogate father
Ruby Cole, the consummate next-door neighbor earth happened to you?” Fearing I would undoubtedly be punished,
Ruby rapidly cooked up a tall tale of how she had stumbled going down the back door stairs and hit her head on a concrete block. I just stood there in amazement as she lied through her teeth, while Blue just winked at me and smiled.
Mother always resented their doting attention. One time when I
was very small, I had begged her for a wagon, a Radio Flyer that I had seen in a catalog. She had refused, saying that it was too expensive and I didn’t need it anyway. Then Blue came home one day, in his dark green Standard Oil truck, with a bright red Radio Flyer strapped on the side. He dismantled it, tied a rope to the wagon tongue and began driving up and down the gravel street pulling me in the wagon. Mother witnessed what was going on from her kitchen window and came outside screaming at Blue, using language that was a bit strange for me. Blue just shrugged it all off and said, “Well,
he wanted it, and I wanted him to have it. That’s just all there is to it,
Frances. Y ou should just cool down!”
Our family did not own a car until 1951. Before then, Sandy and
I walked the half-mile to school every day. Whenever the weather was particularly cold or inclement, Ruby would crank up her old black
Plymouth early in the morning and then appear on our doorstep volunteering to take us to school. Mother would reluctantly agree,
mumbling in the background about how she resented having to rely on others to look after the welfare of her children.
I remember so many small things about these wonderful people.
I still remember Ruby’s hugs. Her skin was the so ftest of anyone I
have ever known. I remember her cats, and how she doted on them.
I remember Blue ge tting spruced up for choir practice during the week; he would shave and put on a fresh clean shirt, but he smelled like an odd combination of gasoline, oil, aftershave lotion and toothpaste. I remember him si tting on the floor with me lining up dominos in an S shape and laughing as I toppled them over. I
remember them both laughing a lot and having fun with us, always a refreshing interlude.
During my teenage years, Blue and Ruby moved to another part of town. Although we didn’t see them every day as in the past,
we visited them regularly. Over the years, whenever I was back in
Calhoun City visiting, I always tried to make a point of stopping by to visit with them. On one of my last visits, Ruby was almost one hundred years old, living in a nursing home, almost deaf and practically blind. She was si tting in a recliner in her room, sound asleep. I pinched her on the toe and said, “Ruby… ” . She immediately jumped and said, “Is that you, Keith?” Deaf as she was, she had recognized my voice from a dead sleep. I stood there shedding a tear or two, recognizing that this was a crystalline re flection of Ruby’s love and affection for me.
However, it was Sandy who was the most devoted to her, and that devotion was reciprocated. During Ruby’s waning years, Sandy and Daddy looked after her every need. They visited her in the nursing home regularly. Daddy disposed of all her household goods and sold the house for her when she became too feeble to live there alone. Sandy managed all her personal affairs and kept her abreast of the local news.
When Daddy was disposing of Ruby’s household items, I
happened to be in town and went by to look over some of the things remaining in the house. I decided to take a small end table that still contained the set of dominos that Blue and I had played with when
I was a child. The next week, I visited Ruby at the nursing home and mentioned to her, “Ruby, I want you to know that I took a piece of your furniture down to my house. ” Without hesitating, she looked at me and said, “Oh, I’ll bet you took the domino end table. ” I have that little table, with the original dominos still inside, sitting in the living room of the Mallory House today.
Ruby died peacefully in 2008 at age one hundred. She had no close relatives. When her will was read, it came to light that she had left her entire estate to be divided equally between Sandy and
Daddy. Unbelievably, as her safety deposit boxes were emptied and her certificates of deposits collected and tabulated, she had a sizable estate. The big question in our minds at the time was (and still is),
where on earth did Ruby Cole get that kind of money?
She had lived practically as a pauper for as long as we could remember, a hardscrabble life pinching pennies and scrounging every day of her life. Sure, she was thri fty and probably saved every nickel she could, but one would have to save a huge slice of personal income for a lifetime to accumulate that much money.
The origin of the money is still a mystery. But the money was real and there to count. We had a theory or two, but those ideas were more speculations than anything else. When Daddy passed away a few years later, we transferred his half of Ruby’s estate over to Sandy.
Ruby’s bequest was a marvelous reward for Sandy’s dedication to her over so many years.
These are all terri fic memories. I am grateful for having known this wonderful couple for so many years and for the legacies they left for Sandy and me, both material and emotional gifts.

## Principles

- Consistent generosity of time and small acts builds deep, lasting emotional bonds.
- Long-term devotion and caregiving can lead to unexpected tangible rewards.
- Protecting a child may justify shielding them from consequences, even by bending the truth.
- Small, everyday behaviors contribute to enduring legacies—both emotional and material.

## Decision heuristics

- If a child's simple wish brings clear joy and caregivers refuse, consider providing the gift directly to create lasting positive memory.
- When an elderly person becomes unable to manage, proactively visit regularly and assume practical responsibilities (dispose property, manage affairs) on their behalf.
- If a child faces certain punishment from caregivers, quickly create a protective, plausible explanation to shield them.

---

# P1_S09 – A Work Ethic Develops

# A Work Ethic Develops
**Story ID:** P1_S09
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1940s–1950s (including summer 1953), Family businesses, retail, agriculture, services, Balancing childhood/play with heavy work responsibilities and choosing between immediate work opportunities and long-term education/career goals., Instill work as a fundamental part of childhood de, Hands-on tasks create skill, pride, Fairly share proceeds when others provide capital, Maintain confidentiality in business matters, Trust and responsibility accelerate learning and l

## Summary

Narrator describes growing up with early assigned chores, family small-business work, and successive summer jobs that developed entrepreneurship, skills, and savings habits.

## Full Text

A Work Ethic Develops
Bayne and Frances Cobb began instilling a work ethic in their children at a young age. Work was considered a fundamental part of childhood development. The process probably began at about age six, with definitive assigned chores around the house. These quickly developed into routine responsibilities. My earliest memories of chores include helping my dad with the vegetable gardening and assisting my mother with washing and folding clothes. Making our beds daily and keeping our clothes and toys picked up were basic responsibilities from as early as I can remember. These duties progressed quickly to more advanced chores.
As I mentioned earlier, Mother had a small business sewing for the public, and one of my early jobs was to make covered bu ttons and belt buckles for her customers; she sewed the bu ttonholes and made the belts. She had a simple mechanical die cutter and press that
I learned to operate when I was about ten years old. I would fold the material, cut circles with the die cu tter to cover the bu tton-shaped metal disks and use the press to crimp the material firmly onto the disks. I made hundreds of these buttons over time. The belt buckles were a little more complicated, but I mastered that too. I actually enjoyed it. The finished products were a source of pride; they never had a wrinkle in the fabric or a crimp in the shape. When they were finished, I would line them up in a row and stare at them with pride.
My sense of accomplishment was sometimes burnished when the customers were complimentary and talked about how remarkable it was that I was doing these things at such a young age.
I also became my mother’s color coordinator, of sorts. I became an expert at color matching, at least in my mind. And, like the button customers, the ladies at the Ben Franklin store would o ften be complimentary. They said things like, “Can you believe that child can get those colors right every single time?” I always beamed and was proud. Like most kids, I enjoyed praise.
Daddy decided I needed to be exposed to outside work and suggested that I try raising chickens. So we ordered fifty baby chicks by mail. Y es, you could actually order baby chickens by mail! I
remember going to the post office and picking them up. There were two boxes of twenty-five chicks each. Daddy built a wire mesh coop for them and bought the feed for them, but I was assigned the job of feeding and watering the flock daily. Periodically, I also had to clean the manure from the cage, a despicable job. The daily grind of taking care of the chickens was drudgery and interfered with other things I
wanted to do, but I actually enjoyed watching the chicks grow.
When they were fully mature, it was time to prepare them for market. At this point, Mother stepped into the picture, and the two of us killed and dressed all fifty chickens in two days. I chased them around the large pen and caught them with a bent coat hanger,
then killed them by wringing their necks. We then soaked them in boiling water, plucked the feathers and butchered them. So, there I
was: eleven years old in our back yard with a large butcher knife in hand, chopping up dead chickens! And Mother made sure I did it correctly. We took the dressed chickens to town and sold them to
Calder’s Grocery on the square.
I don’t recall the dollars the chickens yielded, but I do remember that Mother and Daddy split the proceeds with me. She and Daddy explained that they had paid for the baby chicks, built the cages and bought the feed, and therefore deserved a portion of the proceeds,
even though I’d had the large manual task of raising the chickens.
This was my first lesson in entrepreneurship and division of profits.
My first real job outside the home was with the Mississippi
Valley Gas Company in the summer of 1953. I was twelve years old.
Mrs. Flora Mae Wilson, office manager of the gas company, hired me to sit at the front desk and serve as a receptionist. I worked five days a week for four or five hours each day and was paid $2 per day. From time to time she gave me small odd jobs, but most of the time I just sat there and answered the phone. One job assignment was to file the meter readers’ tickets with customer account cards that were kept alphabetically in a big gray filing tray. As I plodded along with this boring chore, I couldn’t resist reading some of the files. I was curious,
even at that age. Several delinquent accounts popped up, and I asked
Mrs. Flora Mae why some of the customers were not paying their bills. She replied abruptly, “Son, that’s none of your business. Y ou just forget about what you saw and don’t worry yourself about that.
And another thing; don’t mention those customers to anyone! Y ou understand me?” It was my first lesson in confidentiality.
At the end of the summer I went to the post office with some of the money I had saved and bought two $25 Series E U.S. Savings
Bonds for $18.75 each. I understood that they matured in ten years,
so I put them away in a safe place. T en years later, Dot and I went to the Five Points branch of First National Bank in Jackson two weeks after we were married and cashed the bonds. We walked directly from the bank to the Kroger supermarket and used these funds to buy our first groceries.
The next two summers were spent working at various things almost full time. I tried to play a little baseball in those summer days, but Mother never thought that was a good idea. I had to slip around to do it and always got in trouble when she found out. She thought I was old enough to be working full time and insisted that
I get busy with something productive. So I called on some of my earlier entrepreneurial experiences and launched two businesses —
peddling tomatoes and providing lawn maintenance services. I still kept up the button and belt buckle assistance, but there was little or no compensation for that.
I planted two long garden rows of tomatoes with Daddy’s careful supervision. Daddy was an expert on tomatoes and had a reputation for the best tomatoes anywhere around, but these were my tomatoes and my responsibility. I bought the plants at the co-op and paid for them out of my savings. I had actually learned something about the value of capital investment from my chicken venture. When the tomatoes began to ripen and were picked, I would load them up on a little red wagon and begin my marketing expedition. Sandy was my helper, of sorts, and trailed around with me on the sales calls. I called on all the local groceries and then went house to house. I didn’t have any scales, so I sold them by the gallon, while being sure to pile an extra one or two on top to guarantee customer satisfaction. This was a piece of advice from Daddy. I have no idea how many tomatoes
I sold over the two summers, but it was a large number. I can still remember my pockets stuffed with quarters and dollar bills. It felt so good to realize that I had started a venture from scratch and earned real money through my conscientiousness and hard work. This time
I didn’t have to split my earnings with anyone!
I also mowed lawns—a lot of lawns. This was hard, sweaty work in the blistering heat of summer. But the money was good. I learned a lot about the nature of people through my customer relationships.
Some were cheap, always requesting extra work without extra pay.
Others were generous to the bone, always paying a fair price and offering me refreshments while I worked. My best customer was
Mrs. Ethyl Cooner, who paid top dollar and always tipped. This seemed like a strange practice, but I was always grateful, and I
respected this generous woman until the day she died. Lawn service was also an experience that taught me a lot about mechanics. Lawn mowers were invariably unreliable in those days, and ours seemed to be particularly cranky. By necessity, I learned about spark plugs and carburetors, and o ften found myself crouched under a shade tree, changing blades, taking engines apart and replacing rings and bearings.
So much for entrepreneurial ventures. By age fifteen, I had moved on to regular employment. Mother was working full time for
H.W. Lunceford in his bargain stores, so I began working with her on Saturdays, their busiest days and my day o ff from school. When summer arrived, I began working full time. I never worked outside the home on weekdays during the school year, although I had plenty of responsibilities at home, as I mentioned earlier. Since Mother worked fulltime, I cooked a full dinner practically every evening,
did most of the washing and ironing for the family and much of the cleaning. Sandy helped, but she was two and a half years younger and thus carried a somewhat reduced responsibility at the time.
I did a little of everything at Lunceford’s Bargain Store. I
unpacked and organized merchandise, waited on customers, swept the floors, kept the place clean and orderly, counted the money,
made the bank deposits, painted sale signs and did whatever else needed to be done. It was truly a learning factory for me. H.W. liked me and continued to dish out new responsibilities over time. He had an old green Plymouth, with loudspeakers mounted on the top,
that he used to drive around and advertise the store. Before my first summer ended, he had me driving the Plymouth and pitching store sales over the loudspeaker. I drove all over town and all through the cotton fields trying to roust people out of their houses or get the attention of the field hands.
I remember that during my second summer, a salesman came through town with a truckload of surplus merchandise, including some cheap umbrellas made in Japan. He said he had a “gross dozen”
(a new term for me) that he would sell us for $5 a dozen for as few or as many as we wanted. I made a rapid calculation and suggested to H.W., “Let’s offer him $400 for the whole shipment. ” H.W. asked me if I knew how many umbrellas were in a gross dozen, and I boldly told him 1,728 (reasoning that a gross was 144, so a gross dozen must be 144 x 12). He reflected for a moment, smiled wryly and then said, “Let’s do it. ” I especially remember the exhilarating feeling I got from the “let’s” in his comment. He was treating me like a partner!
After some mild haggling, we finally settled on $432 for several huge boxes of umbrellas, which amounted to exactly $0.25 each. We split the shipment between stores in Calhoun City, Vardaman, Bruce and Houston, and priced the umbrellas at $1. They weren’t great umbrellas, but they were certainly worth a dollar, I thought. They looked good—red, blue, yellow and green plaids—and they worked,
at least initially. I painted large signs and we left instructions at each store to position the umbrellas at the front door any time it rained.
They went like hotcakes! By the end of the summer, we had sold the entire lot at $1 apiece, for a whopping profit of almost $1,300. H.W.
loved to tell the umbrella story, claiming he didn’t know what a gross dozen was and bragging about me talking him into the deal.
One day the next summer, H.W. walked into the Calhoun City store, handed me a set of keys and said, “Keith, I want you to run the Houston store this summer. ” Just like that. So, the next day there
I was in Houston, Mississippi at eight a.m., unlocking the front door of Lunceford’s Bargain Store and opening up for business. I
worked there six days a week all summer with a lady helper and full responsibility for everything. H.W. would call occasionally to see how things were going and he would send fresh merchandise from time to time, but he never once came to the store. Looking back on it, the level of con fidence he placed in a seventeen-year-old kid is almost incredible. It’s just as incredible how much a seventeen-year-old kid can learn in an environment like that in a couple of months.
My Lunceford Bargain store career ended when I went o ff to college. H.W. actually tried to talk me out of college and offered me a percentage of his business if I would stay and work with him. I was honored and humbled by his con fidence and generosity, but my head was in a different place.
At the time, I felt as if I had been missing out on a lot of life,
working every Saturday and six days a week during the summer all through high school. In retrospect, those were some of the most fruitful days of my life. I could probably fill an entire book with anecdotes from those years. And, in reality, I probably didn’t miss out on as much as it seemed at the time. I was always allowed time off for band trips, Boy Scout events and other things.
T oday I am extremely thankful for these experiences and grateful for the leadership of my parents in guiding me in these directions.
These experiences take me up to my college days. When I le ft home for college, I le ft with a virtual career under my belt. I had experience in manual labor, office work, entrepreneurship, retailing,
store management and the solid values that come from hard work and responsibility.
I worked each summer while I was in college. The first summer
I worked for H.W. ’s brother Rupert in his new grocery store, with a smaller second job of handling insurance paper work for Dr. Durbo
Harrison, a new young physician in town. The second year I worked for Harry Robinson in the M.M. Winkler & Associates CPA firm in Bruce. I spent the third summer in New Orleans on the internal audit staff of Humble Oil & Refining Co.
I hit the ground running in college, bu ttressed with an outstanding public school education and a fully developed work ethic that most people do not have the opportunity to see at such an early age. I was fortunate in these respects. The lessons learned, both at school and at work, would serve me well in the years ahead.

## Principles

- Instill work as a fundamental part of childhood development to build habits and responsibility.
- Hands-on tasks create skill, pride, and quality workmanship.
- Fairly share proceeds when others provide capital or resources.
- Maintain confidentiality in business matters.
- Trust and responsibility accelerate learning and leadership development.

## Decision heuristics

- Accept meaningful responsibility when offered to accelerate practical learning and leadership.
- Turn earned savings into long-term secure investments rather than immediate consumption.
- When evaluating a bulk inventory opportunity, compute unit cost and likely resale price before committing.
- Share profits proportionally with those who provided capital or infrastructure.

# P1_S10 – Roominations

# Roominations
**Story ID:** P1_S10
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Desire to explore the wider world and pursue knowledge versus local cultural expectations that young people remain close to family and community., Create a private physical space to enable reflecti, Sustained curiosity is a primary driver of learnin, Assemble and use information resources and media t, When opportunity appears

## Summary

A teenager describes how a small private bedroom, books, radio and music fostered curiosity and reflection, leading him to seize a university opportunity and leave his insular hometown.

## Full Text

Roominations
Age thirteen was a transformational time for me, as I reached a development stage where I began to think for myself and develop a sense of wonder at the world around me. I was beginning to find my way. It was also a transitional time for my parents as they began to realize an ambition—their dream to own a new home.
After years of planning and saving, Bayne and Frances negotiated a deal with Curtis Ball to construct a brand new house.
They watched as workers jacked up the small home they had lived in for so many years and moved it across the ditch to an adjacent lot so that construction could begin on the newly vacated property.
The home construction fascinated me. I watched practically every board and nail put in its place as the home took shape. I knew every detail about the house—the lumber, drywall, hardware, paint,
plumbing, electrical —every detail. It had Masonite siding with
Permastone trim on the front. Large blueprints detailed 1,780 feet of floor space. There were hardwood floors throughout and the kitchen had built-in appliances, an emerging innovation at the time.
The house cost $12,500. Daddy took out a $7,500 mortgage, his first and only debt. He never stopped worrying about it and made double
Childhood home at 401 South Madison Street payments each month until the mortgage was completely retired seven years later.
The most important detail for me was that for the first time,
Sandy and I had our own rooms. My room was on the north end of the house, opposite my parent’s master bedroom and Sandy’s bedroom, and it provided a modicum of privacy for the first time in my life. The room was not huge—about 11 feet x 11 feet—but it was large enough for me to carve out a private existence.
My bedroom was my personal place, with my own personal things. I loved it. It had brand new walnut bedroom furniture, with a matching chest and desk. The bed had a bookcase headboard, and
I could tuck away things that I didn’t want out in the open simply by sliding the little door open and then closing it. A small radio fit neatly into the headboard. With savings from working odd jobs,
I ordered a new stereo hi- fi phonograph, which rested on a stand next to my desk. I also purchased several 33 1/3 stereo records with some of my earnings. Over time, I built up a good collection of great albums, which I stored in a rack under the stereo and played hundreds of times.
A set of 1948 Compton’s Encyclopedias occupied most of a small painted bookcase tucked into the corner of the room. My mother had purchased the encyclopedias for the family under an installment plan and made monthly payments for two or three years, as I recall.
The bookcase also housed my baseball card collection, Boy Scout manuals and miscellaneous keepsakes. Around the room and on the walls, I had photographs, certificates and memorabilia from school,
church, sports teams, the high school band, Boy Scout events and other reminders of my small world.
My room was warm in the winter, a cozy place for a teenager to snuggle and dream in privacy. It was also warm in the summertime!
The house was not air-conditioned. An a ttic fan kept a breeze flowing through the open screened windows. This breeze brought in the distinctive odors of the community —the smells of fresh cut grass and honeysuckle in the summer. It also allowed in the various sounds of the community —birds chirping, the steady hum of the cotton gin two blocks away and the vibrating drone of crickets somewhere out there.
Music filled my room most of the time. I owned the soundtrack from My Fair Lady and listened to the beautiful music as I memorized every word of every song. I also had the soundtrack from The Eddy
Duchin Story, featuring Carmen Cavallaro on the piano. I must have played it a thousand times. I listened hundreds of times to Van
Cliburn’s recording of T chaikovsky’s Concerto in B Flat Minor and his historic performance of Rachmanino ff’s Concerto No. 3 in D
Minor. His artistry and the haunting melodies of the Rachmaninoff music were mesmerizing. When I hear it today, more than fifty years later, it takes me back to my solitude in this small room.
Late at night, I tuned my little orange radio to WWL, a 50,000-watt station in Chicago, which seemed to me to be a million miles away. The station delivered wonderful music, brought to me over the airwaves by loquacious DJs who seemed to know everything about contemporary music. I would close my eyes and wonder what
Chicago might be like and what these DJs were really like in person.
I also listened to sports on my radio. Mostly, I was interested in the St. Louis Cardinals. Although I had never travelled to many places outside of Calhoun City, I could listen to that little orange radio and imagine myself in Busch Stadium in St. Louis, si tting beside Harry Caray as he broadcast the Cardinal games over station
KMOX. I imagined that Harry was my best friend and that I was a valuable assistant to him because I knew so much about the Cardinal players. I even learned to mimic his unique voice as he stretched the first syllables of words and varied the pitch of his commentary.
“Hooooly Cow!” Maybe he could use some help, I thought. I knew the statistics of all the Cardinal players by heart. I knew every obscure fact about Stan Musial, even the fact that he played the harmonica, as
I did. I could lie there on my bed and visualize Stan the Man vividly in my mind —Number 6, left-handed, standing eagerly at first base and later hi tting a triple o ff the right field wall. It was almost real.
Well before the age of sports on television, all these fantasies played out in virtual T echnicolor right there in my little domain.
The encyclopedias were more than a mere adornment for my room. They represented my personal portal to the rest of the world.
I would spend endless hours thumbing through the fifteen volumes.
I routinely read at random about hundreds of things I knew nothing about. Sometimes I would just read through them alphabetically.
I would look up Poetry in search of some fact, then flip the pages and find myself reading about Poisonous Plants, only to flip the pages again and find fascinating information about Poland—and so on through the material. All manner of arcana seemed to be at my fingertips. There was so much I didn’t know! Curiosity welled up inside me.
I took the things I learned in my high school classes back to my personal space—my room—to contemplate and re flect. It seemed that so much of my life gravitated to this room, as though some magic magnet was trying to bring the universe into focus for me,
there in my personal window to the world.
The local culture was insular, at least in my view at the time.
The expectation was that all young people would live out their lives close to their families and would be unquestionably loyal to the local community. High school graduates who went to college normally attended a nearby community college, Ole Miss or Mississippi
State, all of which were less than fifty miles away, came home every weekend and then for good once their college careers ended. Only a handful ventured beyond this area, and most of those never returned. I admired the loyalty and the glue that bound these folks together, and I still do. However, I reasoned —perhaps selfishly—
that it wasn’t for me.
It was in this private place that I began to let my mind wander and began to set my sights on the future. Here in this small room,
much of my fundamental philosophy and destiny developed. I
can remember marveling at all I read, wondering where all these things were. Most everything I saw in Calhoun City could be characterized as a simple life, certainly not as complex and worldly as the thousands of topics covered in the books, school, the radio and musical recordings I was being exposed to. I wondered about what might be on the other side, in places beyond what I had seen.
How were all these things accomplished? Where were these creative people who did such spectacular things —music, art, literature,
engineering, government, travel, sports, etc.? Were they available for me to experience? Would I ever see such things? Surely, they wouldn’t come to Calhoun City! So, with this backdrop of incessant questions, I began to plot my exodus.
Serendipity arrived at my doorstep. I had a chance to a ttend the University of Southern Mississippi, two hundred miles away in Hattiesburg. I seized this opportunity, much as an animal seizes its prey. Thus began my journey toward another world. I was so fascinated by my new surroundings that I only returned home three times my freshman year—Thanksgiving, Christmas and Easter—to the dismay and consternation of my parents. From there I seemed to never stop wandering and I never lost my lust for knowledge and adventure.
There have been lots of times over the last forty or fifty years when I missed my family and old friends. But honestly, the thing
I really missed the most about my hometown surroundings was the solitude and contemplation that took place in that 11 feet x 11
feet room on South Madison Street —a truly emotional place for me. Fi fty years later, just before we sold the house following my father’s death, I made one last visit there, alone. The house had been stripped of all its furnishings. I went into that empty room on the northeast corner of the house and sat down on the barren floor for about an hour. Five years of the most critical time in my life dri fted through my mind as I revisited and cherished the chunks of warm memories of those days in my room. I vaguely recalled an old poem that I had once memorized. It had a verse that went something along the following lines: “My old and cherished friends from years gone by, now changed they are as figures in a dream. But this special place remains the same and most certainly will always reign supreme. ” My room was a special place!

## Principles

- Create a private physical space to enable reflection, identity formation, and concentrated learning.
- Sustained curiosity is a primary driver of learning and life-direction.
- Assemble and use information resources and media to access the wider world and expand perspective.
- When opportunity appears, act decisively rather than hesitating.

## Decision heuristics

- When you feel constrained by local expectations, actively pursue concrete opportunities that expose you to new environments.
- Create and protect a dedicated private space to think, accumulate knowledge, and develop personal perspective.
- Use diverse media—books, radio, music—to broaden curiosity and inform future choices.
- Invest saved resources in tools that support learning and identity (books, music players), rather than immediate consumption.

---

# P1_S11 – Early Wanderlust

# Early Wanderlust
**Story ID:** P1_S11
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1950s–1960s, Desire to explore and engage with the wider world versus rooted small-town expectations and potential social disapproval., Curiosity and travel stimulate learning that canno, Direct questioning and engagement accelerate under, Firsthand exposure to broader peers reveals true s, Treat small losses or mistakes as lessons and adju, Spontaneous

## Summary

A narrator recounts formative travel experiences from childhood through college that sparked curiosity, personal growth, and a lifelong passion for exploring beyond a small hometown.

## Full Text

Early Wanderlust
I think I may have been born a wanderer. T ravel has been a focus and a theme for most of my life. So much of my memory is centered on places—places and experiences outside my immediate environment.
My journeys began early in life. Some of my earliest memories are visits to my grandparents’ homes. The Cobb grandparents lived in Banner, a whopping eighteen miles from Calhoun City. The
Clements lived near Oxford, about forty miles from my home. Those distances seemed enormous when I was four or five years old. I have a vivid memory of one trip to Banner in the old red grocery truck that my Dad drove for the Justice Company. It was a cold and rainy winter day. The road was muddy and bumpy. Daddy, Mother, Sandy and I
were all stuffed like sardines into the cab of the truck. The engine was loud and noisy. The inside of the cab was shivering cold and smelled like oil. I sat in the middle, with my legs straddling the long gearshift protruding from the floor, watching the windshield wipers flop back and forth and feeling Daddy’s tension as he navigated the slippery hills and curves. Mother nagged on and on about how foolish we were to be out visiting on a day like this. Especially just to spend time with her in-laws! Despite the discomforts of the day, however, I
was experiencing a thrill of wanderlust. I was going somewhere. And
I was anxious and excited. There were new things to learn about:
muddy roads, noisy trucks, how gear shi fts and windshield wipers work, my grandmother’s fried apple pies, an old hand-cranked
Victrola that played scratchy music, and on and on. The tension in the air was palpable, too. So many new and different things to learn,
all crammed into a single day on the road to Banner. In a sense, this was the beginning of a lifelong quest for experiencing the mysteries of life beyond my immediate surroundings.
In 1951, Daddy bought our first automobile—a brand new two-door maroon Plymouth. One of our first trips in this car was to visit my Uncle Bill and Aunt Katherine in Oak Ridge, T ennessee. It was a long trip, interrupted by frequent restroom stops, gas fill-ups and lunch on a roadside picnic table. Daddy grumbled about the price of gas and putting almost one thousand miles on a brand new car. I
remember getting bored and tired from being cramped in the back seat for hours with nothing to do. But I also remember that there was mystery and intrigue in the air, which piqued my interest. Uncle
Bill worked for the Atomic Energy Commission, and his work was reportedly top-secret stu ff. There was intense speculation about whether he was building atomic bombs for the military. I wanted to know and asked him a lot of questions. But he wasn’t talking.
Uncle Bill also had a huge ham radio setup in his basement. This was ostensibly his hobby. All the sophisticated equipment with lights,
buttons, dials and indecipherable abbreviations mesmerized me. He would sit and talk to people who claimed to be located all over the world. Amazing! I remember wondering, even at age ten or eleven, if he might be a secret spy! He was amused at my curiosity, and I think urged me along by spinning yarns and watching my jaw drop. Uncle
Bill took us to an atomic energy museum in Oak Ridge and told more hair-raising stories. I have no idea if any of them were true or if he just delighted in the fantasy of it all. And then there was the long trip home—hot, sweaty, but not so boring this time because of the vivid images of atomic bombs and secret ham radio communications.
These memories of this trip have been lodged in my brain ever since.
Just the mention of the words “Oak Ridge” causes my nostalgia to spring to life, even to this day. This was just one early life experience fueled by travel—learning that never could have happened at home.
My first signi ficant travel on my own was a journey to Valley
Forge, Pennsylvania in 1957 to a ttend the National Boy Scout
Jamboree. A small group of teenage boys from T roop 40 in Calhoun
City piled into a bus with scouts from nearby towns and a couple of chaperones. The bus rumbled along two-lane roads for the thousand-mile trek to Philadelphia. Then, upon arrival at Valley Forge, we proceeded to our assigned campsites, pitched our tents and set up camp. We had all the traditional camping gear, along with enough food and supplies for a week’s stay. The jamboree was a beehive of activity for days —sporting activities, educational programs, skills contests, camp fires, singing, patriotic programs, etc. I remember carrying with me a little hard-earned money and spending a lot of it buying various souvenirs. It is one of my earliest recollections of useless trinket collection. Many of my newly acquired souvenirs were immediately traded at some of the huge trading bazaars that were set up. I distinctly recall the dismal feeling of a sense of loss. I
felt a strong suspicion that perhaps I had been outsmarted by some urbane traders who might have been much shrewder than I was.
This was an early lesson that served me well later in life. I resolved then and there to be more careful with my hard-earned money. I also remember meeting scouts from all over the U.S. and keeping a log of their names and addresses. I later communicated with many of them as pen pals from time to time.
From Valley Forge, our bus made a side trip to New Y ork City,
where we had the privilege of seeing the Brooklyn Dodgers and
Chicago Cubs play in Ebbets Field and a ttending a Billy Graham crusade service in Yankee Stadium. Both were rather historic events.
At this Dodgers game, Duke Snyder hit his three-hundredth career home run. Also, it was one of the last games in historic Ebbets Field,
which was closed a few weeks later when the Dodgers moved to Los
Angeles. There were over 100,000 people at the Billy Graham service,
which was later reported to be the largest crowd ever gathered for a religious event. The good fortune of me being at these two unique occasions was nothing short of amazing. Like the Oak Ridge trip,
the long journey home was not so bad because of the panoply of remarkable experiences that kept tumbling over in my mind. Thirty-five years later, when I was living in Philadelphia, I went out to Valley
Forge National Park one Sunday afternoon and found the exact spot where we had pitched our tent. I sat down on the grass and relived in my mind every moment of the experiences that I could recall.
A couple of years later, I found myself in New Y ork City again,
marching down Fi fth Avenue with the Lion’s All-State Band.
Sidestepping the story about how this opportunity came about, I
have fond memories of this experience as well. It was on this trip that
I saw my first Broadway musical —My Fair Lady with the original cast, including Rex Harrison and Julie Andrews. This was truly a spellbinding experience. I have listened to the sound track of that performance hundreds of times over the years. Each time I hear the overture theme and the score for “I Could Have Danced All Night” ,
those same goosebumps pop up on my arms —the same ones that appeared on a mesmerized seventeen-year-old si tting in orchestra seats in the Broadway Theater. How could one ever forget such an experience?
And then there were a number of travel experiences closer to home. These were mostly related to Boy Scout camping activities and band events. I served as a counselor one summer at Camp Palila in Louisville for a couple of weeks. Our band marched in parades throughout the state, usually around Christmas time, and I attended a band clinic in Jackson and one on the Gulf Coast. My recollection of these experiences is peppered with emotional memories related to my observations about the skills of musicians from other places.
The competition curve was noticeably steeper, I quickly noticed. I
was accustomed, for example, to being one of the best musicians in our high school band, but when I attended the state band clinics,
I was slapped with the reality of my own mediocrity. These were humiliating realizations at times, but valuable lessons nevertheless.
They were lessons I would never have learned if I had stayed at home.
It was during my senior year in high school that I reached a decision to a ttend the University of Southern Mississippi, which was a journey itself in a way. I wanted to engage with a world beyond my small-town con finement. Largely for this reason, I wandered two hundred miles away for a four-year odyssey in Ha ttiesburg,
Mississippi. My parents were clearly disappointed by my exile, as were many others in the local community. Some looked upon me as a disloyal deserter. But it was the right decision for me. No doubt about it.
Inching into my college days, my glimpses of the world outside began to expand. I became active in the Student Government
Association, and through that medium I had the opportunity to participate in activities of the Southern Universities Student
Government Association (SUSGA), an organization of student leaders throughout the South. I attended conventions at Clemson and Florida State University and visited other universities. These were nothing short of eye-popping experiences for me in so many respects. I met fascinating student leaders and saw impressive college venues and programs that would have been foreign to me if I had remained tucked away in my dorm playing cards with the locals. It was at one of these SUSGA conventions, for example, that I first met and became friends with T rent Lo tt, who later became a powerful
United States senator. A few years ago, T rent and I reminisced about the value of these student government experiences. The student leadership days and the associated travel le ft enormous lifetime impressions on me. They became an integral part of my personal fabric as I matured over the years.
On a different plane, I also remember walking across the USM
campus one morning on a Saturday in February, with my friend
John Simpson, when he said, “Hey, why don’t we go down to Mardi
Gras this weekend?” At the time, Mardi Gras was nothing more than two words to me; I knew absolutely nothing about it other than the fact that it was some kind of celebration in New Orleans.
Without breaking stride, John and I continued walking, straight to the highway, stuck out our thumbs and hitchhiked to New Orleans.
Just like that. We probably had less than $5 in our pockets between us. A fter three or four rides, we somehow arrived in the French
Quarter mid-afternoon. We immediately joined in all the festivities and revelry, wandering from bar to bar far into the night. Most of the details of that evening are a vague fog today, but I do have vivid memories of seeing and doing things I had never even thought about,
much less experienced. Another notch on my wanderlust gun!
During my senior year at USM, I began campus interviews with a number of firms. These all went well, and I was invited to visit businesses in Memphis, New Orleans, Dallas and Houston.
Naturally, I accepted all these invitations and embarked on expense-paid treks to these places. I marveled at the tall buildings, nice hotels and plush offices. The splendor of these business environments was magnetic and made my career decision very difficult. But the overall attraction of the travel experiences, frankly, was just as exciting as the job opportunities. Once again I was seeing new worlds previously foreign to me!
So, by college graduation I had developed a track record of wandering around and thoroughly enjoying it. My life experiences had been immeasurably enriched by my limited travels —whether it was Banner, Oak Ridge, New Orleans or New Y ork—and I knew it. This set the stage for a lifetime of worldwide adventure and an understanding of the emotions embodied in Dr. Seuss’s classic, Oh,
The Places I Have Been.

## Principles

- Curiosity and travel stimulate learning that cannot occur within a narrow local environment.
- Direct questioning and engagement accelerate understanding of unfamiliar subjects.
- Firsthand exposure to broader peers reveals true skill levels and drives improvement.
- Treat small losses or mistakes as lessons and adjust future behavior accordingly.
- Spontaneous, low-resource ventures can create lasting, formative memories.

## Decision heuristics

- When seeking personal growth, deliberately leave familiar surroundings to engage broader environments and communities.
- When presented with a rare opportunity, prioritize firsthand experience even if it requires modest resources or spontaneity.
- Convert minor setbacks into learning rules: after a loss, adopt a concrete corrective behavior.

---

# P1_S12 – By the Light of the Silvery Moon

# By the Light of the Silvery Moon
**Story ID:** P1_S12
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1940s–1960s, Reconciling a promising early-career opportunity that required separation with the emerging realization that the narrator had found a life partner and deciding to commit., Shared core values are foundational for durable pa, Honesty, integrity, Consistent communication sustains relationships ac, Deliberate saving and preparation enable committed, Mutual support and aligned fundamental values redu

## Summary

A first-person account of two neighbors who moved from casual acquaintances in high school to lifelong partners, built on shared values, sustained communication during separations, and a deliberate engagement.

## Full Text

By the Light of the Silvery Moon
In the 1940s and 1950s on South Madison Street in Calhoun
City, Mississippi, there resided two kindred spirits. At the time,
neither knew anything about kindred souls, much less suspected that one might be so close by. Keith Cobb and Dot Hill lived less than two blocks apart on the same street and were only a year apart in age. Y et, in so many respects, they occupied different worlds.
My life revolved around school organizations, academics,
the school band, working at various jobs and generally hanging out with friends. Dot’s life was centered on family, music, church,
cheerleading and generally enjoying the fun of an active social life. I
was the more serious one, sharply focused on personal achievement in whatever I did and looking toward a venturesome future. Dot was the consummate free spirit, life of the party, focused on enjoying every step she took and living in the moment.
We knew each other, of course, and often found ourselves in the same venues, usually through the church or school affairs. But in high school neither ever gave the other a serious glance. We were friends for sure, but never seemed attracted to one another. She thought I was a nerd and I thought she was a little zany—both gross exaggerations,
but somewhat descriptive in a vague sense. Occasionally, when one of us needed a date for an event, we would call upon the other for an escort. T otally innocent—merely a neighborhood convenience.
Since we both were interested in music, we were sometimes invited to perform together at functions. The earliest recording of one of these events is a prom program, in which we are listed as singing “By the Light of the Silvery Moon” together. We have a copy of that program in our memorabilia, but neither of us can remember anything about the event or even know the date. My best guess is that we were fourteen or fifteen years old.
We both emerged as leaders in our high school, but in di fferent spheres of school activities. I was the editor of the school newspaper,
for instance. She was the head of the school cheerleaders. I was a year older and a grade ahead, so we never had any classes together.
Only occasionally did we find ourselves in the same activities, such as the glee club or a school play.
Upon graduation, we chugged o ff to di fferent colleges. I exiled myself to the University of Southern Mississippi, where I majored in accounting. Dot enrolled at Mississippi College, where she majored in music.
Then in the summer of 1961, we began the process of binding our pathways together. Here’s how it happened.
I was home from college for the summer and working with
Harry Robinson in the M.M. Winkler & Associates accounting firm. Outside of work, there was little activity in our small town,
and I often wandered the streets in the evenings. Dot was home that summer, along with two of her sisters, and their house, only a couple of blocks from mine, became an entertaining place to hang out in the late afternoons and evenings. They had a small Hammond organ in
First record of Keith and Dot together their home and I enjoyed trying to learn to play it. But I’m not really sure that the organ was anything more than a convenient excuse to enjoy the company of all these girls! In any event, I began spending more and more time there. Before long, Dot and I began to strike out and look for other things to do outside her house. There were few things in town to keep us occupied individually and gradually we began to lean on each other for companionship and entertainment.
Somewhere along the way over this summer, we began to realize a few things that we had in common —commonalities that had escaped us over the years. For example, we both loved music and we both enjoyed a wide spectrum of friends. More importantly,
we discovered a mutual value system, which is so o ften missing in relationships. We both valued honesty, integrity and a work ethic.
We were socially conscious and put family and friendships on a high pedestal. We discovered that we respected these traits in each other. From that point of discovery, we began to develop a mutual dependency, even in the short span of time in a summer. I found her bright personality magnetic, captivating, and at the same time, I was beguiled by her solid values and crystalline honesty. She was totally real—to the bone. And I loved that immensely. I think she found my integrity, solid grounding and serious commitment a ttractive. I
think maybe she began to say to herself, “We would be good for each other. ” I certainly came to believe that she would be good for me!
She made me feel happy! So, looking back, I believe our relationship was planted on a bedrock of mutual dependency.
I am not sure of the exact time when our relationship began its gradual re-characterization as “love” , but it wasn’t long after the summer ended and we had headed back to our respective colleges. We had busy schedules of studies and activities, and extensive networks of friends at college. However, in the midst of all this, we missed each other’s company. Clearly, it wasn’t because we were lonely. We began communicating, by le tters mostly. Long-distance phone calls were expensive at the time, but we managed an occasional call. Gasoline was also expensive, but I scrounged and made the hundred-mile trips to Clinton from time to time. By the end of that school year, we were both clearly hooked.
In the summer of 1962, I was o ffered an attractive summer job on the internal audit sta ff of Humble Oil and Re fining Co. in New
Orleans. The opportunity was a major steppingstone in launching my career, so I accepted it and moved to New Orleans for the summer, while Dot attended summer school at Mississippi State
University. We were forced to be apart for most of the summer, but were in constant communication. Dot came to New Orleans one weekend to see me. Looking back, I think that visit might have been the lynchpin that locked us together. A few weeks later, both of us went home to Calhoun City for a short time before returning to college. I have a vivid recollection of a serious conversation, si tting in my car in Dot’s driveway and discussing all the rami fications we could think of related to a life together. I have no recollection if that conversation took place By the Light of the Silvery Moon. But it was then and there that I came to realize I had found a soul mate and was prepared to spend the rest of my life with her. I recall a deep philosophical conversation I once was having with my good friend
Charlie Brumfield, and he posed the question, “What in this world can we be really sure of?” My immediate answer was, “I’m sure about
Dot. There’s not a doubt in my mind. ”
I saved most of the money earned that summer and began shopping for an engagement ring. On September 15, 1962, we made it official. I formally asked for her hand in marriage and gave her the ring. Thankfully, she said “yes” and accepted it. And thus the long,
lifetime journey began.
Our wedding was a marvelous occasion. We were married in the
First Baptist Church, surrounded by a sanctuary full of friends and family. The photographs of this event are among my most cherished memorabilia.
I love to think back about the life we have built together,
remembering the ups and downs we’ve weathered and feeling proud of the family we’ve created. The mutual dependency and shared values formula has been one that has worked well for us. We have been strong supporters for each other and we have almost never disagreed on the fundamental values that guide our lives.
Every day, I feel so fortunate to have spent my life with Dot.
She is my best and most trusted friend and has filled my days with happiness. She has the most consistent beautiful spirit of anyone
I have ever met. I simply adore this woman. The things that mean the most in my life are all the things I have shared with her —our home, our family and our love. I am so grateful for our more than fifty wonderful years together.
The Big Day—June 15, 1963
The most wonderful wife!

## Principles

- Shared core values are foundational for durable partnerships.
- Honesty, integrity, and work ethic are central selection criteria for a life partner.
- Consistent communication sustains relationships across distance.
- Deliberate saving and preparation enable committed steps despite financial constraints.
- Mutual support and aligned fundamental values reduce conflict and strengthen long-term bonds.

## Decision heuristics

- When a potential partner shares core values, have deep conversations and prioritize time together to test compatibility before deciding.
- If career opportunities require temporary separation, maintain regular written and occasional phone communication and plan visits to preserve connection.
- When preparing for an engagement amid limited funds, save earnings specifically to cover the symbolic and practical costs of commitment.
