# P1_S13 – The Power of Momentum

# The Power of Momentum
**Story ID:** P1_S13
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1959–1963 (college years), education / accounting (student to professional transition), How to sustain and leverage early academic success (momentum) while navigating institutional unfairness, financial constraints, mentorship opportunities, and career decisions., Early wins create sustained momentum that fuels fu, Investigate and challenge actions that appear unfa, Leverage mentor relationships; mentors can open op, Listening and understanding in the moment can subs, Accept practical compensations that address immedi

## Summary

A student from a small town leverages early academic advantages to build momentum in college, helps teammates, confronts perceived unfairness, and makes pragmatic career choices aided by mentors.

## Full Text

The Power of Momentum
As with many people, college was a pivotal juncture in my life.
When I packed my bags and le ft Calhoun City behind, in the fall of 1959, to enter the University of Southern Mississippi (named
Mississippi Southern College at the time), the two-hundred-mile journey was a huge distance in my mind and in the perspective of my parents and others. I was still frail and weak from the lung surgery
I had undergone that summer, but my spirits were high and I was excited to be embarking on a new journey in my life.
As I re flect on this transformational time and the ensuing four years, one important concept and life lesson comes to mind.
It is the importance of what I call “momentum” . As a result of my fortunate high school education experiences, I was extremely well prepared for college, particularly academically. As a matter of fact, I
was surprised at how ill prepared some of my fellow students were.
Therefore, I enjoyed a built-in competitive advantage from the get-go. Thankfully, I was somehow alert enough to use this edge to build a pattern of success in my collegiate experience. Then momentum set in and, as the expression goes, success bred success. My early academic performance acted as a magnet and set the stage for success in other endeavors. I believe that positive momentum pushed me forward and allowed me to maximize the academic opportunities before me.
The initial core curriculum in those days was virtually the same for everyone —English, mathematics, history, sciences, etc.
I launched my first school year with these courses, together with
Principles of Accounting, which normally is a second year course—
eighteen credit hours each term. I was astonished at how easy some of these courses were for me. Clearly, I was expecting something more arduous. Freshman English and algebra seemed like nothing more than a repetition of my high school courses. Even the introductory accounting courses seemed eerily reminiscent of Frances Hardin’s bookkeeping course in high school. My performance in these courses was excellent and I finished the first year with a near straight
A average. Somewhere in my memorabilia, I have a slip of paper that recognizes my first-year grade point average as ranking number one out of 970 students. And I was able to do this with an absolute minimum of studying outside the classroom. A skinny country boy from the red clay hill country. Imagine!
Perhaps a few anecdotes might illustrate how my academic career unfolded and how momentum played a pivotal role. The stories also might illustrate how academic performance played a role in other valuable lessons and friendships along the way.
I spent my first year in Forest Hall, a freshman dormitory where rooms and roommates were pre-assigned. My roommate was a student named Vince Colandrea, a rough and tumble guy from New
Jersey. Vince was the stereotypical Italian street guy. He could have played a leading role in the Broadway musical Grease. He was one of several New Jersey and Pennsylvania fellows who had migrated down to Mississippi for some reason. Lower admission standards for athletes probably played a role in the migration. Most were, in fact,
athletes, and a few had made the football team. Through Vince, I met and became friendly with all these fellows, although I actually had little in common with them. It was quickly apparent they had not enjoyed the excellent academic background that I had. It was hard to believe they ever graduated from high school. Their academic skills were atrocious. Most couldn’t even distinguish a noun from a verb.
I’m serious! They all struggled desperately to survive academically and were constantly looking to me for help.
The football players were particularly concerned because their athletic scholarships were at risk. One evening around mid-year,
there was a knock on my dormitory door. It was Coach Thad Vann,
the head football coach. He introduced himself, took a seat on the edge of my bunk bed and said he had heard about me. He told me about his concern for the grades of the football team and asked if I
would be willing to tutor these New Jersey and Pennsylvania football players. Before I could ask what was in it for me, he gave me a lecture about the importance of athletics to the University and collegiate team spirit. He then o ffered me a seat at the training table in the cafeteria. Now, to most people that may not sound like much, but to me it was a really big deal. I accepted and for the rest of that year,
I was no longer subjected to the mystery meat and sloppy macaroni dished out at the cafeteria. I ate every meal in the private athletic dining room and feasted on thick steaks, fresh vegetables and terrific desserts. In my debilitated physical state (I’d had the two surgeries the summer before), that was the perfect compensation for me. So, I
worked with these guys as they struggled through their courses and,
fortunately, no one failed a single course. A miracle, actually! Coach
Vann was pleased and appreciative of my e fforts. He o ffered me a few other perks along the way and invited me to travel with the team the next year for a couple of away games, but I never managed to make it to one.
At the end of my freshman year, I was presented with the Phi Eta
Sigma Outstanding Freshman Award in recognition of my academic performance. Dr. J.R. Switzer, Dean of Student A ffairs, presented a trophy to me in a ceremony and invited me to visit with him in his office the next day. I knew his daughter Carolyn, who was also a freshman. During this visit, he talked about his dreams for her success in life and hoped she would buckle down and get more serious about her education. He also announced that he had actually called my parents the evening before to express his congratulations.
I was impressed that he had done this. I found this man warm and comfortable to be with. He had a fatherly countenance, and I liked him a lot.
Each year at USM, the Winn-Dixie grocery chain awarded three
$500 scholarships to second-year students. T o put that amount of money in perspective, my total tuition, room and board for my freshman year in 1959–1960 was $618. I checked out the criteria for the scholarships, which focused on academic performance and financial need, and filed an application over the summer. Given the
Phi Eta Sigma award, I felt confident that I would receive one of the three scholarships. When registration time came at the outset of my sophomore year, I inquired and was told that I was not a recipient.
Very surprised and curious, I asked who the recipients were. They demurred. A fter some wrangling with the office workers and insistence by me, the Registrar finally came over and coughed up the names. (Incidentally, the Registrar was Aubrey Lucas, who years later became the University president.) Lo and behold, the three recipients were two sons and a daughter of college administrative officials, including Carolyn Switzer of all people! I stood there,
with my mouth agape, on the college gymnasium floor, where the registration process was taking place. On an impulse, I le ft and walked straight over to Dr. Switzer’s office a few blocks away, and barged in. I told him what I had discovered and how dumbfounded
I was. I pointed out how my grades were be tter than all three of these students and my economic station in life was surely inferior to his and probably the others. He was quiet and contemplative for a while. I began to wonder if I might have overstepped. After a long,
pregnant pause, he said to me, “Son, I do understand your concern,
but I’m not going to try to explain this to you. Y ou should just settle down and go on back down to the gym and finish your registration. ”
I did, and when I arrived at the tuition payment station, the cashier said, “Keith, you don’t owe anything. Everything has been taken care of. ” That same message was delivered at the next two quarterly registration processes as well.
This episode was an extraordinary learning experience for me.
First, I was reminded in vivid color that life is not always fair. I learned that it pays to investigate actions and circumstances that appear questionable—perhaps a precursor to my later auditing days. I also learned that when one has the facts on his side, he should not allow himself to be intimidated by anyone. Lastly, I came to realize that
Dr. Switzer was fundamentally a good man who had made a slight misstep in judgment along the way. This event sealed my friendship with him for the next three years and cemented his respect for me as a person. I consulted with him regularly about college policies, and he often looked to me for advice on student affairs. Many years later,
when I was back at U.S.M. for an alumni function and Dr. Switzer was getting up in years, he told the large group of people assembled for the event that I was the smartest student he had ever met. Now,
I understand that this comment was gratuitous —and certainly not true—but it demonstrated a lasting bond that was first established in his office when I was nineteen years old. I consider Dr. Switzer one of my valuable mentors. And this experience served to fuel further momentum in my college career.
My grades continued to be excellent throughout my college days, even when I did not put forth much effort. My solid grounding continued to propel me forward —and somewhere along the line, I
managed to learn the art of listening and paying a ttention in class.
Many students skipped classes regularly. I rarely ever missed a class in my first three years. I always paid close a ttention to the lectures and, somehow, I usually managed to make sense of the material being presented.
I remember one interesting occasion when I was taking English literature with a professor named Mrs. Webb. She assigned the
Russian novel Crime and Punishment by Fyodor Dostoyevsky at the beginning of the term and said we would begin discussion at an assigned date. I procrastinated the reading and, in fact, never bothered to buy the book. Then, suddenly, the witching hour arrived and stared me in the face. Mrs. Webb reminded the class that we should have already read the entire book and announced that class discussion would begin the next day, following a quiz to evaluate our independent comprehension. I froze and then trembled. Later that day, I met with my friend Charlie McDaniels, who was also in the class and had read the book in detail. Charlie was a squirrelly little guy,
but friendly and conscientious to a fault. I said, “Charlie, you have to tell me something about this book. ” So, we sat down on a sofa in the
Student Union building and Charlie proceeded to lay out for me the story of a Russian guy named Raskolnikov who killed a pawnbroker.
He told me about the relationship of Raskolnikov with his mother,
sister and brother, how he likened himself to Napoleon and how a detective dogged him until he was caught and sentenced to Siberia.
A pretty interesting story, I thought at the time. Charlie delivered more detail than I had bargained for. The entire conversation lasted no more than a half hour. The next day we arrived in class and took the quiz, which had twenty questions. The following day Mrs.
Webb pranced into the classroom with a super-serious countenance and announced that the class had not performed well on the quiz.
“However,” she said. “I’m pleased to say that Mr. Cobb scored 100!
Mr. Cobb, I’m looking forward to hearing your perspective on this wonderful novel. ” I turned around and looked back at Charlie, who was shaking his head in confusion. He had scored 80, which was the second best grade in the class. I’m really still not sure how that happened. But I think it had something to do with listening and understanding and mentally assembling and organizing facts. And it probably had something to do with test-taking in general, and perhaps a bit of luck. In any event, I was summarily embarrassed and spent that entire weekend reading Crime and Punishment.
A similar thing occurred a year or so later, when I was taking
Money and Banking with Dr. Joseph Green, dean of the School of
Commerce and Business Administration. My roommate, Charlie
Brumfield, was taking the class with me. I still remember exactly where I was seated—on the second row next to the window. I also remember where Charlie sat, the row behind me two seats to the left.
The material about the Federal Reserve System and monetary policy fascinated me and I found myself absorbed in Dr. Green’s fascinating lectures. He was an acknowledged authority on the subject. By the end of the course, he sensed my keen interest and seemed to be talking directly to me, to the exclusion of the rest of the class.
Charlie noticed this attention and was confounded by it. He was my roommate and of course knew how little effort I applied outside the classroom. Charlie was an economics major and a serious student,
later earning a PhD in economics. Late each evening in our room he would study and slave over Money and Banking—hours and hours.
He chided me because I never purchased the book and predicted
I was going to fail the course because of my lack of e ffort. He was wrong. I made an A and he made a B. Now, I can’t explain that situation any more that I can rationalize the Crime and Punishment story, except for the force of the momentum I had developed by that time.
Dr. Green was a tall and lanky guy from Big Stone Gap, Virginia.
He smoked a pipe and looked the part of a business school dean. And he was one of my fans. He knew me well and was familiar with my background, both academically and through my college leadership roles. Near the end of my senior year, he called me to his office for a serious discussion about post-graduate work. He told me he had taken the liberty of arranging for a fully paid scholarship for me at the University of Virginia, his alma mater, where I could work on a three-year doctoral program in economics. I was totally flattered and taken aback. However, I was not particularly interested in a career in economics. By that time I had several attractive offers from all the Big Eight accounting firms, two banks and IBM. Moreover,
I was about to get married, and I had no money! So, I thanked him profusely but declined the opportunity. He understood my rationale, but was noticeably disappointed. He stayed in touch with me throughout the rest of his tenure at Southern Mississippi while
I was working at KPMG. He was always generous with his time, as well as his compliments. Dr. Green was a true mentor and friend. He is a platinum member of my personal Hall of Fame.
My days at U.S.M. were filled with stories similar to these. There were many great and memorable experiences in a wide spectrum of classes over the four years. I have no illusions that I won the DNA
lottery or was natively brighter than anyone else. But neither can I
assert that I was just a hard worker. I wasn’t. At least academically.
As discussed previously, my excellent high school grounding set the stage and the momentum of that initial edge catapulted me forward.
I think the fact that I was an interested student —as contrasted to someone just passing through —was a huge contributor to my success. And, of course, the Harry Lorayne memory tricks I had learned when I was sick didn’t hurt things at all. My grades slipped a bit in my senior year, when I was totally absorbed in Student
Government affairs, job interviews and shu ttling back and forth to
Mississippi College to see Dot. But I managed to end my college career in 1963 with a straight A, 4.00 academic average in accounting.
My overall average was 3.50, qualifying me to graduate with honors.
I am a great believer in the value of momentum. My three sons can probably recite verbatim my “get ahead early” speech, which they heard countless times during their school years. It is a concept that served me well, both in college and throughout my business career.

## Principles

- Early wins create sustained momentum that fuels future success.
- Investigate and challenge actions that appear unfair rather than accepting them passively.
- Leverage mentor relationships; mentors can open opportunities and provide lasting support.
- Listening and understanding in the moment can substitute for exhaustive outside study.
- Accept practical compensations that address immediate needs and align incentives.

## Decision heuristics

- Leverage early accomplishments to create momentum and open further opportunities.
- When you suspect unfair treatment, gather facts and raise the issue directly with the responsible party.
- Accept reasonable non-monetary perks when they meet real needs and align with requested contributions.
- When underprepared, seek quick, focused briefings from knowledgeable peers and rely on attentive listening to synthesize understanding.
- Choose pragmatic career options when immediate financial obligations and multiple attractive practical offers exist.

---

# P1_S14 – T ogetherness on Saturday

# Togetherness on Saturday
**Story ID:** P1_S14
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1959, Higher education / University, Whether to drop a required, paid-for course assigned to a feared professor or remain and find a way to succeed under an opaque, uncommunicative grading system., Treat difficult situations as laboratories for pro, Analyze patterns in outcomes and iteratively test, Trust your intuition and sharpen the ability to th

## Summary

A 1959 freshman is assigned to a notoriously strict, silent English professor and must decide whether to drop the required course or stay and figure out how to earn a passing grade; through pattern analysis and experimentation the student improves from an F to an A.

## Full Text

T ogetherness on Saturday
Freshmen at the University of Southern Mississippi in 1959
were assigned English classes by lottery. Each class comprised about thirty students. There was no option to transfer out of one’s assigned class; you either hung in or dropped the course. The classes assigned to me in the first two terms had excellent professors; however, my luck changed dramatically in the third quarter. I drew Dr. Wilbur
Stout, who had a long-standing reputation for being the toughest professor on campus. He had not awarded an A in five years. The failure rate of students in his class was in the stratosphere. Moreover,
he was a really weird duck. He had a disheveled appearance, with uncombed silver hair and a sloppy seersucker suit that hung on him like a limp sack. He smoked a long crooked briar pipe and shu ffled when he walked. His eyes wandered blandly around a room and he rarely spoke a word.
So, there I was. Stuck. I could take my chances with this mysterious man or, alternatively, I could drop a course I had already paid for and gamble on a better professor the next year. (The English composition course was required.) On the second day of class,
only twelve people showed up. Eighteen students had dropped the course—a predictable reaction based on Dr. Stout’s reputation. For some reason I can’t explain to this day, I bowed my back and refused to be intimidated by this character. I took a risk and remained in the class.
Here’s how the class was conducted. There were no lectures and no instructions—just a grinding daily drill of writing essays. On the first day, Dr. Stout walked into the classroom promptly on the hour,
wrote a topic on the blackboard and le ft the room. At the end of the fifty-minute period, he returned and collected the papers. The next day he strolled in and handed out the graded papers from the previous day. He then wrote a new topic on the board and left. And so it went. He never said a word; he just ambled in and out of the classroom at the beginning and end of the hour every day for three months. The graded essays were littered with arcane remarks, difficult to interpret or understand. Missing from this bizarre scenario was a forum to have his cryptic commentary explained.
The topics assigned were all offbeat and esoteric. Some subjects I
remember included: “Dividing by Two” , “Wondering or Wandering”
and “I Heard Their Footsteps” . Weird stuff!
On the first essay returned to me, Dr. Stout had bled all over the paper with red ink, including a big red F on the front page accompanied by indecipherable scribbling. I was crestfallen. It was the first F I had ever received on anything in my life! I combed the paper, trying to figure out where I screwed up. That continued for about a week, when at last a D appeared. And then a week or so later a C–. I spent hours poring over these papers trying to figure out a pattern to the grades —i.e., what was the key to this crazy puzzle?
I would place them all out on a table in the library, analyzing them in every minute detail. Finally, I zoned in on a few theories. Volume was obviously important—how much could I speed-write in forty-five minutes? And it was clear that grammar, punctuation, spelling,
etc. were unimportant to this professor. Also, obliqueness seemed to trump clarity every time. It gradually became clear that the more abstract my writing —i.e., not taking the topics so literally —the better the grade. So I began to seriously experiment with these theories and before long I was getting occasional As and Bs on a few of the daily papers.
My day of triumph came near the end of the term when Dr. Stout assigned the topic “T ogetherness on Saturday” . All twelve students stared blankly at the blackboard and said, “Huh?” Suddenly, I came up with an idea. I had worked the past summer at Lunceford’s Bargain
Store in Calhoun City. Next door to it, there was a makeshift café
that catered to African-American farm people. The café specialized in chitlins and hog jowls. On Saturday nights, dozens of customers would come in from the co tton fields and congregate at the café.
Due to the proximity of the café to my work, I came to know them fairly well. Their happy, fun-loving spirit, particularly in light of their destitute plight in life, intrigued me. So, in a sudden flash of creativity, I started to write about the uniqueness of these dirt-poor,
uneducated laborers. I described their “T ogetherness on Saturday”
and how their weekly camaraderie gave them a sense of respectable identity and perhaps contributed to their happiness.
The next day my paper was returned with a big A+ in large letters on the front and this remark scribbled in red at the end: “Mr.
Cobb, this is the best paper I have read in many, many years. ” By the end of the term, I had wri tten forty-four essays on the wildest array of subjects one can imagine. On final exam day, there was no exam and no writing. Dr. Stout simply shu ffled into the room and started handing out scraps of paper with the term grade. My scrap said A. I looked up at him and said, “ Thank you. ” He nodded and miraculously responded with, “Well deserved” Until then, I wasn’t sure he could speak!
I learned some valuable life lessons from this stressful experience: first, the class was a laboratory for problem solving. I
learned to critically analyze, parse out patterns and develop and test solutions. Second, the class sharpened my ability to think on my feet and trust my intuition. Third, it helped me see that sometimes it’s better not to take everything so literally. And, finally, it made me realize that togetherness on Saturday (or any day) with family and friends is a pretty good idea for life as well as for an essay topic.

## Principles

- Treat difficult situations as laboratories for problem solving.
- Analyze patterns in outcomes and iteratively test solutions.
- Trust your intuition and sharpen the ability to think on your feet.

## Decision heuristics

- When feedback is cryptic, collect multiple examples and analyze them side-by-side to infer patterns and scoring criteria.
- Adapt your output to what is rewarded: prioritize producing sufficient volume and the form of expression that the evaluator values.

---

# P1_S15 – Public Service 101

# Public Service 101
**Story ID:** P1_S15
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1962-1963, Higher education / student government, Balancing effective, constituency-focused public service with the temptations and ethical compromises of political power., Diligent preparation builds credibility and influe, Implement transparent processes to address institu, Stand against discrimination publicly, Power can lead to ethical compromise; self-awarene, Accept reprimand and learn when mistakes endanger

## Summary

A college student's rise to Student Government Association president, accomplishments (election reform, campus campaigns), ethical lapses (press interference, celebratory arson), and a concluding resolve to avoid politics.

## Full Text

Public Service 101
By the end of my freshman year in college, I had met a lot of people and forged solid friendships with students from various venues—my classes, my dormitory, the athletic teams, ROTC and social hangouts on the campus. The University of Southern Mississippi had quickly become my new home, a home without the constant tension caused by my mother’s desperate unhappiness and dissatisfaction with her life. Somewhere along the line that year, friends suggested that I run for the Student Senate and represent some of the non-fraternity/
sorority constituencies. The Greek community had dominated most of the student government organizations for years. Although these fraternity and sorority members were generally viewed as elitists by the rank and file student body, they seemed to know the ropes and managed to get elected to all the high-profile offices.
I mulled this over for a while. My involvement in leadership positions in high school had given me some experience. But I was cautious about wading into a coterie of the privileged and perhaps finding myself out of place. Finally, with some prodding by fellow students, I took the plunge. Some of my friends organized a phalanx of active supporters. And voilà! I was elected.
Thus began my college political career.
I served in the Senate throughout my sophomore year and thoroughly enjoyed every minute of it. I never missed a meeting and participated in the meetings actively. What I quickly noticed was that the majority of the members of the Senate didn’t take the position seriously. They spent most of their time relishing their status and notoriety, and did little real work. I was just the opposite.
I did my homework before every meeting, worked harder than most anyone and represented my constituencies aggressively. Needless to say, this diligence paid off. Before long, I was recognized as a serious leader among this group, a group comprised largely of socialites. So when springtime came, I signed up for the next election, this time as a junior senator. I was easily elected, despite some aggressive competition from fraternity row.
As a second-year senator, I began introducing and ramming through a spate of student legislation that I believed bene fitted all students, and becoming more involved with the executive branch of the Student Government Association (SGA). My good friend John
Simpson was the SGA president. He was a bright and talented guy,
well-liked by everyone. But he was not big on process or details. In those arenas, he defaulted to me and appointed me administrative assistant to the president. Before long, I was practically running the place, and by the end of that year it seemed only natural to me that I
should run for SGA president and succeed John.
I won the election handily and assumed the office in late spring of 1962. So there I was: a country boy from tiny Calhoun City, duly elected as the leader of a college population of roughly five thousand students. I felt like I had arrived! I remember calling my parents to tell them the good news. T o my dismay, neither of them could connect with what I was talking about and didn’t understand thesignificance of my election. Their attitude was sort of a “whatever… ” . They were
College politics no longer my entire world, however. I regained my exuberance and enthusiasm, and poured myself into this new responsibility.
The next year was one of the most pivotal in my life —mostly positive, but at times a bit negative.
The trappings of political office and the a ttendant power,
whether perceived or real, were intoxicating. I have o ften since likened the experience to being the mayor of a small municipality. I
was even paid a salary of $100 per month, funded by the university’s student activity fees. The SGA had an office in the Student Union building that was all fitted out with desks, typewriters, telephones,
file cabinets and such. As if in a flash, I found myself the center of a great deal of a ttention. I was consulted regularly by the Dean of
Student A ffairs and called upon occasionally by the university’s president. Other student leaders sought my counsel or in fluence. I
was asked to speak to various student groups, was interviewed and frequently asked for comments by the college newspaper and invited to greet visiting dignitaries. It was a constant whirlwind of activity,
requiring high energy and a sense of weighty personal responsibility.
I was even invited to fraternity parties. And all this commotion was taking place while I was trying to carry on a romance with Dot one hundred miles away and conducting interviews for my career following graduation. I was a very, very busy guy.
The Student Government Association consumed my senior year. I practically lived in the SGA offices. I normally arrived at the office immediately after an early breakfast and was usually kicked out by Bud Kirkpatrick when the building was closed and the doors locked at about eleven p.m. My dormitory room was nothing more than a place to sleep. I kept all my books and even changes of clothes in the SGA office. I went to classes sporadically, despite taking some advanced academic courses. By this time, most of the professors knew me well and were aware of my leadership role. My excellent academic record was an asset in this regard, and they cut me some slack insofar as a ttendance was concerned. I was still expected to deliver on the periodic tests and examinations, however, which I did.
Somehow I stumbled through this frenetic academic year, got good grades and managed to graduate with honors.
We accomplished a lot in that 1962–1963 school year. A few specific things stand out in my mind.
• Election misconduct had been a perennial problem, with substantial criticism of the fraternities and sororities; they shouldered much of the blame for perceived unfairness in the process. Accusations of fraud abounded. We streamlined the election process and instituted internal controls over it. We even initiated computerized balloting, years before such a thing emerged in large municipalities and long before computers were in general use anywhere. At the time, IBM was developing a punched card balloting process, and we asked if we could be used as a test market.
(No, I don’t remember anything about hanging chads. They showed up forty- five years later!) The computer balloting was a marvelous success, and we demonstrated the system at SUSGA (Southern
University Student Government Associations) conferences around the South. I don’t know if we were actually the first out of the gate with this technology, but we certainly were one of the first.
• Our institution was lobbying the state legislature for a name change from Mississippi Southern College to the University of
Southern Mississippi. This was a big deal in the minds of our faculty and students. It represented a degree of dignity and respect that we believed we deserved. I organized various student groups advocating the name change. I supported the move in appearances throughout the institution and actually met with various state legislators to champion our cause. In the end, the name change was approved. On the day the bill was passed in the Mississippi legislature, I led a small group that poured gasoline on the large MSC le tters carved in the hedge outside the Administration Building and set the hedge on fire.
I can remember the cheers of the large crowd assembled, as well as the screaming sirens of fire trucks in the background.
The phone in my office rang early that afternoon. It was Dr.
William David McCain, the no-nonsense university president,
demanding that I immediately report to his office. After a rather stiff reprimand and a lecture about safety and human lives, he finally said to me, “Keith, if this was anyone other than you, I would have you expelled. But with you, I’m going to resist that impulse. Now go on back and do some more of those other things—better things—that you do so well. ” I have that speech stamped indelibly, verbatim, in my head to this day.
• A later experience with Dr. McCain is even more vivid in my mind. It was in the fall of 1962, when James Meredith made his successful bid for admission to the University of Mississippi.
Shortly after Governor Ross Barne tt reluctantly consented to
Meredith’s admission, I received a call from Dr. McCain requesting that I accompany him to a meeting in Jackson, the state capitol, with the State Board of Institutions of Higher Learning to discuss racial matters. I of course agreed. He drove his car and I rode along on the ninety-mile journey to Jackson. We met and briefly engaged in small talk with some board members, whereupon Dr. McCain directed me to a bench in the massive hallways of the Capitol building. I sat there alone for a while. Sometime later, he appeared and said, “Keith, the governor wants to see us. ”
I remember walking into this seemingly enormous office with a large oak desk sitting squarely in the middle of the room. Governor
Barnett invited us to take a seat and, without any foreplay, looked me squarely in the eye with his penetrating eyes, overshadowed by thick bushy eyebrows, and said, “Son, what do you think we ought to do about all these niggers?” I was absolutely stunned and was speechless for a few moments. Quickly regaining my equilibrium and boldness, however, I said, “Sir, I personally think it’s wrong for us to discriminate against anyone. If the students are quali fied,
then I believe they should be a fforded the opportunity of higher education, regardless of their status in society, and that should be afforded to them wherever the state o ffers educational services. ”
He didn’t respond directly. He lowered his head briefly, indulged in some small talk and then excused us. I doubt that I had any impact whatsoever on his thinking, but I do know I caught his a ttention.
That was evident. It is one of the proudest moments of my life. I
walked out of that Capitol building on a cloud.
On the way home, Dr. McCain drove silently for a while and then he quietly said, “Keith, I was proud of you today. ”
There were many other exciting and memorable occasions —
walking onto the football field and crowning the homecoming queen, dressing up in a tuxedo for formal appearances, speaking to nine hundred incoming freshmen at their orientation assembly,
introducing Bob Hope to a packed gymnasium before a performance,
attending conferences and conventions on behalf of the university,
and on and on. But there were some stressful times as well. And there were times where my fundamental values were tested.
I was constantly bombarded with requests for intercession on behalf of students with issues. I must admit that I fixed a few parking tickets and leaned on the Student Court (which I appointed) for leniency in a few cases involving regulatory infractions. I didn’t like it, but I did it. And I invariably regre tted it soon afterward. My actions weigh on my conscience to this day.
My worst memory is an abject interference on my part with the freedom of the press. Before long in office, I found myself becoming extremely sensitive to any publicity that criticized the Student
Government Association or failed to promote SGA activities. I often disliked the way published accounts were wri tten and found myself arguing with the Student Printz editorial staff frequently. Somewhere along the way, I learned through an informant at the Registrar’s office that the Printz editor had failed to maintain the 2.5 academic grade average required for his position. This was an obscure fact, unknown to anyone in the journalism department or anyone else with influence over him. I confronted him with my knowledge of the situation and overtly threatened to expose him. The editorship was a prestigious and well-paid job, essentially providing a full scholarship to whoever held it. At his suggestion, we covertly entered into a Devil’s bargain wherein I was granted editorial veto over all newspaper coverage of the student government in exchange for keeping his academic grade average from becoming public knowledge. From that day forward, I
spent the late hours of every Thursday evening reviewing the galley proofs of the next day’s newspaper and sometimes ordering changes in the text, headlines, photographs or the placement of articles. I
wasn’t proud of these actions at the time, but I continued to do it nevertheless. And I am still ashamed of this, too.
It was through these lapses that I began to take a serious look at the political world around me. I mused about the negative influence of decisions that seemed justi fied at the time on my fundamental value system. Despite all the public recognition and genuine sense of accomplishment, I felt compromised. Whenever I thought about my position, I thought about my lapses in fundamental ethics because of it. Then and there I resolved to never again seek a political office.
I saw firsthand what it does to well-intentioned people. I wanted no part of it. I entered a more honorable profession and to this day I
have honored that abstinence pledge. No politics for me.
The experiences of my senior year in college are among the most important ones of my life. I find it difficult to describe the impact the student government presidency had on me as a person. I matured many years in a mere twelve months. Somehow, I managed to keep my eyes focused on my constituency, the average student. I had a deeply ingrained sense of gratitude for being elected to this office and I desperately wanted to be faithful to their trust. This unique opportunity was the equivalent of Public Service 101 and I was committed to graduating from that course with the same honors that I had accomplished academically. I believe I did that. I walked away from the experience with a renewed con fidence in my own abilities and a deeper appreciation for hard work and allegiance to the right things.

## Principles

- Diligent preparation builds credibility and influence.
- Implement transparent processes to address institutional unfairness.
- Stand against discrimination publicly, even when confronted by powerful figures.
- Power can lead to ethical compromise; self-awareness and corrective action matter.
- Accept reprimand and learn when mistakes endanger others or break rules.

## Decision heuristics

- Prepare thoroughly before meetings to build credibility and represent constituents effectively.
- When institutional procedures appear unfair, implement controls and transparent processes rather than relying on tradition.
- When facing discriminatory policies or rhetoric, voice principled opposition directly and clearly.
- Avoid secrecy and coercion to manage criticism; preserve press freedom instead of imposing censorship.
- If political office leads to recurring ethical compromises, consider exiting to protect core values.
