# P1_S16 – Launching a Career

# Launching a Career
**Story ID:** P1_S16
**Part:** One
**Tags:** final three months of 1962, accounting (and related corporate opportunities, e.g., IBM), Choosing among competing job offers and locations while balancing salary, career advancement potential, cultural fit, and personal/family considerations., Make a deliberate list of evaluation criteria befo, Consider both objective measures (reputation, Prioritize long-term fit and advancement over shor, Use in-person visits to assess organizations and p, Seek and weigh counsel from trusted advisors and f

## Summary

A graduating senior in late 1962 evaluates multiple job offers (Big Eight accounting firms, IBM, others), uses deliberate research and visits to choose Peat Marwick in Jackson, prioritizing cultural fit and long-term advancement over short-term pay.

## Full Text

Launching a Career
This vignette is included in the memoir collection because of its pivotal influence on me. Forks in the road define life’s journey, with the choices made having a dramatic in fluence on one’s entire life.
Such was the case as I stood at a set of crossroads in the final three months of 1962.
In the midst of all the important distractions of my senior year at USM, I found myself facing a lot of di fficult questions with not-so-obvious answers. What should I do when this is all over? Where should I launch my career? Where are the best opportunities? How should I evaluate career opportunities in relation to other personal goals? Where should we live? How can I best provide for a wife and family? And on and on.
In the fall of that year, I paid a visit to the placement office on campus and consulted with Dorothy Lenoir, the director there. She advised me to sign up for interviews with a variety of companies scheduled to visit the campus. So I did. I registered for interviews with all the Big Eight accounting firms and IBM, which seemed the most interesting. For good measure and comparison, I also signed up to see a few other companies, including State Farm Insurance,
Humble Oil and Refining and a couple of banks.
Initially, I was apprehensive about the interviews. These were big firms represented by mature, well-dressed and articulate executives who had the power over my future in their grasp. The interviews would be critically important. I couldn’t afford to stub my toe and I
was naturally nervous. The campus interviews were typically a half-hour, and the recruiters were well prepared —they generally knew all about me before I walked into the room. Importantly, most of them were skillful at pu tting students like me at ease. Much to my surprise, I realized that that the recruiters were in a selling mode and spent most of their allotted time trying to persuade me that the opportunities with their particular firm were my best option.
I had not expected that. I had underestimated the value of the credentials I had built up. All of the firms placed a high value on my academic record and campus leadership. So, while I had embarked on the project of meeting with various firms’ representatives thinking
I was looking for a job, I suddenly found myself in the driver’s seat.
I set aside my apprehensions and wended my way through the brochures and recruiters, trying to sort out my best overall career opportunity.
Conscientious about being careful and deliberate, I made a list of the criteria that I deemed important. The list was typical. It included assessments of the overall reputation of each firm, its size,
number of offices, any specific industry concentration or specialties,
and other demographics. I was especially interested in advancement opportunities—the pace of upward movement of the firms’ young professionals. On a more subjective side, I decided to look hard at their people and their clients.
My bias was toward the public accounting firms, inasmuch as
I had been majoring in accounting for four years. I had worked in two summer internships in that field—one for M.M. Winkler &
Associates, a CPA firm in Tupelo, and one on the internal audit staff of Humble Oil & Refining Company in New Orleans. All of the Big
Eight accounting firms were prestigious and offered excellent career advancement opportunities. They were the crème-de-la-crème of the professional world at that time. Their starting salaries ranged from
$500 to $550 per month. And they all seemed like a good fit for me.
Then IBM threw a monkey wrench into my thought process.
During the first few minutes of the campus interview, the recruiter made an on-the-spot offer for me to join them in their Jackson office at a starting salary of $600, with a guarantee of $10,000 annually in two years. He said that IBM had authorized only two o ffers for
USM students. The other o ffer was going to Linda Escobedo, a straight-A math major from Ha ttiesburg and a friend of mine. The difference in compensation between this o ffer and the low end of the public accounting firms was 20 percent. Moreover, IBM’s fringe benefits were far superior. Economics signi ficantly complicated my decision process! I remember observing that all the IBM recruiters
I’d seen over the years werevery tall men —invariably well over six feet. I wondered about this. Seriously, I worried about it! But then
I reasoned that Linda, who was about five feet two inches, certainly didn’t meet the perceived criteria that an IBM executive be a man who is at least six feet tall. In due course, I was able to put short-term monetary considerations and peculiar physical observations aside and concentrate on the professional aspects of my opportunities. I
made a decision to narrow my selection to the accounting firms.
All of the accounting firms invited me to visit their offices. In
November and December of 1962, I made several trips to New
Orleans, visiting Arthur Andersen, Haskins & Sells, Ernst & Ernst,
Peat Marwick Mitchell & Co., and Price Waterhouse. I visited
Arthur Y oung in Houston. I had already dismissed T ouche Ross and Lybrand as candidates and therefore didn’t visit them. Each of the companies I visited was impressive, to say the least. All the partners and managers I met were extremely professional. They were sincere, articulate, well dressed and urbane. They reeked of success and competence. As I sat in their plush offices, all of them in high-rise buildings, I could dreamily envision myself as one of them.
I was quite taken by them! Their client lists were impressive, too,
mostly recognizable large companies in a wide variety of industries.
Their policies and terms of employment were similar, with only a few unremarkable di fferences. I had a di fficult time distinguishing between them.
I was probably the most impressed with Arthur Y oung, which was somewhat smaller than the other firms and had fewer offices,
but seemed a bit more sophisticated and classy. They treated me royally during my visit to Houston, made an a ttractive offer during the visit and made a full court press for an early decision. They had a professional recruiter named Hal Reister (a non-accountant based in Dallas) who had interviewed me on the campus at USM and who stayed in constant contact. He was a personable, persuasive guy with piercing blue eyes and a beguiling smile. I thought his bright white teeth resembled Chiclets. I bonded with him quickly. My primary hesitancy, however, was that Houston seemed a million miles away from my roots. Despite my wanderlust and attraction to the corporate world, I felt a subtle desire to take a few baby steps as I embarked on my career. I also knew that Dot would have a natural reluctance to start our lives together so far away from home. I gradually began to narrow my focus to New Orleans.
During the Peat Marwick campus interview, I had met Harold
Dey and Bill Norton, managing partners of the Peat Marwick offices in New Orleans and Jackson. Harold Dey took the lead and followed up our interview with an o ffer for a position in New
Orleans. However, I sensed a subtle connection with Bill Norton. It was a sixth sense that seemed to be reciprocated. A fter the campus interview, he stayed in touch with me and sometime over the next month extended an invitation for me to “stop by” and visit his
Jackson office in mid-December, when I visited Dot at Mississippi
College. So I did, along with my friend Bob Hishon. Though the offices and the people in Jackson were not quite as impressive as the ones in New Orleans, I felt comfortable there. I wouldn’t say that I
was intimidated in the other office environments, but certainly the
Jackson office gave me a feeling of self-con fidence and that I would do well there. I can remember making a casual observation to myself about the people there and could see myself competing successfully with them for career advancement. Even though I mentioned that to no one, I felt embarrassed at the thought, frankly. But it was there nevertheless. In fact, I was bullish about my success probabilities in that particular environment. Also, by that time, I had read all the materials from all the firms and studied their business models and strategies. My gut instincts kept leading me back to Peat Marwick.
The culture seemed to match up with my fundamental values and work ethic. Many years later during a program at Harvard, I heard
Peat Marwick described as the classic “hunter” in a hunter/gatherer survival analogy. Although I didn’t know anything about such an analogy or organizational strategies at the time, I think I sensed the essence of that description and how it fit my basic persona and skill set. I saw Peat Marwick as aggressive, progressive and moving faster than the competition. It seemed less systematic and stilted than the other firms. More flexible.
Dot and I discussed all the opportunities extensively over the
Christmas holidays, and we decided to accept the Peat Marwick offer in Jackson, notwithstanding the fact that the salary was the lowest of the competing o ffers. I contacted all the firms upon returning to
USM in January and notified them of my decision. That put my two-month career search to bed, and I started making arrangements to transition from college to career in July.
The entire interview process was both educational and enjoyable.
I learned so much about the business world as I visited with each of the firms, and I met some terri fic people. Several of the partners in the other firms stayed in casual touch with me, in case I might have a change in heart. I also bumped into a few of them over the years.
Hal Reister from Arthur Y oung frequently touched base with me and wrote me a nice le tter when I made partner at Peat Marwick seven years later. The letter started out with, “I knew this was going to happen!” It was a warm feeling to know that I had credentials that were highly regarded and sought after.
I should mention more about the good advice and counsel I had along the way as I went through this process. Dorothy Lenoir, the placement director, was a crusty old lady with a brusque personality;
I think she might have had a military background. She could be condescending to students and actually vulgar at times. However,
she turned a warmer side to me, for some strange reason, and insisted on a report from me every step along the way. She advised me how to handle the a ttention I was ge tting, and without a doubt was extraordinarily helpful. Dr. Joe Green, Dean of the Business
School, sat with me on a few occasions and talked to me about my future. I can remember my rapt a ttention as he sat in his rocking swivel chair, smoking his pipe and prophesying about my future,
much as I imagined a grandfather might have done. I felt as if I was in the presence of Mark Twain or some other luminary. All of the accounting professors were keenly interested and helpful, as they tried to steer me to the public accounting firms—perhaps they saw me as an extension of their work and a symbol of their well-deserved reputations as excellent professors. And then there was Dr. McCain,
the university president, who beseeched me to stay in Mississippi,
whatever I chose to do!
I never once looked back with any regret over my decision. Peat
Marwick turned out to be the absolute perfect match for me. The decision was a complex product of diligent research, focused effort,
hands-on inspection, listening to good advice from others and my personal career and lifestyle ambitions. It was a carefully weighed decision. I ultimately spent thirty-two years with Peat Marwick.
Within seven years, I became the youngest partner in the history of the largest accounting firm in the world and then advanced over the years to become the third-ranking executive in the entire firm before retirement. So, clearly no regrets!

PART T WO

## Principles

- Make a deliberate list of evaluation criteria before choosing.
- Consider both objective measures (reputation, size, advancement) and subjective fit (people, clients, culture).
- Prioritize long-term fit and advancement over short-term salary.
- Use in-person visits to assess organizations and people.
- Seek and weigh counsel from trusted advisors and family when making major career decisions.

## Decision heuristics

- List and weigh specific objective and subjective criteria (reputation, size, advancement, people, clients) before deciding.
- Visit employers' offices and meet partners to judge cultural fit and working environment.
- Discuss major career choices with your partner and consider lifestyle and location impacts.
- Accept lower short-term compensation when long-term advancement and cultural fit indicate better career outcomes.
- Maintain relationships with recruiters and keep communication open to preserve options.

---

# P1_S17 – Chronology of a Career

# Chronology of a Career
**Story ID:** P1_S17
**Part:** One
**Tags:** career beginning 1963, spanning forty-plus years, business, Navigating a long career with evolving roles and an uncertain, fluid concept of retirement., Practice gratitude and acknowledge others' contrib, Cultivate and value mentorship and tutelage, Treat career as a lifelong learning process to hon, Remain adaptable about phases of involvement

## Summary

A reflective chronology of a forty-plus-year business career emphasizing lifelong learning, mentorship, serendipity, and a fluid view of retirement.

## Full Text

Chronology of a Career
How does one chronicle a professional career spanning forty-plus years? My story begins as I disrobed from a cap and gown following graduation ceremonies at the University of Southern
Mississippi in 1963. I immediately went to work and began launching a long and fruitful career.
Narratives like this normally end with retirement, but in my case
I’m not sure when that occurred—or whether it has occurred —or whether it will ever occur—or when it occurs in general. Retirement is a fuzzy concept for me. I’m still active in the business world, just in a different way and a smidgen less involved than in the past.
The following pages represent a collection of stories and events that occurred over various times and in various places. They are about my good fortunes and the many wonderful people who have shepherded me along the way. These stories are the backdrop to a lifelong learning process that has honed my skills and shaped my character. They also describe much of the serendipity that has accompanied me along the way.
Most of my stories are not dramatic. However, in the aggregate
I am hoping they paint a picture of how a lone country boy from
Mississippi was able to scramble around in a vast business world and find his way.
If there is a theme in this chronology, it’s one of gratitude.
As I re flect on my career, my focus is trans fixed by the numerous personalities that have in fluenced my life. I have had a lot of help along the way and people have been extremely generous in their tutelage and mentorship. For this I can only say, simply, that I am profoundly grateful.

## Principles

- Practice gratitude and acknowledge others' contributions.
- Cultivate and value mentorship and tutelage.
- Treat career as a lifelong learning process to hone skills and shape character.
- Remain adaptable about phases of involvement, including retirement.

## Decision heuristics

- When building a career, actively seek mentors and accept their tutelage.
- Treat professional development as ongoing: continually refine skills through experience and reflection.
- Stay open to serendipitous opportunities and adapt plans when unexpected events arise.
- Do not assume retirement is fixed; adjust involvement level to fit current interests and capacities.

---

# P1_S18 – Early Y ears at Peat Marwick

# Early Years at Peat Marwick
**Story ID:** P1_S18
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1963–1970s, Public accounting / auditing (Big Eight), Proving professional competence and securing career advancement while managing high-stakes client work with inconsistent partner support and limited resources., Assume personal ownership of deliverables when acc, Cultivate and rely on mentors and sponsors to cham, Seek technical expertise and formal review when op, Embrace intense

## Summary

A newly minted accountant joins Peat Marwick in 1963, rapidly advances through demanding audit work, assumes responsibility during crises, and becomes the firm's youngest partner by 1970.

## Full Text

Early Y ears at
Peat Marwick
On July 1, 1963, a skinny young guy with a flattop haircut, fresh out of college, newly married and essentially broke, stumbled into
Jackson, Mississippi in his beat-up 1957 Ford and reported for work at a venerable accounting firm. The firm was then named Peat,
Marwick, Mitchell & Co. At the time it was the largest of the Big
Eight accounting firms, with offices scattered worldwide.
Peat Marwick had emerged at the top of the stack during my job interviews primarily due to a perceived personality fit with the firm overall and the pro file and comfort of the Jackson office.
It was an intimidating environment —a city with high-rise office buildings, professionals dressed in formal business suits, rules and regulations to learn, rigorous schedules and deadlines, and unstated or undeclared expectations. I was a bit nervous and anxious, but only for a few days. Then I called upon some of the self-con fidence
I had developed in college and dove headlong into a thirty-two-year career with the firm.
My starting salary as a junior accountant on the audit sta ff was the lowest of all the o ffers I’d had in my senior year, but it proved sufficient for Dot and me to stay a float. I had a bank account with
$100 (a wedding gift from my parents) and $125 in Series E bonds that I had saved for ten years. Dot and I had rented a small furnished apartment on the west side of Jackson for $75 per month, and walked hand-in-hand over to a First National Bank branch near the apartment to cash in my bonds. On the way home, we stopped off at
Kroger’s grocery store and used up most of that money for groceries and household supplies. We smiled nervously at each other as we pushed the loaded cart all the way to our apartment and reflected on our empty pockets. (We returned the cart to the store, of course.)
We had been married only two weeks when I reported for my first day of work.
The Jackson office employed about twenty- five professionals and enjoyed a reputation as the preeminent accounting firm in
Mississippi. The office was respected and well connected throughout the entire business community. It had operated as an independent firm, Dick D. Quinn & Co., for many years before merging with Peat
Marwick in 1961. The rapid transition from a small, local business into part of a multinational one had apparently proven challenging over the previous two years for most of the Peat Marwick staff there,
and it soon became clear that I wasn’t the only anxious soul in the office. I would see this scenario play out again and again during my thirty-two years, whenever a small local company merged with a larger and more complicated organization.
Fortified by my excellent accounting education at USM and summer internships at M.M. Winkler & Associates and Humble Oil
& Refining Co., I was fortunate to be able to hit the ground running.
My first assignment was the annual audit of Mississippi School
Supply Company and related companies. Bob Duncan was the senior accountant on the engagement. He was an energetic, thoughtful guy and was pivotal in ge tting me started o ff on the right foot, with his great balance of instruction, delegation and supervision. I can still see his cryptic review notes scrawled with his green-leaded pencil.
I can also still close my eyes and see the twinkle in his eyes as he labored to teach me the nuts and bolts of auditing. Bob gave me superior ratings for my performance, which led to new and more varied assignments throughout my first year. I still have copies of some of his evaluations. Meanwhile, I continued working hard —it was a year of constant activity —and gaining invaluable experience,
and good performance evaluations continued. At the end of a first year, I was astonished and grati fied to receive a 20 percent raise to
$7,200, something virtually unheard of at the time. That feverish and fruitful first year set in motion a pace and momentum that lasted more than thirty years.
I took the three-day CPA examination in November 1963,
five months after joining the firm, and passed all three accounting sections with excellent scores; I completed the business law section the next May. I was then awarded the CPA certificate and license, the passport to my chosen profession.
I moved quickly from junior accountant to senior. At the end of my third year, I was promoted to supervisor, a full year ahead of the normal schedule. Two years later, I was promoted to manager,
again considerably ahead of the norm. During those first five years, I had the opportunity to work on a highly diversi fied array of client accounts. My assignments included a wide assortment of commercial businesses—lumber, meat packing, wholesale clothing,
retail furniture, newspapers, a television station and a printing company. I also had the opportunity to be exposed to several specialized industries —savings and loan associations, banks,
municipalities, construction, bond dealers and colleges. Usually,
I spent a few weeks during the winters in the tax department,
preparing individual returns.
This early experience included more than two dozen personal,
trust and partnership tax returns each year for the extensive
Hederman clan, our wealthiest clients. I also audited their sprawling newspaper businesses. The three brothers and one cousin in this large family werevery private, but for some reason they developed a trust in me and brought me into their confidence with virtually every aspect of their financial lives. Twice, the Hedermans o ffered me attractive positions, attempting to lure me away from Peat Marwick.
Where else on earth could a young man straight out of college have found such a diversified opportunity to learn the fundamentals of business and financial management but right there at Peat Marwick in Jackson, Mississippi? Still locked —but accessible!—in my mind today is a treasure trove of valuable lessons and unique experiences gleaned from this wide-ranging set of businesses and organizations.
William L. Norton was the office managing partner and a respected member of the Jackson business establishment. He was the most revered mentor for me in my entire career and was also one of the finest friends I have ever had. He wrapped his arms around me from the get-go and never let go until the day he died. He had an unflagging confidence in my ability and continuously heaped larger and larger responsibilities on me. I was never really sure why, but he believed in me and promoted me everywhere he went.
In many ways, Bill was an odd duck. His demeanor was much like that of the stereotypical absent-minded professor, as he stumbled along in his own self-contained world. Sometimes in the midst of a business crisis, one could find him tending his orchids in his greenhouse. He was extremely intelligent and financially astute,
but always seemed like an old man to me. I always respectfully called him Mr. Norton, never Bill. One day shortly after I was promoted to
William L. Norton manager, his secretary, Gloria Smith, came into my office, closed the door and said in a low voice, “Keith, Mr. Norton would like for you to call him Bill!” So I did, from that point forward. Bill practically adopted me as his surrogate during my two-year stint as a manager.
In many respects I served as his alter ego.
My workload increased dramatically as a manager, with responsibilities that were technical as well as managerial. On two major engagements, I was e ffectively the partner with full responsibility and almost sole contact with top-level client management. One of these was L&A Contracting Company, a road and bridge builder. L&A was a family business with a father and two brothers as the owners. I developed an excellent relationship with my primary contract there, Harley Sims (one of the brothers), and he consulted me regularly on virtually everything financial. Because of this close relationship, I gained a deep understanding of their business, as well as a serious commitment to their welfare.
In late 1968, I was handling a complex audit assignment at a pivotal time in L&A’s history. Bill Norton, who was the official client service partner, was delinquent in his a ttention to this client, and we were down to the wire with our report —a report where the company’s very survival hung in the balance. The company had a major construction contract for the Golden Glades interchange in
Miami, and their Florida license was being held in abeyance pending submission of audited financial statements. The day before the deadline, Bill still had made no review of our work. I was desperately trying to press him into action with predictions of dire consequences.
Seemingly a bit frustrated with me, he picked up the previously untouched report drafts lying on the floor behind his desk, peered over his glasses with a sober stare and said, “Cobb, is it OK for me to sign this report?” I said, “Sure… ” and without further hesitation he heavily signed the firm’s name—Peat, Marwick, Mitchell & Co.
Then he escorted me out of his office, his hand on my shoulder and the reports under my arm.
It was an expression of absolute confidence. I stayed up all night double checking every one of the numbers! This single event was a turning point in my career, when I began to realize the burden of decision-making. From that point forward, I began to take total personal ownership of my work. T oday, almost fifty years later, I still think about this experience and the lesson it taught me every time
I drive down I-95 to Miami and pass through that Golden Glades interchange.
An even more signi ficant turning point occurred shortly thereafter. Cal-Maine Foods, Inc. ’s initial public offering was in 1969.
At that time, I was the audit manager for Adams Egg Farms, a major egg producer in Mississippi, when Fred Adams decided to embark on a huge three-way merger with Dairy Fresh Products in California and Maine Egg Farms in Portland, Maine, to create the largest egg supplier in the United States. He indeed did that—by a wide margin.
The three businesses standing alone were themselves incredibly complex conglomerations of corporations, partnerships and sole proprietorships. T rying to combine and amalgamate them into a cohesive single entity was nothing short of mind-boggling. Making matters worse, the financial records of all the entities were a mess.
This is when, as if the merger didn’t provide enough complexity,
Fred decided to simultaneously launch an initial public offering and raise capital for further expansion.
My team worked feverishly on the Cal-Maine project for six months, migrating from one emergency to the next. Near the end, I
was working seven days a week, twelve to fourteen chargeable hours each day, to the point of almost total exhaustion. It was my first experience with a public offering or even an SEC filing. Coupled with the backbreaking workload and exponential learning curve, I had virtually no partner support. Guy Story, the designated engagement partner, was perpetually AWOL. He had basically checked out, in a shameful demonstration of unprofessional conduct. So I bypassed the engagement partner and took on the partner responsibilities myself. It wasn’t that I was so cocky at thevery young age of twenty-eight; I was desperate and determined to serve the client professionally and responsibly.
I enlisted the guidance of a senior Peat Marwick SEC reviewing partner in the Houston office named Bill Su ttle and he agreed to review my work and sign off on behalf of the firm. Bill was a serious professional, solemn and straight-laced. He was the quintessential technical accountant nearing the end of an illustrious career with the firm. He was a man of few words—tough and rigid, yet practical and level headed. His maturity and judgment were critical assets to me.
Without him, I would never have made it through the engagement.
Eventually, we got the job done: the merger was completed and the surviving company became publicly owned. Despite the agony, the rigorous experience turned out to be a godsend to me personally.
Bill Suttle was impressed by the quality of my work on Cal-Maine,
much as a proud father might view his only son. He was lavish in his praise and compliments, which was uncharacteristic of him. He even took the initiative to provide wri tten recommendations to regional partner-in-charge Gray Wake field, saying that Cal-Maine was the most complex audit engagement he had ever seen in his long career and that my performance was the best he had ever witnessed in any professional setting. That recommendation essentially became my ticket on a continuing bullet train.
Buried somewhere in my memorabilia, I have retained the SEC
prospectus from that 1969 Cal-Maine IPO. It still looks complicated!
The company has survived all these years, through all the ups and downs of the commodities marketplace, with Fred Adams still serving as the chairman emeritus. His son-in-law is now the CEO
and the company has a flock of over thirty- five million chickens producing a billion dozen eggs per year. Y es, a billion dozen! T oday,
the company’s revenues represent over 20 percent of the nation’s total egg consumption. Whenever I read about Cal-Maine, I quietly take pride in knowing that I played a major role in its financial engineering and complex formation. I conjure up a wry smile when
I read its annual reports and come across a few extant words that I
actually wrote for its initial prospectus over forty years ago.
I lost track of Fred for many years as I moved around the country, but bumped into him at a Federal Reserve dinner in Atlanta a few years ago. Seeing me, he embraced me in a huge bear hug and bounced me up and down for several minutes (he’s a giant of a man).
He then began reciting war stories from 1969. Good memories of some tough times!
One of the highlights during my time in Jackson was the assortment of people whom I had the privilege of knowing. There was Cowan Gooch, an elderly distinguished partner who was a great teacher with a fatherly demeanor. Guy Story, an audit partner, was a Barney Fife look-a-like and a rather goofy guy who didn’t work very hard —certainly the antithesis of the traditional image of a
Big Eight partner. Rhyne Neubert was a taciturn tax partner who seldom had a nice thing to say about anything. Then there was the positive Julian Noel, a career manager who had worked in the office for more than thirty years and handled some of the old-line clients.
Bob Duncan was an Auburn University transplant, a smart, energetic and conscientious professional. Jimmy Smith was a hard-drinking,
often vulgar supervisor and, nevertheless, a capable auditor who was dedicated to his clients. And so the list went on. It was quite a menagerie of personalities. I didn’t necessarily follow or buddy up with any of them, but I learned a lot from all. There were also a number of contemporaries with whom I worked or who worked for me. I had a lot of respect for them —Murray Underwood, Atlee
Harmon, Paul Breazeale, Mike Green, Homer Cummings, Bill
Eubank, Johnny Black, Cecil Brown and many others. But the most important person of all these associates was Bill Norton.
I mentioned him before but I didn’t write that he was a vocabulary and grammar nut, a word connoisseur. For years and years, I played an odd game with him —a game of vocabulary riddles. The game was our own invention and we never actually discussed the origin or rules; we just played it. It normally started by him making a statement using some arcane word. Then I would try to craft an esoteric response, letting him know that I understood and causing him to think hard about my response. This would in turn force him into an even more abstruse rejoinder. And so on.
Sometimes these comebacks would be weeks apart —a subtle offhand remark, a cryptic note in a margin, an outright question.
This made the game even more di fficult, calling on the memory of the last iteration. And we often had multiple games going at once. In fact, it would occasionally be months or years before we would pick up once again on an old string. All the while, we never outwardly acknowledged that it was a game at all. The game actually started when Bill used the word “quixotic” incorrectly once in describing
Ernie Jenkins, a client who changed his mind frequently. I picked up on the error, and rather than correct him overtly, I crafted an oblique response that subtly caused him to realize his error and consult the huge dictionary in the living room of his home. A week or so later,
he mentioned that I was much like Don Quixote in chasing a wild-eyed tax scheme, but he suspected that I was unwilling to change my mind. I knew then that he knew that I knew that he knew what quixotic meant.
And so it went until he died at the age of eighty-nine. What an interesting man! I really miss him. If I could see him today, I would casually mutter something like, “Y ou know, Bill, ten years is just too long for any caesura to last. Don’t you think?” Y ears ago, I bought a two-thousand-page Random House dictionary just like his and it sits prominently today on a stand in my study.
But back to the chronology of the working years. In late 1969, I
was approached by Dave Thompson, partner in charge of personnel for Peat Marwick, asking me to transfer to New Y ork and work in the
Department of Professional Practice (DPP) for two years. DPP was a highly coveted assignment in the technical nerve center of the firm and a virtual guaranteed pathway to partnership. Only three or four managers were selected annually for the prestigious assignment. I
was flattered and honored to be among this elite corps, but basically
I didn’t want to do it. Though I felt that I was technically capable of the task, I did not relish the idea of sitting in an ivory tower without client contact for two years. Moreover, I didn’t want to move to New
Y ork. I fretted and fre tted over the decision, fearing that declining the o ffer would signal the end of my career. Nobody ever turned down such a rare opportunity! Nevertheless, I did my best at politely explaining my views and sheepishly turned my back on the brass ring. Dave Thompson appeared stricken by a state of lockjaw, but he eventually moved on and let me be.
Lo and behold, only a few months later the unbelievable happened. On a Sunday afternoon in June 1970, Bill Norton showed up unexpectedly at my home and told me that I had been elected to partnership in Peat, Marwick, Mitchell & Co. I was stunned.
And overwhelmed. It hadn’t even occurred to me that I would be nominated, much less elected. Here I was, just a regular guy, barely twenty-nine years old, stuck in the back woods of the vast empire of Peat Marwick. Y et I was being o ffered a seat in the club. Wow!
As it turned out, I was the youngest person ever to be admi tted to partnership in the seventy-three-year history of Peat Marwick. How did this happen? I was not totally certain. Everything about the partnership was a big secret back then. But I later learned that Bill
Norton made a forceful and passionate nomination, and Bill Su ttle championed my candidacy in the background. Gray Wake field thought Bill Suttle could do no wrong and thus decided that I must be the best thing to come along since ice cream. Dave Thompson was apparently not in the mix of this decision-making process. Gray picked up Bill Su ttle’s mantle and, I think, rammed my nomination through the executive commi ttee. Gray at least claimed full credit for it. For the next twenty- five years, even on a couple of occasions far after his retirement, whenever I saw Gray he always crowed about making me the youngest partner in the history of the firm. I was grateful for all of their efforts, to say the least.
So, I ended this phase of my career as a paid sta ff member and joined a group of about five hundred distinguished professionals as a full general partner. My partnership position became e ffective exactly seven years to the day from the time I started with the firm.
My compensation had compounded at a rate of about 30 percent per year since my first day. In my last year as a manager, I earned
$26,753. From that point forward, my income would be wholly dependent on a share of the firm’s earnings, a share that would prove to increase steadily over time.
Peat Marwick partners’ meeting, 1970
Among my most cherished memorabilia from those early days is a photograph of the five hundred partners attending the seventy-third annual partners’ meeting in October 1970. The meeting was held at the luxurious Greenbrier resort in White Sulphur Springs,
West Virginia. The partners in the photograph are all dressed in black tuxedos and reflect a countenance that conjures up images of the captains of industry in the mid-1900s. Simply put, I felt as if I
had arrived! It is di fficult to describe my emotions as I sat in that massive ballroom, my mind racing all the way back to my childhood in Calhoun City, through college and then all the hard work over the past seven years. Most importantly though, I re flected on all of my mentors over the years, much like the scrolling credits at the end of an epic movie —Miss Frances Mallory, Dr. Carter Dobbs, Dr.
McCain, Dr. Switzer, Bill Norton, Bill Su ttle and a host of others.
They had all played lead roles in my drama. And I thought about
Dot, the star headliner of the movie, who loved me and supported me through those frenetic times. I pinched myself to be sure it was real. When the goosebumps eventually subsided, I saddled up and began moving on the next phase of my career.

## Principles

- Assume personal ownership of deliverables when accountability gaps appear.
- Cultivate and rely on mentors and sponsors to champion advancement.
- Seek technical expertise and formal review when operating at the edge of regulatory complexity.
- Embrace intense, challenging assignments as accelerants for learning and career momentum.

## Decision heuristics

- When partner support is absent, assume necessary responsibilities and secure an independent, qualified reviewer to validate the work.
- Prioritize client-critical deadlines and act decisively if the client's survival depends on timely deliverables.
- Decline prestigious roles that sacrifice the type of work you value after weighing long-term fit and personal priorities.
- Treat explicit expressions of mentor confidence as authorization to accept greater responsibility and act without hesitation.

---

# P1_S19 – On the Move as a Peat Marwick Partner

# On the Move as a Peat
**Story ID:** P1_S19
**Part:** One
**Tags:** July 1970 – August 1971, Accounting / Professional services, Whether to act on observed partner negligence that endangered clients and the firm despite partnership norms protecting partners from dismissal., Assume ownership and use formal authority to addre, Raise concerns about professional negligence when, Leverage formal position to make previously withhe, Include family considerations in major relocation

## Summary

A newly made partner at Peat Marwick asserts ownership by pushing for the removal of a negligent partner and later accepts an invitation to establish a new office in Orlando after securing a visit with his wife.

## Full Text

On the Move as a Peat
Marwick Partner
My early days as a Peat Marwick partner, beginning in July 1970,
were heady times for me. I was showered with congratulatory letters,
cards and phone calls from both inside the firm and outside from the local community. I remember with fondness a joint visit to my office by the three Hederman brothers —Zach, Henry and Bob —
who came over in tandem to personally express their compliments.
These old guys (they surely seemed old to me) appeared to be as proud as I was. Later that month, Henry made an announcement at an Exchange Club meeting, and everyone stood and applauded. I
felt like a celebrity. However, the euphoria faded quickly; I had work to do. And I was keenly conscious that I had a responsibility to live up to my new credentials.
The move into the partnership position was a relative non-event from a client management standpoint. I already had complete responsibility for the majority of the clients (including Cal-Maine
Foods and L&A Contracting Company) that had been virtually abandoned by the previous partners. The only big di fference was that I could now o fficially sign the firm’s name to the reports. I was already deeply involved in the office management, so there was no big transition there either.
My feeling of self-con fidence and pride in the firm escalated,
and I took on the role of ownership with sincerity and earnestness.
A change in prestige and respect came with the position, both internally and externally. I developed a boldness that soon exhibited itself in an interesting initiative on my part. Prior to that time, I
had constrained my outrage over the negligence of the designated partner on the Cal-Maine Foods engagement. I had felt that open criticism of a partner might be viewed as overstepping my position.
As a partner myself, however, I felt that I now had the imprimatur to make my views known.
Therefore, in the first week of July, I strode into Bill Norton’s office and told him I strongly believed that the partner should be evicted from the firm. His conduct and negligence on this major client engagement and others had been egregious and grossly unprofessional, in my view. Moreover, it was reckless and put the firm at risk. Bill didn’t disagree, but he was reluctant to act. First,
the man had been his partner for more than twenty years. Secondly,
firing a partner was an extremely rare event for Peat Marwick in those days. Partnership was considered a permanent career position, tantamount to a federal judge. Only Walter Hanson, the firm’s distinguished senior partner, handled forced resignations of partners. Hanson approached this type of task with guarded reluctance and trepidation, and he personally explained each instance at the annual national meeting of partners. Emboldened by my own personal convictions and sense of right and wrong, I pressed hard on the matter. Eventually, Bill Norton was persuaded and made a formal recommendation to the firm’s executive committee, with my testimony and that of Bill Su ttle as background. Walter Hanson accepted the recommendation, and that guy was history. It was a moment of catharsis for me.
My first year as a partner was a busy one on several fronts. I had responsibility for a number of the important clients and continued my role in the administration of the office as Bill Norton’s surrogate.
Bill was convinced that my destiny was to be a managing partner and ultimately assume a national leadership role in the firm. He encouraged me to consider transferring to another office to broaden my experience and exposure. He also encouraged the firm to consider me for such a role somewhere. That moment arrived the following spring. The firm had decided to open an office in Orlando and invited me to move there and establish a presence. I went over to Atlanta and met with Norm White, the regional partner in charge, to discuss the prospect. At the conclusion of our meeting, I
said I would like to take my wife and make a visit to Orlando to be sure it was the right thing for us. I’ll never forget his response. As he sat in a restaurant chomping on a big cigar and sipping his Jack
Daniels, he muttered, “Y eah, that’s probably a good idea. However,
you shouldn’t forget that our executive committee has already met,
and we’ve decided you’re the best damn man in the firm to do this.
But, hell, who knows? Y ou might get down there and decide your judgment is better than ours!” Not to appear stupid or slow on the uptake, I quickly said, “OK, I’ll do it. Sign me up. But I want to make the trip just for my wife’s sake?” He smiled wryly, with the cigar still rolling around in his mouth, and said, “ Attaboy!”
So it would be off to Orlando in August 1971.

## Principles

- Assume ownership and use formal authority to address previously suppressed problems.
- Raise concerns about professional negligence when it endangers clients and the firm.
- Leverage formal position to make previously withheld views known.
- Include family considerations in major relocation decisions.

## Decision heuristics

- When promoted to formal authority, speak up about critical issues you previously withheld.
- Persistently press leadership until action is taken when conduct endangers clients or the firm.
- When offered an important relocation, involve and consult your spouse by visiting together before committing.

# P1_S20 – The Florida Frontier

# The Florida Frontier
**Story ID:** P1_S20
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1971–mid 1970s, Accounting / CPA firm (Peat Marwick), Whether to persist in building a Peat Marwick office in Orlando despite limited local market opportunity and short-term losses, or to close the office to avoid prolonged unprofitability., Assess local market fundamentals before committing, Be candid with leadership and present a documented, Build local networks and leverage introductions to, Start small and iterate: use temporary arrangement, Accept responsibility for firm resources and act t

## Summary

A young Peat Marwick partner relocates to Orlando in 1971 to establish a new office with little local research or resources, builds a small team and client base, then recommends closing the office when local market fundamentals prove weak.

## Full Text

The Florida Frontier
Walt Disney World opened its massive theme park In Orlando in August 1971, and the powers that be at Peat Marwick reasoned that there would be an in flux of business, which would be good for the firm. As sophisticated as Peat Marwick was, the rationale for this conclusion was nothing more complicated than that. When I
learned that the firm had done practically no market research on
Orlando, my mind wandered o ff to the California gold rush in the
1800s and how people trudged o ff to a new life with nothing more than a rumor or perhaps a scrap of paper saying things would be better there. I went back to Atlanta to meet again with Norm White to see if he had any advice for me. He was friendly and cordial, as much as a crusty old curmudgeon or salty dog can be. But at the end of our bland meeting, he summed up his advice with, “Hell,
what do I know? I never opened a damn office! Just go down there and beat the bushes. Y ou’ll do fine!” So, we loaded up our virtual covered wagon and o ff we went to Orlando in August 1971. I was the “managing partner” of an office that didn’t even exist. It was not exactly an excursion for the faint of heart!
Dot and I reconfigured our lives as we adjusted to our new home in Altamonte Springs, an Orlando suburb. I was thirty years old and
Dot was twenty-nine. Our first son, Paul, was three years old, and Dot was six months pregnant with John. We felt like true pioneers, setting out to explore a new land. Our family life was a positive experience in multiple respects, right from the get-go. We had the good fortune of settling in next door to two of the most wonderful families —the
Letourneaus and the Morans. Both became fast friends from the start and both were extremely helpful in getting us acclimated to our new surroundings. They have remained valued friends to this day. In late 2014, I had the opportunity to spend a wonderful day with an ailing Roy Letourneau in Dallas, just a month before he died.
As it turned out, I was the first Peat Marwick partner to work officially in the state of Florida. Florida had what were locally called
“snow bird laws” that prohibited national CPA or non-Florida law firms from practicing in the state, as well as some types of medical practices. So, the major firms had a ffiliates to handle their Florida work. These affiliate firms operated under profit-sharing agreements and were essentially shadows of the national firms. The laws were in the process of being changed in 1971, with a ra ft of restrictive clauses being implemented. Compliance was tricky and penalties for non-compliance were stiff. T echnically I was supposed to resign from the firm and join an a ffiliated firm in Jacksonville for about a month until my reciprocal CPA certificate was issued by the state.
Peat Marwick had no clients in the Orlando area and I had no staff to work with at the beginning. It was just me—a lonely feeling.
But I plunged in head first, trying to find my way around and get connected in the business community. I remember si tting at my kitchen table working on plans, while my thoughts dri fted off into the clouds wondering what in the world I had gotten myself into.
After a few weeks, I came across a secretary who had worked for Peat Marwick in Boston. I hired her immediately and rented a temporary one-room office downtown. It was just this secretary and me sitting face-to-face in a small, lonely office. As it turned out, the office was adjacent to a real estate promoter named George Barley,
who I met in the hallway. George operated on a shoestring and always had projects in the works. He was invariably short on cash—
and financial documentation—for his ideas. He asked me to help him with some financial projections, and in lieu of fees, he agreed to introduce me around town and recommend me to others. George was a rather gay blade, a bit of a bon vivant and a bit of a flaneur. He seemed to be friendly with virtually everyone in town.
George Barley was a lucky find. Through him, I began to pick up bits and pieces of work, clients that actually paid fees for the services. George introduced me to his brother Kim, who headed the Dean Wi tter Reynolds stock brokerage firm in town. Like
George, Kim seemed to take pleasure tucking me under his wings.
He sponsored me for membership in the exclusive University Club downtown. He introduced me to Don Senterfitt, the senior partner in the prestigious law firm of Akerman Senterfitt; Don later became the chairman of SunT rust. Don insisted that I move to the brand new
CNA building, in the heart of the business district, where his firm was located. This made a lot of sense to me. Don was also generous in his client recommendations. And so the wheels began to turn.
All of these growing recommendations were then supplemented by serendipity. Our Minneapolis client General Mills acquired Red
Lobster restaurants, a still-embryonic restaurant chain that had been started by William Darden in Orlando. Our office promptly took over all of the audit and tax work from a small firm, and Red
Lobster thus became our office’s first sizable, vibrant audit client.
A Rochester, New Y ork office client, Ganne tt Newspapers, quickly followed that good fortune. Ganne tt was secretly developing an experimental prototype for what eventually became USA T oday.
It was called Cocoa T oday and was located in Cocoa Beach, only fifty miles away. In a chance meeting, the Rochester partner on the Gannett account con fided in me about this highly con fidential
R&D project. I told him I had some signi ficant experience with the
Hederman newspapers in Mississippi, and volunteered to help his client. Bingo! I was suddenly on the ground floor of a burgeoning national newspaper success story, analyzing the financial dynamics and preparing confidential pro forma financial information. T oday,
every time I pick up a copy of USA T oday, my mind races back to that grubby, windowless room in a nondescript warehouse a stone’s throw from the Kennedy Space Center, where I pored over yellow working papers as the Ganne tt executives struggled with the new concept. In an interesting twist ten years later, Ganne tt purchased the Clarion-Ledger, the Hederman family flagship newspaper in
Jackson, Mississippi.
About six months after we arrived in Orlando and in the midst of scrambling around trying to get something going, I received an interesting phone call from Bob Boutillier. Bob was the deputy senior partner of the firm and effectively the chief operating officer.
He opened the conversation by saying, “Keith, do you realize you’re losing money down there? We don’t have any offices that actually lose money!” My initial reaction was, “What the hell do you expect?” but
I restrained my outrage and explained the situation as best I could.
Bob knew absolutely nothing about the situation. Once I made my case and he learned about the firm’s strategy of investing in a new marketplace with a young partner, he was very supportive.
We gradually began to construct a skeleton sta ff for the office.
With some heavy persuasion, I was able to arrange a few transfers of experienced personnel from other offices—Bill McKeever from
Philadelphia, Jim Felcyn from Chicago, Paul Breazeale from Jackson and David Graham from Jacksonville. Lower-level sta ff members were recruited from Florida colleges. Over a four-year period we assembled a staff of fourteen professionals and three administrative people. Although small, it was a tight knit and e ffective team. I
remember them all with fondness. Paul Breazeale was especially helpful in those days. He took a huge leap of faith to follow me to
Orlando and worked his tail off to help me make it work. After a few years he moved back to Jackson and established his own accounting firm. His firm is now a flagship business in Jackson, and Paul is a distinguished professional in the community. Paul and his family have remained dear and valuable friends to this day and I stay in touch with him regularly.
I worked hard during those four years and was proud of our accomplishments. I had started from absolute scratch and now had built a legitimate Peat Marwick office. Also, my family was comfortable and happy in Orlando.
But despite our apparent success and personal happiness, I was plagued with a nagging notion that the stars were not lining up for the firm long term. Orlando was a virtual boomtown, reminiscent of scenes from Old West movies, recast in modern glass, concrete and multi-lane highways. Construction and new businesses were springing up in all directions, most of them tethered in some way to Walt Disney World and the tourism boom it promised. I began to realize that all of this hustle and bustle did not necessarily translate to opportunity for Peat Marwick. The overwhelming majority of commercial development there was owned, operated and accounted for elsewhere, in some other headquarters. There were actually very few sizable, locally headquartered businesses —
businesses that could potentially be profitable clients for the firm—
in the Orlando area. And none of this was helped by the 1973–1975
recession throughout the United States, which, coupled with a major international oil crisis, put a temporary damper on travel and tourism. Accordingly, as I reviewed our revenue base I came to the stark realization that further development of a mature Peat Marwick office was likely to string out over several years down the road.
I brooded privately about this prognosis for several months. I
wanted to keep a positive front for my staff to keep them motivated.
And I didn’t want to upset the apple cart with my family as they basked in their happiness. Y et, I felt an overwhelming sense of responsibility to the firm to do the right thing. I had to provide the firm’s management with my on-the-ground best judgment as to the future of our business. Finally, I bit the bullet and wrote a serious letter to Walter Hanson. I slaved over the words. I outlined my assessment of the marketplace and feasibility of signi ficant growth and development in the short run. I concluded with a recommendation that we close the office and lay plans to come back in five or ten years, after Orlando had matured to a stage beyond a branch office town centered on the hospitality industry. I was thirty-four years old when I wrote that le tter and didn’t have a clue about what this might mean for my career.
A few days after dropping my situation assessment in the mailbox, Walter Hanson called me to say he agreed with my report.
He complimented me on the courage (as he characterized it) to voluntarily submit this recommendation and assured me he would personally see that a meaningful career opportunity was placed in my pathway. He instructed me to lay plans to close the office, transfer client responsibilities to the T ampa office and await further word as to my future. And so I did just that.
About a month later, I received a call from Norm White. He advised me that I had been selected as the managing partner of the
Fort Lauderdale office, replacing Howard Miller, who was moving to
Switzerland. So, off we were to Fort Lauderdale and a new adventure in our odyssey.

## Principles

- Assess local market fundamentals before committing long-term resources.
- Be candid with leadership and present a documented, on-the-ground assessment even when recommendations are difficult.
- Build local networks and leverage introductions to generate early clients when starting from scratch.
- Start small and iterate: use temporary arrangements to establish presence before scaling.
- Accept responsibility for firm resources and act to prevent prolonged unprofitability.

## Decision heuristics

- When entering a new geography, verify market demand with research instead of relying on general optimism.
- Use local connectors and barter (e.g., services for introductions) to build initial paid engagements quickly.
- If a candid assessment shows limited near-term prospects, recommend an orderly exit rather than prolonging losses.
- Document and communicate a clear, evidence-based market assessment to firm leadership to enable constructive decisions.
