# P1_S21 – New Roots in Fort Lauderdale

# New Roots in Fort Lauderdale
**Story ID:** P1_S21
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1975–1989, public accounting / audit, Establishing credibility and a growing, high-quality practice as an outsider in a tight-knit business community while managing client risk, fraudulent engagements, and firm-level strategic change., Proactively seek counsel and ask questions to lear, Invest personal resources—time and money—to build, Recruit and retain top talent as the foundation of, When you don't understand a prospective client's b, Act on ethical intuition promptly

## Summary

A managing partner relocates to Fort Lauderdale (1975), builds a successful audit practice by recruiting talent and cultivating relationships, navigates major client work, fraud investigations, and a disruptive firm merger, then accepts a transfer to Baltimore in 1989.

## Full Text

New Roots in Fort Lauderdale
We arrived in Fort Lauderdale in the summer of 1975. A fter searching the various neighborhoods and surrounding suburbs, we decided on living inside the city itself and purchased a house in the
Coral Ridge area. The neighborhood was nice, probably a notch above our means at the time, but it appealed to us for a number of reasons. Having made the plunge from Jackson to Orlando just four years before, this move was smoother and easier. The people in Fort
Lauderdale were friendly and the business was well established. We settled in quickly, enrolled Paul in public school and began to find our way around town.
We joined the Coral Ridge Presbyterian Church, where Dot becamevery active over time. We joined the Lauderdale Yacht
Club, which seemed to be the social center of the community. After a year, we moved Paul to Pine Crest School, which lived up to its reputation as one of the country’s finest educational institutions—
and turned out to be another valuable connection point for us in the community. We became quite comfortable and enthusiastically made For Lauderdale our new home. We were to stay there for fourteen years.
From a business point of view, the next fourteen years was an unquestionable success in almost every respect. Looking back on those days, I a ttribute our business success to two basic things:
recruiting outstanding professionals and cultivating valuable relationships.
I was the newly appointed managing partner of the Fort
Lauderdale Peat Marwick office. There were two other partners —
Bob Happ and Hy Indowsky —and a total sta ff complement of twenty-four professionals. The firm’s practice was an emerging business, as contrasted to an “establishment” mainstream business.
The offices were on Sunrise Boulevard, outside the main drag of the city and its commercial center. Real estate and construction clients, bustling industries at the time in South Florida, largely dominated the clientele. Our clients were also tightly tethered to the
Jewish community, re flecting the in fluence of Howard Miller, the former managing partner, and Hy Indowsky, who were both active personalities in that segment of society.
Realizing that I needed to establish my own identity in the community, I set about to build a network. I recalled my success in
Orlando, reaching out to others with initiative and boldness. I asked a lot of questions of everyone I met and sought advice liberally. I
invited Larry Horner to lunch and sought his counsel. Larry, the future chairman of the firm, was the managing partner of the Miami office at the time. He had been incredibly successful networking as a newcomer and an “outsider” in the tight-knit Miami community.
Larry had some wonderful advice for me, much of which became a fundamental part of my relationship strategies for the balance of my career. For example, he advised me to use some of my personal funds to invest in my own personal success, as contrasted to waiting for the firm to make a move. That was something that had never explicitly occurred to me. He told me how he made a personal contribution to the University of Miami and was immediately appointed to the board of directors. I began doing that sort of thing, both financially and with my time, and I learned how people recognize and respect those personal commitments. I recently saw Larry, who is now in poor health and con fined to a wheelchair, and I thanked him again for his sage advice forty years ago. He smiled and looked pleased that
I remembered.
I also recall receiving a cold call from Ellio tt Barne tt, who managed a successful law firm in the city but was also somewhat outside the mainstream in those days. Ellio tt welcomed me to the city, invited me to lunch and o ffered to help in any way he could.
That one lunch triggered a close friendship with this brilliant and influential man, a friendship that lasted for twenty years, until he died. One ironic piece of advice from him stuck with me. He said,
“Keith, you need to steer your firm toward the mainstream business community and not be so reliant on the Jewish community. ” A
strange comment, I thought, coming from the managing partner of a
100 percent Jewish law firm. Nevertheless, I began watching his firm and saw how he was diversifying his own practice. So I set about to emulate his behavior. I absorbed and cataloged Elliott’s advice, along with all the others.
I learned a lot from these two guys and I grew up a lot in the process.
I did considerable research on the makeup and pro file of the business community and worked hard at trying to understand the demographic landscape. Admittedly, it was all a little foreign to me.
During this process, I identi fied two individuals who appeared to be among the best-connected and most respected businessmen and professionals in Broward County. They were David Rush, the owner and CEO of ACR Electronics, and Hank Watson, a well-known lawyer, banker and political hack. Both were twenty years or so older than me, and I had no connection points with either of them. Working o ff visceral intuition, I picked up the phone and called them both. I told them that I would like to meet them and to seek their counsel. I suppose they were curious about such boldness,
so each of them agreed. I laid out my situation and openly asked,
“Would you be willing to help me get established in the business community?” Both of them jumped at the opportunity and went out of their way to assist. Even today, I’m not sure why. I remember with fondness how Hank said, in our first meeting, “I’ll tell you what. My wife is chairing the Easter Lily Ball in a few weeks, and she’s short on sponsorships. If you’ll write me a $500 personal check, I’ll stand at the door and introduce you to every person who walks through it!”
So, of course I did. And he did!
These two encounters developed into cherished relationships that lasted for decades. I cannot begin to recite the ways these two men were helpful to me over the years. I tried to reciprocate from time to time, but I’m afraid I still have a dangling liability on my relationship balance sheet. And to think, it all began with a cold call to two strangers. Dave Rush passed away a few years ago. I sat somberly at his funeral at T emple Beth T orah and felt big tears streaming down my face. Hank is living at a retirement home in
Pompano Beach. Even at his advanced age, he is still wheeling and dealing among the elderly residents there. I see him from time to time at church, and he speaks to me much the way a father might address a favorite son. What a couple of guys!
Gradually, the business began to get traction in the broader Fort
Lauderdale business community. A fter five years, we moved the office to a new downtown building at 110 East Broward, just one floor below the Ruden Barne tt law firm, which relocated there at the same time. Symbolically, we all felt as if we had arrived. By that time, the City of Fort Lauderdale was a flagship audit client, along with Century Banks, a long-time Broward institution. We began working with Alamo Rent A Car, which initially was owned by the estate of John D. MacArthur. The Fort Lauderdale staff had grown to about forty professionals, and the business steadily broadened and diversified as it grew. Real estate and construction clients remained in the portfolio, but clients in government, financial services,
manufacturing, health care and service industries eventually overtook them in dominance. Elliott Barnett’s advice was spot on.
Over time, Alamo Rent A Car developed into our largest and most important client. Michael Egan and a group of investors acquired the company from MacArthur in the late 1970s, and they retained our firm as independent accountants. I first met Mike around 1979, when our firm found ourselves in a dispute with the company over some accounting matters. I was called in to help settle the dispute and ended up in heated negotiations with Mike, whom
I met for the first time in the process. That initial confrontation and ultimate settlement resulted in a mutual respect between the two of us that forged the basis for a long and wonderful relationship. As part of our deal, Mike insisted that I assume personal responsibility for their account, which I did for the next ten years. Over that time
Alamo grew from a small car rental company tucked away in South
Florida to a nationwide presence commanding more than 13 percent of the industry market share. Our services grew right along with the company. I have dozens of stories about the variety of services we performed and the company crises we helped Mike navigate.
One signi ficant event occurred in 1986, when I worked side by side with Mike as we negotiated his personal acquisition of controlling interest in the company from the Hanley Dawson family of Chicago. I worked with him every step of the way through the negotiations. T ogether, we landed on a deal valuation of $50 million.
There was a problem, however. Financing was extremely tight. Near the end, we were $3 million short on cash and it looked as if the deal was falling through. As a last ditch measure, Mike and I went over and met with Gordon Oliver, the president of C&S bank, and worked out a $3 million unsecured loan. No collateral, just personal persuasion with an old-fashioned banker who was willing to place his faith in his intuition. This was a seminal event for Mike, and the quality of my personal services in the transaction did not go unnoticed by him. He was profusely grateful.
I can’t say enough about Mike Egan as a client. He was the consummate client. He had absolute con fidence in me and rarely made an important strategic move without consulting me. He is an exceptionally bright man and was extremely demanding, keeping me on my toes constantly. But he was always willing to pay top dollar for quality service. In all those years, while other clients whined incessantly and wheedled about fees, I never discounted our standard rates to Alamo by as much as a dime. And I’m convinced that even today he feels he got his money’s worth. Interestingly, Mike and I
were never close social friends or what one might describe as buddies.
We didn’t golf, have casual lunches or socialize together. Our relationship was strictly a business one, across a desk or conference table dealing with strategic and tactical issues. When I told him I was moving to Baltimore, he expressed frustration and offered to match my compensation if I would decline. I demurred of course. So he made a counter proposal. He offered to pay me a retainer to remain on call for consultation on important matters. I accepted, and several times over the next six years I found myself absorbed in long phone calls discussing General Motors contracts, financing agreements,
legal matters or whatever. I distinctly remember one Saturday night in Baltimore when I was all dressed up in a tuxedo on my way to a black tie social event, and Mike called. I spent an hour and a half on the phone with him while Dot grumbled in the background that we were late for our event. My explanation: Mike came first.
In the mid-1980s, we opened a satellite office in West Palm
Beach and transferred a New Y ork partner, Charles Hurty, to expand our practice in that area. Around the same time, we inherited a small office and practice in Vero Beach. The Vero office had been a part of the W.O. Daley practice, merged into the newly reestablished office in Orlando (about ten years from the Orlando closing, just as I had advised Walter Hanson in 1975).
In 1987, Peat Marwick entered into a large international merger with KMG Main Hurdman, a respected Europe-based firm with a second-tier U.S. branch that did not enjoy the same respect;
the merger was strategically important for Peat Marwick’s global operations, but many of our U.S. offices suffered. I was not fond of the idea for a number of reasons. Peat Marwick chairman Larry Horner organized regional partner meetings beforehand to explain and garner support for the merger. When he announced at the Atlanta meeting that the proposed new firm name would be “KPMG Peat
Marwick” , I stood up and said that it seemed to me that “‘KPMG’
means ‘Kiss Peat Marwick Goodbye’” . And that is what happened.
“Peat Marwick” was dropped from the name and the firm became known as “KPMG” less than two years later. For years, partners teased me about my prophetic remark! At first, the merger boosted the size of our local office by about 20 percent. But Main Hurdman clients and personnel proved not up to our standards and it was not long before much of this new business evaporated.
This was a busy, stressful and productive time for me, trying to make the merger work but struggling with the quality of their people and clients. I had my hands full managing two burgeoning satellite offices, a merged contingent from Main Hurdman, and Alamo.
During our time there, Fort Lauderdale was often referred to as the fraud capital of the country. And for good reason. Inexplicably,
it seemed as if South Florida was a breeding ground for criminal behavior. And we saw more than our fair share of it. In the mid-1980s,
we were called in to investigate a fraud at an outfit called Flowerland,
which, it turned out, had bilked investors out of approximately
$40 million. With the expert skills of Don Dawn on our sta ff, we were eventually able to unravel the unbelievably complex scheme.
One Saturday morning during the same time period, a friend and board member at Commonwealth Savings and Loan in Coral
Springs approached me about doing some forensic work at their institution. I assigned Mary Wood, a senior manager on our staff, to the engagement. After about two months of digging, she managed to identify approximately $45 million of bogus investments that had been camouflaged by the chairman’s brother, who had lost it all in the bond market. I was personally involved in both the Flowerland and Commonwealth engagements, as well as in a high-pro file case with Sahlen Associates, a public company we inherited as a client with the Main Hurdman merger.
On the eve of a proposed $100 million public o ffering by
Sahlen, I was called in to help resolve some problems. Meeting with the Sahlen executives in our conference room, I sni ffed out that something serious was wrong. I began grilling them. The more questions I asked, the weaker their answers were. I finally turned to
Harold Sahlen. It was bold of me, but I said, “Harold, I think you’re lying to me. I’m going to ask you to leave my office, and I should tell you that I’m placing a call to the chairman of your board to report my suspicions. ” My partners cringed; they were afraid of what would happen and were probably embarrassed by the sharpness of my accusation. Harold was stunned, but obediently left, and I made the call. I was right. The entire company was a wholesale fraud and soon became totally unraveled. The next day, I met with Richard
Rainwater of the Bass Brothers, major investors in Sahlen, laid out the issues and told him how their entire board had been duped.
Harold Sahlen was later convicted of securities fraud and sentenced to a federal penitentiary. Thank God for my intuition that day and for the courage to act on it. Earlier in my career, Bill Norton had o ften counseled me to do what was right, even in the face of adversity and opposition. He would say, “Cobb, sometimes adversities set in, and you just have to face into the wind. ” I think he was looking over my shoulder that day.
One other high-pro file fraud to hit Fort Lauderdale was the International Gold Bullion Exchange, an alleged gold trader operated by two brothers named Alderdice. They came on the scene with a splash, leasing three floors in our office building and placing a huge, gaudy sign on the roof. They were throwing around money as if it was chump change —political and charitable contributions,
lavish parties and receptions, beautiful women, full-page ads in
The Wall Street Journal, etc. Bill Alderdice, the other brother —who had formerly been a hair stylist and costume jewelry peddler, of all things —came to visit me, requesting that we sign on as their auditors, but it was clear to me that he wasn’t looking for auditors.
He was looking for respectability and wanted to use our name to establish their credentials in the world. I declined, on the basis that I
didn’t really understand their business. (I didn’t.) He left, but came back a second time, this time begging us to take them on as a client.
He offered me $50,000 as a non-refundable retainer, indicating he could make the check out to me personally. I again declined his offer.
Needless to say, the company washed up in a few months. It was a highly publicized $75 million fraud. All of the purported gold bars in their o ften-photographed vault turned out to be wooden blocks painted gold! Bill and Jim Alderdice went to jail, and Bill was later murdered. Thank God, once again, for all that I had learned from the frauds that came before this one. I wrote a song about the folly and,
in celebration of our good fortune, had it performed at company outings. It was called “Has Anybody Seen My Gold” to the tune of
“Five Foot Two, Eyes Are Blue” .
There were dozens of other bizarre incidents in which I was involved one way or another, although some were not as spectacular or public, and I remember every one of them with a twinge of nostalgia. These episodes honed my skills as a professional executive.
Not only did I mature as an auditor; I acquired some valuable skills in judgment, discernment, ethics and communication.
Relationships were the foundation of our success in Fort
Lauderdale. We gave careful a ttention to constantly expanding and cultivating our relationships. Dot and I were both interested in community service, and found ourselves immersed in a variety of local organizations. We enjoyed people and always felt a sincere obligation to be an integral part of the communities where we lived. Being part of community organizations also led inevitably to new and meaningful relationships over time. So, much of our lives outside of the family were centered around the Coral Ridge
Presbyterian Church, Pine Crest School, Lauderdale Yacht Club and a host of community service organizations. We served on the boards of a number of organizations and were frequent volunteers,
if not their leaders, at all sorts of events. At varying times during these years I served as chairman of the Broward Public Library
Foundation, the Museum of Science and Discovery, the United Way and the Community Foundation. Most of my business relationships and many of my closest friends today are traceable directly to these organizations.
I knew from my experience that the success of a professional practice is highly dependent on quality people. Accordingly, we spent a significant amount of time recruiting and cultivating thevery best accountants I could find. I made treks to Florida State University,
the University of Florida and Florida Atlantic University and personally interviewed the top accounting students. As our practice grew faster than our training programs, I also scouted the firm for quality transfers. I put a full-court press on the best people and lured them into our fold. That involved hard work, persistence and a great deal of persuasion. I occasionally overpaid, I am sure, but in the end it was clearly worth it and more. Ours was a superb cadre, and our clients and the business community knew it. The pristine quality of our sta ff is evidenced today by the evolution of their outstanding careers twenty-five years later. Dozens of them are senior financial executives today and many of them have become superstars in a wide array of businesses. Several of them became partners in KPMG. As I
reflect on those days, I think about Hy Indowsky, Tim Gupton, Larry
King, Rosalie Arthur, Bob Liszewski, Mary Wood, T om Kester, Jill
Vales, Dick Norwalk, Don Dawn, Gene Ballard, T erry Kelley, and on and on. Interestingly, several of these outstanding people, including
Rosalie, Mary and Jill, were picked o ff by Mike Egan at Alamo and
Certified T ours and showed up there years later, when I became the CEO. These are all exceptional people, and I look back on our time together with an enormous sense of pride. And with extreme gratitude. As a group, they were the cornerstones of my own success in Fort Lauderdale. I am truly appreciative of their service. I still stay in touch with a number of them and value their friendship.
During the mid-1980s, I developed a wonderful and lasting friendship with Jim Brocksmith, who was serving as managing partner of the Miami office. Jim and I were “birds of a feather” of sorts, both young emerging leaders in the firm and both harboring similar intellects and value systems. We bonded in those days and worked closely together in the South Florida marketplace. Jim later went on to become deputy chairman and chief operating officer of
KPMG. Our friendship continued after we both le ft South Florida,
and I thoroughly enjoyed working directly with him during my last years with the firm.
From 1975 to 1989, when Dot and I moved to Baltimore, our practice grew five-fold, from twenty-four people at the outset to a full staff complement of 125 professionals. By the end of the 1980s,
we were widely recognized as the “go to” firm in South Florida.
We lapped all the other established firms and actually vanquished
Coopers & Lybrand, which closed its Fort Lauderdale office due to the atrophy of its practice. Our only really strong competitor was Arthur Andersen. Theirs was a quality, well-managed firm that handled all of Wayne Huizenga’s businesses, including Blockbuster
Video. Wayne, a high-profile serial entrepreneur, was solidly in the
Andersen camp. But then I had Mike Egan and Alamo Rent A Car in the KPMG camp. Over a ten-year period ending in 1989, the annual fees billed to Alamo grew from about $30,000 to $1.3 million. That was a huge client for an office our size in that day, and it ranked among KPMG’s largest clients nationally. In the midst of this growth and development, we were also consistently profitable.
KPMG recognized our strong performance, and my personal stature in the firm rose along with it. But after running the Fort
Lauderdale practice as managing partner for fourteen years, it was time for a change —for the firm and for me. Harold Stokes, the regional partner at the time, came down for a visit in early 1989.
During the visit, he casually mentioned some issues he was seeing in Baltimore. Reading between the lines was not di fficult; his comments were not idle gossip and were not accidental. Two weeks later, I booked a flight, and Dot and I paid a weekend visit to the city of Baltimore, just to look around. We had never been there before.
We liked what we saw. Not unexpectedly, Harold showed up again about a month later and advanced the proposition that I transfer to
Baltimore as managing partner of the office. Having been favorably impressed with the city already, Dot and I said, “Let’s go for it!” So off we were—saying goodbye to friends and associates, and moving on to a new adventure.

## Principles

- Proactively seek counsel and ask questions to learn the local landscape.
- Invest personal resources—time and money—to build credibility and obtain leadership positions.
- Recruit and retain top talent as the foundation of a successful professional practice.
- When you don't understand a prospective client's business, decline the engagement to protect integrity.
- Act on ethical intuition promptly, even if it risks discomfort with peers.

## Decision heuristics

- When new to a community, directly solicit advice from well-connected locals and offer time or money to demonstrate commitment.
- If a client relationship requires trust, accept personal accountability to build deeper, strategic bonds.
- When answers to probing questions are weak and intuition signals deception, confront directly and escalate to governance.
- Prefer declining engagements you cannot reasonably understand over accepting suspicious-feeling retainers.
- Use community service and board participation to create durable professional networks.

# P1_S22 – KPMG/u2014.dBaltimore

# KPMG—Baltimore
**Story ID:** P1_S22
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1989–1991, Accounting / Professional services (KPMG), An insular, collegial office culture loyal to internal succession resisted external leadership and market-focused change, threatening client relationships and financial performance., Aggressive, Avoid groupthink and test internal assumptions aga, Engage clients at senior executive levels to prote, Make difficult personnel decisions when objective, Verify internal narratives by asking clients direc

## Summary

An external managing partner is brought into an insular, stagnating KPMG Baltimore office (1989–1991), uses aggressive external networking and decisive personnel actions to reposition the office and broaden client relationships before being reassigned to Philadelphia.

## Full Text

KPMG—Baltimore
Baltimore has been described as the largest small town in America and as the smallest large town in America. That characterization fits well. And for that reason it fit almost perfectly for us when KPMG
asked us to move there in 1989.
The Baltimore office had been an important cog in the KPMG
wheel for decades with fifteen partners and about 150 professionals.
The office serviced an impressive array of important clients who had been loyal to the firm for many years.
Over the years, the office had developed a rather insular personality, coupled with an exaggerated office collegiality. Most of the professionals in the office had begun their careers right there,
right out of college. That is to say that a large part of their day-to-day activity, other than routine servicing of existing clients, was focused on each other, not on the business community or strategically directing the practice. It was a brotherhood of sorts. Accordingly,
the growth of the office had stagnated, and the financial results were shriveling. The firm’s executive committee read this phenomenon correctly and reasoned that the office needed a leadership change—a leader from outside the practice. Meanwhile, Arch Fort, who had been the managing partner for twenty- five years, had recently reached retirement age and announced his departure from the firm.
The heir apparent to the managing partner position was Art
Mehlman, a fine man who had spent his entire career in the office.
However, the executive committee passed over him, to his extreme disappointment and that of most of the partners in the office. This was my first hurdle —winning over the partners who were deeply loyal and supportive of Art. I liked Art a lot, but knew that I had to rise above this hurdle quickly.
Our Baltimore experience was essentially an applied laboratory for both Dot and me. We took everything we had learned in Fort
Lauderdale, Orlando and Jackson and put it all to work for us. We hit the ground running. We le ft our mistakes behind in the closet and resolved not to duplicate them. In short, our life in Baltimore was a wonderful two years for us.
Although the office was a bit larger than Fort Lauderdale, I
sensed that I knew exactly what to do. Administration was a piece of cake. The KPMG ropes were as plain to me as the back of my hand. At first, I spent some time ge tting to know the partners and staff, but soon I also began hi tting the streets. It became clear that the partners were not much help on the streets, but I plodded on. I
was able to persuade a few partners to introduce me to some of the major clients, usually at the second-tier financial management level,
but managed to leverage a few of these contacts, as well as a few of my Florida contacts, for further introductions.
I also called a few key high-pro file business organizations in
Baltimore and asked if I could get involved —not unlike my calls to David Rush and Hank Watson in Fort Lauderdale years before.
Not surprising to me, but astonishing to our partners, most of them jumped at the o ffer. I made a call like this to the Greater Baltimore
Committee, the leading business organization. The next day I
received a call from Ben Civile tti, chairman of GBC, inviting me to lunch. Ben was a former U.S. attorney general and the high-pro file senior partner of one of the largest law firms in the city. He became a good business friend, and the next thing I knew I found myself with him, Chip Mason, chairman of Legg Mason, and Maryland
Governor Donald Shae ffer on a private plane on my way down to the Eastern Shore to look at a business development opportunity for the State of Maryland. Ben Civiletti quickly earned a spot on the long list of names in my personal Hall of Fame —people to whom I
owe enormous gratitude for helping me along the way.
Dot and I joined a senior Leadership Baltimore program that paved the way for some valuable relationships, and became involved in the United Way, a long-time stalwart for us. Along the way, I was appointed to the board of the Baltimore Museum of Art; I also joined the Center Club, a recognized melting pot for the business community, and began having lunch there regularly with local executives. Dot founded and led a terrific United Way organization,
called Friendly Baltimore, that engaged the spouses of transplanted
CEOs moving to the city.
For the most part, our partners’ contacts with our major clients were centered on the client’s chief financial officer or chief accountant. Partners’ contacts with other executives and their clients’ boards of directors were o ften extremely limited, which was uncomfortable for me. I was accustomed to dealing with the senior executives of our clients. One of our major clients was Johns
Hopkins University, a pillar of the community with a high-pro file
Board of T rustees. (Michael Bloomberg was chairman of the board!)
In 1990, they hired a new university president, Bill Richardson, the former provost at Penn State. Richardson was an elusive executive who was sharply focused on several key issues facing the university,
but I wanted to meet him. Our partners’ highest level of contact at
Johns Hopkins was the CFO; they said they could not arrange an introduction to Richardson. I mentioned this to Dot one evening at dinner, and she said, “Oh, I know his wife through Friendly Baltimore.
Let’s invite them to dinner. ” And so it went. The relationships we developed through aggressive networking paid off time and again.
One other client experience that is worth mentioning involves
RTKL Associates, an architectural and engineering firm with a stellar international reputation. KPMG had inherited the firm as a client in the Main Hurdman merger. With that heritage, RTKL was not a part of the office “club” , so to speak, and accordingly had been given short shrift in client service since the Main Hurdman merger. One of our managers was notified that RTKL planned to switch to another firm that promised better service. Again, our sole contact there was between a tax manager in our office and the controller at RTKL.
Our partners were resigned to lose the account. Not me. I inquired about the client’s leadership, picked up the phone and called Harold
Adams, its chairman and CEO. He was quite cordial and invited me over to his office to discuss the relationship. I came back from that meeting with a new contract —and a new friend. Harold Adams turned out to be a prince of a guy and a highly in fluential figure in the Baltimore business community. His wife, Janice, became one of Dot’s dearest friends. Harold was and still is on the board of directors of Legg Mason, as well as a number of other important organizations. And he was quite helpful to me. T oday, twenty- five years later, I still stay in touch with Harold. I have dinner with him in New Y ork about once a year before an annual conference we both usually attend. And I suspect RTKL might still be a KPMG client.
I also made it a point to get to know our competitors. This rankled most of our office partners. The mantra among the partners went something along the lines of, “We’re the greatest; our competitors stink!” It was a steady drumbeat, loud and repetitive. The partners literally despised the other accounting firms and used up incredible energy to ridicule and demonize them. The facts were plain, however;
they knew hardly anything about our competitors, except for the spurious office lore they themselves had engendered. This of course often led to miscalculations and tactical missteps that resulted in lost opportunities in competitive situations. I didn’t tumble for the lore. I proceeded to call the managing partners of the other five large national firms and invite each of them to lunch, much to the chagrin of my partners. The managing partners at the other firms jumped at the invitation, and through these luncheon meetings, I gathered an enormous amount of factual information, and in the process,
came to respect these people as fellow professionals. They were all aggressive business professionals of the highest quality, and were tightly integrated with their clientele and the business community in general. Not a demon among them! The acquired information served me well in numerous ways over the next two years.
During my second year in Baltimore, the leadership of KPMG
changed as a result of partnership elections. Jon Madonna and
Jim Brocksmith were elected chairman and deputy chairman, and they quickly began to restructure the management of the firm.
Geographic regions were completely rearranged. I was appointed the area partner for the newly formed mid-Atlantic region. With that appointment came a slot as a member of the firm’s Management
Committee. This placed me among the top twenty- five members of the KPMG partnership and bestowed signi ficant responsibility and authority. In addition to my position as managing partner of
Baltimore, I was suddenly responsible for Washington, D.C. and surrounding offices in Maryland and Virginia.
Shortly following the dramatic restructuring of the firm in 1990,
the KPMG Management Commi ttee embarked on a signi ficant downsizing of the partnership, agreeing on a 15 percent reduction in active partners. Each area partner was asked to identify the weakest links in his region and was assigned a proportional number of partners to be counseled out of the firm. I of course had my share of the outplacement strategy and proceeded to focus on three individuals in the Baltimore office. Attempting to be collaborative,
I invited four of the most senior local partners to my home on a
Saturday morning to discuss who should be outplaced. The first two were easy; they were former Main Hurdman partners and thus were anathema to most of the other partners —not legitimate members of the old boys’ club, so to speak. The third candidate
I proposed was Bob Fedo; I had found him to be an extremely non-productive partner with a very light client load and almost no contact outside the inner offices of KPMG Baltimore. The wrinkle was that he was a charter member of the “club” and dearly loved by the other partners. He was a sort of pinch hi tter for the office, fielding technical questions from others. He generally sat around the office kibitzing on all the audit work. From my point of view, he may as well have been the office water boy, worthless as a business professional. The other partners were unanimous in their support of their buddy and literally begged me to spare him from the downsizing. I listened to them politely and thought about their pleas for a while. I then decided to abandon my collaboration strategy. Early Monday morning, I stopped by Bob’s office and gave him the bad news. Interestingly, he did not seem at all astonished,
just surprised that his buddies had been unable to talk me out of it.
I mention this event for a number of reasons. First, I felt good about having the formidability to do the right thing in the face of considerable pressure from a group of mature professionals. Second,
the event established my posture as a leader. Despite the partners’
emotional heartache, they respected the decision-making. Third,
when Bob le ft, it was like removing your finger from a bucket of water and then wondering where the finger was; he was not missed at all, which proved the wisdom of the move. It was absolutely the right decision. Interestingly, over the next few years, countless
Baltimore partners recalled this seminal event and commented to me about how correct the decision was and how misguided their judgment had been.
Another interesting anecdote from that era is lodged in my memory. For years the office had a small satellite office in Columbia,
Maryland; Columbia was the model city developed by the Rouse
Company, and Rouse was a major real estate client. The office was manned by an idle receptionist and served only as a storage dumping place for supplies and outdated audit working papers; it was unused by anyone on our professional staff. When I proposed that we close the office and save the money we were spending on rent, salaries and utilities, the immediate reaction of the office partners was, “Oh,
no! The Columbia office is crucial to our image with the Rouse
Company. Closing it would be a slap in the face to their model city and severely damage our reputation. ” This sounded too much like the old boys club rhetoric, and I didn’t buy it. I placed a call to T ony Deering, the Rouse CEO, and told him I was considering closing our Columbia office as an e fficiency measure. He laughed over the phone and said, “What? Y ou’re telling me you have an office in Columbia? Why? And why are you asking me? Of course you should close it. That doesn’t make any sense at all. ” I actually had him on a speakerphone with two other partners in my office. They were dumbfounded. It was just another example of how a group of people can sit around in their insularity and talk each other into a ridiculous conclusion. This insularity, in my view, was the root of the office’s stagnation over the years.
My intent in these stories is not to disparage the Baltimore partners. They were all fine individuals with high professional standards and an impressive work ethic. They were technically competent and fundamentally good guys. I liked them all. However,
they had allowed themselves to drift away from the marketplace they served, and then rationalized among themselves that their modus operandi was a good thing. My intent is also to illustrate how so many of my previous experiences, along with lessons gleaned from my mentors, were now manifesting themselves in my capabilities and conduct as a more mature professional.
The two years in Baltimore flew by rapidly. In this short time, we were able to reposition the firm in the business community, solidify our relationships with the upper echelons of our major clients,
broaden the professional maturity of the partners and generally get the office back on a positive track.
From a personal point of view, Dot and I thoroughly enjoyed ourselves and were extremely happy in Baltimore. We have many fond memories of our days there and still have friends from that brief period in our lives.
Nevertheless, during our second year in Baltimore and my first months on the KPMG Management Commi ttee, hardly a month passed without a discussion of serious issues that the firm was facing in the Philadelphia office. The commi ttee proposed four or five potential candidates as managing partner replacements, but none of whom worked out, for one reason or another, after they had been vetted. Then, during a management committee meeting in Atlanta, I
ducked out for a pit stop in the men’s room. When I returned to the meeting, Jon Madonna said, “Keith, we’ve just had a little chat while you were out of the room, and we’ve decided that you’re the perfect guy for Philadelphia. ”
I was stunned. Me? I turned to my friend Harold Stokes and asked, “What do I say to this crazy idea?” His humorous retort has stuck with me over the years for some reason. He said, “My friend,
I think you may as well lie down on the track, because the train has already run over you!”
One of the ironies of this development was that Dot and I had taken a spin up to Philadelphia only a couple of weekends before,
just sightseeing and looking around. On the way home, driving past the oil depots and grubby industrial section of the city, Dot said,
“Wow, I don’t think I would ever want to live in a place like this. ”
One can only imagine how difficult my announcement was upon my return from Atlanta.
So, once again we pack up and move out on a new adventure.
Goodbye Friendly Baltimore!

## Principles

- Aggressive, external networking produces tangible business and community results.
- Avoid groupthink and test internal assumptions against external reality.
- Engage clients at senior executive levels to protect and grow major relationships.
- Make difficult personnel decisions when objective performance and client needs demand it.
- Verify internal narratives by asking clients directly before changing operations tied to perceived image.

## Decision heuristics

- When an office lacks senior client access, personally pursue introductions through your contacts and community organizations.
- If formal channels won't produce an introduction, use spouse or informal networks to arrange meetings with senior leaders.
- When a client relationship appears at risk, escalate directly to senior client leadership rather than accept lower-level reports.
- If consensus collaboration with peers is blocked by emotion or loyalty, be prepared to make an independent, defensible decision and act promptly.
- When competitors are being dismissed internally, meet their leaders to gather facts and reduce bias-driven tactical errors.

# P1_S23 – KPMG/u2014.dPhiladelphia

# KPMG—Philadelphia
**Story ID:** P1_S23
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Early 1990s (arrived August 1991; 1993 reorganization), Professional services / Accounting (KPMG), Fix a large, insular, resistant Philadelphia office and weak client relationships while implementing firm-wide compensation reform and balancing expanding regional/national responsibilities and a serious family health crisis., Use independent, Initiate relationships directly; don't let gatekee, Move decisively and rapidly when leading a turnaro, Leverage civic and philanthropic engagement to bui, Address systemic unfairness with iterative

## Summary

A KPMG leader arrives in Philadelphia (1991), confronts a resistant partner culture and weak client relationships, uses external assessment and direct relationship-building to turn the office around while managing firm-wide compensation reform and personal family health challenges.

## Full Text

KPMG—Philadelphia
We arrived in Philadelphia in August 1991. After an exhausting search for a home, we landed in Villanova in the storied Main Line area of the city. We purchased a beautiful home on Mt. Moro Road that was well-suited for all of our furnishings and belongings; they seemed to fit perfectly. We enrolled Mark at the nearby Shipley
School, Dot got busy with the house and the neighborhood, and I
went to work.
The Philadelphia office suffered from much the same maladies and sluggishness of Baltimore, except on steroids. It seemed as if that was my destiny these days, fixing problems in the firm. In addition to the Baltimore-like insularity, the partners in Philadelphia were a rambunctious and ornery group whose members loudly objected and pushed back on much that was going on within the firm. The managing partner, who had been ousted and forced into retirement just prior to my arrival, had been cut from the same cloth. Prior to his departure, he had fomented their open grumbling and rebellious mood. Some leaders inside the firm referred to him as the Pied Piper of Pennsylvania.
Philadelphia was one of KPMG’s larger offices—about thirty-five partners and over three hundred fifty sta ff members. It had a rich heritage that went back to the earliest days of the firm. Some of the brightest technical professionals in KPMG’s history had passed through there over the years. Like the partners in Baltimore,
the partners here were exceptionally competent technically. They basked in the notion that they were the smartest professionals in the city. At least that’s what they told each other—regularly. Because of their extreme inward focus and the time they devoted to grumbling and moaning, it was clear to me that they had lost most of their objectivity. And although the office served numerous major clients,
I would characterize many, or most of the relationships between clients and our partners as tenuous. I had my work cut out for me. But
I had the unanimous support of the firm’s management committee,
and I had the Baltimore experience in my back pocket.
So I plunged in with all of my energy. My game plan was much the same as in Baltimore—get to know the partners, meet the major clients and then hit the streets. The only difference was that, having learned from the past, I moved more aggressively. I made bold moves, and I made them rapidly.
It didn’t take long to see how difficult it would be to cut through all of the noise and accurately assess the Philadelphia partners’ skills.
Luckily, I stumbled upon the firm of RHR International, a group of corporate psychologists with a fifty-year reputation of counseling
Fortune 500 companies on executive talent. I hired RHR to conduct assessments of the key partners and assist me in coming to grips with the troublesome cultural issues. RHR was nothing short of terri fic.
Their insights were eye-opening. Their work and recommendations led to a sweeping realignment of responsibilities within the practice,
changes that quickly resulted in a ttitudinal adjustments and productivity enhancement. The mood changed almost overnight.
I can’t say enough about the value RHR brought to this KPMG
segment in my early days in Philadelphia.
Getting to know the major Philadelphia clients was a bit like a Baltimore makeover. The partners dragged their feet and procrastinated on making introductions. I soon realized that this sluggishness was due to the fact that they actually didn’t have relationships with the top level of their clients.
One dramatic example deserves mention. Our largest client was Rohm and Haas, a Fortune 500 chemical company. In the first few weeks, I gathered together three Philadelphia office partners to discuss our client service. When I suggested, among other things,
that we make arrangements for me to meet the Rohm and Haas
CEO, Larry Wilson, they said that first I had to meet the controller,
who was their primary contact point. Then, and only then, would it be appropriate for them to introduce me to the chief financial officer.
Protocol, you know. As for the CEO, they demurred, saying he was a chemical engineer and not really interested in “accounting stu ff” .
I challenged them on this point, thinking it unlikely, but they stuck to their guns, saying that Wilson wanted nothing to do with us; the truth of the matter was that they didn’t know him at all. Undaunted, I
tapped my phone and asked my secretary to place a call to his office,
and darned if she didn’t get him on the line. My partners cringed in their chairs, seemingly on the verge of nervous breakdowns one and all. I introduced myself over the phone as the new KPMG managing partner, and he said, “Y eah, I read about that in the newspaper. From
Baltimore, right? Welcome to Philadelphia!” I told him I’d like to meet him, and he immediately replied, “Hell, yes! I’d like to meet you too. ” He asked me a couple of questions about where I was living and then invited me to dinner with him and his wife that weekend.
The first time I met Larry was when he rang the doorbell at our home that Friday evening, followed by a nice dinner together at a Chinese restaurant in Villanova. From that day forward, Larry Wilson was a fine professional friend. He came to the next audit commi ttee meeting and never missed one afterwards.
In addition to making a friend, this experience underscored a valuable lesson: Never sell yourself short and never be bashful about establishing relationships. Also, never allow yourself to be a victim of immature palaver on the part of your own associates.
Not long afterwards, I went out with two partners for a luncheon meeting with another major client, Pete Musser, the chairman and CEO of Safeguard Scienti fic. Pete was a delightful, charming executive with an ingratiating manner. I remember with amusement that he brought his dog, an Irish se tter, to lunch and sat him next to his place at the table. As I was leaving, he said rather matter-of-factly,
“By the way, Keith, I need to get a $10,000 check from you. ” I’m sure
I looked puzzled. He added, “That’s for the United Way. A guy like you can’t be the managing partner of KPMG and not be a member of the Alexis de T ocqueville Society!” Immediately recalling Larry
Horner’s advice those early days in Fort Lauderdale, I sent him a check that afternoon. And thus began my twenty-plus-year journey with the Alexis de T ocqueville Society. A few days later, Pete called me and said he admired my reaction to his admi tted pushiness.
He said he had arranged for me to be appointed to the board of directors of the Alexis de T ocqueville Society. That move launched a series of new relationships for me in the city. I won’t forget that act of kindness. Pete also later arranged for my appointment to the
Philadelphia Boy Scout Council. What a guy! He is another member of my Hall of Fame.
Pete Musser reminds me of another valuable business fundamental: There are all sorts of quid pro quos in life, and they can contribute to success if one plays his cards smartly.
I found the Philadelphia business community to be friendly and welcoming. During my first month there, I received a visit from Ralph
Roberts, the distinguished chairman of Comcast Communications,
along with his son Brian. Comcast was not a client and there was no other official relationship with KPMG. The visit was merely a gesture of good will and welcoming to the city by a genteel gentleman from the old school. I also had the distinct impression that Ralph was conducting a relationship training exercise for Brian, who was about thirty years old at the time. Ralph passed away this year at age ninety-five and Brian of course is now chairman of Comcast, the largest communication company in the world. I have remembered this anecdote with fondness and have a ttempted to emulate Ralph
Roberts’ initiative on numerous occasions since. Shortly after my arrival in Philadelphia, a client executive stopped by my office and put the arm on me for a political contribution for Ed Rendell, a
Democratic candidate for mayor. I politely demurred, saying that I
rarely dabbled in politics and, moreover, had never met Ed Rendell and didn’t even know anything about him. Early the next morning,
my office phone rang. The voice on the other end said, “Keith, this is Ed Rendell. I understand you’re new to town and know nothing about me. I’d like to fix that. How about joining me for breakfast tomorrow morning?” I agreed and met Ed in the restaurant of the
Rittenhouse Hotel, overlooking a beautifully picturesque, snow-blanketed Ri ttenhouse Square. I felt a bit awkward at first, so I
explained my established abstinence from politics and how I was a Republican, anyway. He waved a dismissive hand and said, with his trademark broad, toothy smile, “Not to worry. I have plenty of campaign money anyway. I just wanted to meet you. ” We spent two hours together that morning in a wide-ranging discussion, seemingly hitting it o ff for some odd reason. Perhaps it was because we were both so plainspoken.
From that point forward, Ed always made a distinct point of speaking to me at functions or even walking along the streets.
Shortly after he was elected mayor, he personally called me again and asked me to serve on an elite management and productivity board he established to help curb city expenditures. I agreed, of course, and through this vehicle was able to meet many of the key movers and shakers of the Philadelphia establishment. Ed served two incredibly successful terms as mayor. He was fondly labeled
“ America’s Mayor” by the media. His popularity surged. He later went on to serve two terms as governor of Pennsylvania and then was chairman of the National Democratic Party. T oday, he is a frequent political and sports commentator on television. Every time I see him on TV , my mind races back to that initial meeting and I re flect on what an impressive individual he is. So genuine. Ed’s initiative in this interface taught me some other great lessons. One was to go out of your way to initiate relationships and then to take the time and make the effort to assure they are meaningful. I have used that technique liberally ever since that snowy, blustery morning on Ri ttenhouse
Square. Thank you, Ed Rendell.
The Philadelphia practice had three satellite offices—
Allentown, Harrisburg and Wilmington. They were outposts,
operating much like small-town CPA firms, but with all the liability of a huge international firm. These units consumed an inordinate amount of my time, as I tried to harness them and drag them kicking and screaming into the KPMG mainstream. I also had added Pennsylvania and Delaware to my regional responsibilities and was spending quite a bit of time on trains and planes overseeing those practices. Washington, D.C. was particularly onerous and burdensome, if for no other reason than it was simply Washington,
with all the a ttendant risks and issues. And then there were the monthly two-day meetings of the KPMG Management Committee,
held in venues scattered around the country.
On top of this activity, I was tagged by the management committee to spearhead a project to completely overhaul the partner compensation system for the entire firm. The income distribution system in place for a couple of decades had been loosely administered and reeked of favoritism; it was producing partner compensation that ranged over a wide spectrum of unfairness. It was the single most controversial subject within the KPMG partnership.
And just as controversial, of course, was any change to the system!
I plunged into this task with my handy-dandy Macintosh computer and proceeded to realign the partner salary and profit participation of the 1,500 partners. Following iteration after iteration, and revisions upon revisions, I finally arrived at a process and income allocation that was a dramatic improvement. The management commi ttee unanimously approved my recommendations and implemented the process immediately. This was an unbelievably difficult undertaking,
but I think it was probably the single best thing I ever did for KPMG
over my thirty-two-year career.
Considering all of these responsibilities and the desperate need to right the ship in Philadelphia, I was a very busy guy.
Our social life in Philadelphia was much di fferent than it was in any of the other places we had lived. Establishing meaningful relationships seemed more di fficult for some reason. Perhaps that phenomenon was related to the city simply being much larger than the other cities. Or maybe it was the reputed cliquishness of the Main
Line establishment. Whatever the root cause, we were undaunted.
We loved our picturesque home in Villanova and we used it liberally for entertainment.
In the midst of all the hustle and bustle, travel, long hours at work, parenting and social activity, Dot developed breast cancer.
What a sad day! But she had wonderful doctors and we treated this setback much as we handled everything else—aggressively. She had surgery almost immediately and then a course of radiation that lasted a couple of months. Despite our positive outlook, the psychological impact was as serious as the disease itself, it seemed. Only those who have faced the life threat that cancer portends can full understand the emotional struggle the victim endures. Dot struggled mightily,
but you’d never have recognized that from her upbeat, positive approach to life itself. What a trooper in the face of that adversity!
Then my professional environment changed dramatically once again in 1993. KPMG embarked on a massive reorganization with major changes to the traditional functional and geographic organizational structure of the firm. For the entire existence of the firm, the business had been managed in geographical units—cities—
and further structured along the lines of audit, tax and management consulting. Two years into their administration, Jon Madonna and
Jim Brocksmith pushed through a major restructuring that resulted in enormous cultural changes in the firm, touching every nook and cranny. In one of the biggest surprises of my business career, I was named national managing partner for the new financial services line of business, the largest of the five new national practice units. I was stunned. Where did that come from?
So, I unhitched my Philadelphia managing-partner and area managing-partner harnesses and signed onto two of the most challenging years of my entire career. As I was leaving the Philadelphia managing partner position to take on the national responsibility, the local partners arranged an elaborate dinner to pay tribute to my two years of leadership there. The highlight of the evening, for me, was the local partners’ presentation of the Philadelphia Bowl, a limited-edition china bowl manufactured in 1932 for Bailey Banks & Biddle.
Copies had normally been reserved for high-level dignitaries visiting the city —presidents, supreme court justices and the like. This priceless memento now sits prominently in my office.

## Principles

- Use independent, professional assessment to diagnose talent and culture when internal objectivity is compromised.
- Initiate relationships directly; don't let gatekeeping or protocol prevent access to senior client executives.
- Move decisively and rapidly when leading a turnaround; bold, timely action can accelerate change.
- Leverage civic and philanthropic engagement to build networks and open doors in a new community.
- Address systemic unfairness with iterative, data-driven redesigns to gain credible, implementable solutions.

## Decision heuristics

- When internal bias and noise obscure talent assessment, engage established external experts to provide objective evaluations.
- If local gatekeepers block access to senior stakeholders, contact the stakeholder directly to test assumptions and build the relationship.
- Prefer bold, rapid interventions over slow incrementalism in turnarounds: act quickly and iterate based on results.
- Use visible philanthropic or civic actions to create introductions and credibility in a new community.
- For politically sensitive, large-scale redesigns, iterate models repeatedly until you reach a defensible, improved allocation and seek unanimous governance approval.
