# P1_S26 – Corporate Boards and Consulting

# Corporate Boards and Consulting
**Story ID:** P1_S26
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Late 1990s through 2010s (post-1997 career), Financial services, corporate governance, consulting, education, Balancing opportunities to serve on boards and advise companies (with attendant reputational, time and financial commitments) against the risks of entrepreneurial failure, market forces outside one’s control, and ethical governance decisions that could require opposition or resignation., Judge founders and leaders by intelligence, Accept that external market forces can make outcom, Maintain professional conduct when disagreeing or, Use failures as teaching tools and case studies to, Leverage personal and professional networks to cre

## Summary

After leaving his executive role in 1997, the narrator built a third career as a corporate board member, consultant and adjunct professor, through a sequence of board appointments, entrepreneurial investments, consulting engagements and teaching. He experienced notable successes (Alliance Data, Fed board, consulting retainers) and failures (Laundromax), and learned governance, risk and professional lessons.

## Full Text

Corporate Boards and Consulting
When my Alamo gig came to an end in early 1997, I was fifty-six years old. For a short time, I mused about what I might do with the rest of my life. Then the phone began to ring, and I found myself gradually drifting into a third career. I soon learned that there was a demand for my services, and I slowly morphed into a professional corporate board member, strategic consultant and college professor.
My first call came from the Federal Reserve Bank. I was invited to join the board of the Miami branch of the Fed and served there for six years—two three-year terms—before being term limited out.
I never learned exactly how I was selected for this post. I asked the bank president who had called me and he said, “Beats me, I just got a call from Washington and was told that you’re the man!” I suspected it had something to do with my previous position as head of financial services for KPMG.
Without a doubt, the Federal Reserve Bank is the most professional organization I was ever associated with over the many years. Serving on its Miami board was one of the more enjoyable experiences of my entire career. I rubbed shoulders with some great people, got a practical education about economics and monetary policy and felt that I made a genuine contribution. I attended many meetings of the board at the Atlanta bank, as an emissary for the
Miami bank, and once attended a Board of Governors meeting in
Washington, D.C. I actually had dinner once with Alan Greenspan.
In addition to all of those positives, the board membership carried with it a distinctive cachet; I became recognized throughout the
South Florida business community as some sort of important super economics guru. This reputation was overblown, of course,
and undeserved. But I enjoyed it nevertheless. There are still some remnants of that celebrity hanging around today, more than ten years after I left the board.
I was also approached early on about joining the board of RHR
International, Inc., a well-known international consulting firm,
comprised of corporate psychologists, that served the Fortune 500
community. I had used the services of this firm with human resource challenges at both KPMG and Alamo, and was well familiar with their work. I was a friend of T om Saporito, one of its senior partners who had provided valuable counsel to me over the past few years.
T om recommended me to their board chairman. All of the partner/shareholders in this firm were psychologists. They were fantastic at what they did, but not particularly well trained in business ma tters; engaging a board of independent directors was a wise decision for them. I, on the other hand, had worked with consultants at KPMG for more than thirty years and knew that discipline through and through. For this reason, I think I made a signi ficant contribution to the operations of RHR over the ten years that I served on their board. The firm was a private company owned by the consultants and our board compensation was modest.
Nevertheless, I thoroughly enjoyed the experience.
Another early board experience was with a couple of young guys in South Florida who smoked me out of the woodwork in 1999 and asked me to sign up with a new mutual fund they were organizing. I met with them and was impressed with their enthusiasm and unbridled commitment. I especially was taken with the intellect and energy of David Zwick, their leader. I thought their idea was ambitious and was dubious about their ability to pull it o ff, particularly with their youth and inexperience. Think about it. Two young fellows in their early thirties trying to launch from scratch a highly regulated mutual fund specializing in federally mandated funding for low-income properties. How likely is that to be successful? However, I was intrigued with the concept for some reason and agreed to do it. So, I joined the board as an independent director of CRA Qualified Investment Fund, along with an eclectic assortment of other trustees. There were certainly challenges along the way for both management and the board, but the venture was quite successful. They are still alive today with about $2 billion in assets and a pristine reputation. I served on the board for several years before dropping off to take advantage of other opportunities.
Actually, I became weary of dealing with a few board members who, I felt, were being small-minded, and resigned to move on to something more interesting for me.
In the same general time frame, I was approached by two other young entrepreneurs, Alan Haig and Eric Lambert. They had a bold plan that I found intriguing. Both guys were about thirty years old and had excellent academic and professional backgrounds. Their idea was to build out six hundred modern coin laundries across the nation. I processed these guys through my tried and true formula for judging people —intelligence, energy and integrity —and they passed the test with flying colors. So, I agreed to invest in the start-up enterprise and serve on the board of directors of Laundromax.
Before long, I found myself as chairman. A fter two years of feverish activity, the business failed and the investors lost all of their money.
What went wrong? The founders were bright, capable professionals with unusual energy and drive. They had done extensive research and carefully modeled every aspect of the business before launching the venture. A highly experienced and diversified board of directors supported them. The directors all had their own money at stake.
Approximately eighty-five sophisticated investors had invested $17
million after studying the business plan. Banks were extending credit totaling $24 million. Among these groups, no one had questioned either the soundness of the business strategy or the progress of the concept development. In the end analysis, the answer was simple.
It was fundamental Economics and Financial Management 101.
The capital markets for entrepreneurial businesses completely dried up, quite suddenly, in the year 2000. We could not raise the money necessary to expand the business to the breakeven point.
The investors lost their entire investment —all $17 million! I had invested $400,000 and, of course, lost it all.
Laundromax was a collection of harsh lessons for me. One of those lessons was that some things in life are just not controllable, no matter how much effort is expended. Other lessons are documented in a business case study that I wrote at the request of the graduate
School of Business and Entrepreneurship at Nova Southeastern
University. Most university case studies pivot around stellar business successes. Nova Southeastern reasoned that their students needed to be schooled in failures as well. Over the last several years, I have given lectures on this case study to over four hundred Nova MBA
students. Alan Haig, one of the two young founders, has remained a close friend and has participated in a couple of those lectures.
My board experiences continued to diversify. My good friend
Ned Allen sold a mobile home property business in the late 1990s to Chateau Communities, Inc., a sizable real estate investment trust
(REIT) based in Denver. As a result of receiving stock in connection with the sale, he had a signi ficant investment in the company and was a member of its board of directors. Ned thought I could make a contribution and convinced the board to invite me to serve as an independent director. I agreed and immediately began working on audit committee issues there. It was my first experience as a director of a company listed on the New Y ork Stock Exchange (NYSE). The board membership was a relatively short-lived experience —about two and a half years. The REIT became the victim of a hostile takeover a ttempt by Sam Zell, a well-known corporate raider.
That unfortunate event put Chateau Communities in play, and we ultimately sold it to the State of Oregon pension plan. I enjoyed my short term on this board, especially all the shenanigans associated with the ultimate sale of the business.
One day in 2002, I received a breakfast invitation from Alan
Levan, chairman of BankAtlantic Bancorp, a $5 billion company that owned Bank Atlantic, a community bank with operations throughout South and Central Florida. I didn’t know Alan well.
He had been an investor in Laundromax and had served on the board with me there, though he had dropped o ff when the going got tough. Over breakfast, he told me he had admired my skills as a Laundromax director and particularly the way I had handled the wind-down (when he had ducked and run). He invited me to join the BankAtlantic Bancorp board. I agreed. A year later, I was also appointed to the board of BFC Financial, a related company. Both companies were listed on the NYSE at the time and were intertwined in their ownership of the bank, their primary asset.
I served on these boards for ten years and made some significant contributions along the way. As time passed, a weakening economy,
management issues and vexatious federal regulations made for a dismal scenario and declining fortunes. I became somewhat of a contrarian director as the bank ’s fortunes dwindled. The other directors seemed pathetically passive and generally agreeable with whatever Alan proposed. Despite his controlling nature, Alan seemed to appreciate my independence and respect my insights. The economic and regulatory pressures forced the sale of BankAtlantic in 2012 to BB&T . After the sale, both of the remaining companies were a mere fraction of their earlier size and not nearly as interesting or challenging for me. Moreover, I had a serious disagreement in early 2013 over what I thought was an outrageous $30 million executive compensation package proposed for the four top officers of the surviving companies. I voted against the compensation and then resigned as a director of both companies. I handled this disagreement and resignation professionally and without fanfare,
and I am still on friendly terms with Alan and other management.
In fact, at Alan’s suggestion, they agreed to pay me a $5,000/month retainer for eighteen months in case they might need my advice and counsel after I left the board. That’s good work if you can get it!
I served on several other boards along the way. Steve Swink,
a personal friend since childhood, got himself involved and wound up as the CEO of a delivery company in New York. The company was Dispatch Management Services Corp., a public company with some impressive logistics technology. At Steve’s request, I joined the board. I also persuaded two of my friends,
Ned Allen and Tom Saporito, to join me there. The business was troubled from the start, and Steve’s attempted turnaround was unsuccessful. Steve resigned, and then Ned, Tom and I all left the board ourselves. This was a fairly miserable experience for us all.
On a more positive side, I served for several years on the board of First Fleet Corp., a truck-leasing company privately owned by my friend and neighbor John Flynn. First Fleet was an interesting and complex business that we eventually sold to PHH Corporation (a
KPMG client when I was in Baltimore) for $30 million. I also served for two years on the board of Renaissance Cruises, a prominent cruise line headed by Ed Rudner, an old friend from my early Alamo
Rent A Car days. As a result of all these varied experiences, I learned a lot about corporate governance —valuable experience in today’s complex and litigious business society.
In about 2005, Craig Farlie and Mike Turner approached my good friend Jim Blosser and me about serving as a board of advisors for a new entrepreneurial company they were planning to launch.
We agreed and for the next three years served as a couple of gray-haired consultants as these two young guys embarked on their ambitious venture—starting a new investment banking firm from scratch. Farlie Turner & Co. has emerged as a highly successful middle market investment banking firm and is widely recognized as a quality service provider in the financial marketplace. Watching this success story take place from its embryonic stage to today, and being a part of its development in the early days, has been quite interesting.
The most signi ficant of all the boards I have worked with is
Alliance Data Systems Corp., an incredibly successful company operating in the private label credit card and data marketing space. The company ranks as one of the top five performers of all companies listed on the New Y ork Stock Exchange over the past ten years. Roger Ballou joined their board in about 2001. In
2004, the company sensed a need for additional board talent, and
Roger recommended me. A fter a lengthy interview process, I was appointed to its eight-member board and have served there for the past ten years. Without a doubt, this has been the most sophisticated assembly of individuals I have ever worked with. Every one of the board members is incredibly smart, works diligently, is ethical and behaves responsibly in all situations. It has been a pure pleasure for me. Moreover, it has been financially rewarding, as I have received a significant portion of my board fees in company stock, and the stock has increased eight-fold since I joined.
In the midst of all this corporate board activity, I have been called on for consulting services from time to time. I never sought out any of this activity, but it seemed as if every time I turned around, I saw a door opening.
In 2002, Roger Ballou had moved to Philadelphia as the CEO
of CDI Corporation. For several years CDI had owned Modern
Engineering, an automotive design company in Detroit serving the Big Three automotive companies with 1,200 engineers. CDI’s board began to reason that the business was not synergistic with
CDI’s other contract staffing business. CDI had been trying to sell the company for more than a year, without any success. Roger called and asked if I would take on a project of disposing of the subsidiary.
We worked out an agreement whereby I would be paid a per diem fee for my services with a success fee of $100,000 if I could sign a buyer within six months. I booked a flight and headed to Detroit to review the business. Fortuitously, on this first day of the assignment
I accidentally stumbled upon a guy who expressed a keen interest in acquiring the company. Just dumb luck! We hammered out the details over the next few weeks, and within six months the sale was closed. I never talked to a single other prospect! I of course collected my success fee.
Roger called me again, about three years later, with another problem. CDI’s external auditors had issued their audit report for the year 2004 and reported a material weakness in internal control.
This was in the early days of Sarbanes-Oxley regulations. “Material weaknesses” were considered extremely serious matters, both by the
Security Exchange Commission (SEC) and the company’s board of directors. Roger was disappointed in his CFO and accounting staff, and was frustrated to no end. He was reaching out to me to say, “Help!” I agreed and made several trips to Philadelphia to design remedial measures and monitor corrective action by the financial staff. I also served as an intermediary with CDI’s external auditors,
inasmuch as I had known the engagement partner Jerry Maginnis at
KPMG for many years. I had in fact nominated him for partnership in the firm more than ten years earlier —a convenient coincidence.
In this instance, both financial expertise and relationships combined to result in a happy ending.
Shortly after leaving Alamo, I was contacted by T om Shea, CEO
of Right Management Consultants, who wanted to engage me as an independent consultant. He and his wife, Maureen, jointly owned and operated the Florida/Caribbean franchise for Right Management.
We worked out an arrangement where I would meet with them for four hours each month and advise them on a wide range of matters.
They agreed to a monthly fee for these services. T oday, eighteen years and over two hundred meetings later, I am still serving them in this capacity—at the same fee. Over the course of those years, I have advised them on the complete panoply of their lives —all aspects of their business, their personal investments, managing their sons’
affairs as they matured, community involvement and a host of other matters. They consult with me on virtually every significant decision they make. It’s not that I have any special wisdom or insight. I don’t.
They simply desire an independent sounding board and a resource to test their judgment against. And they are willing to pay for that.
This has been a pleasurable experience for me (or else I wouldn’t do it). I think it has been productive for them as well (or else they wouldn’t do it.). T om and Maureen have becomevery special friends and people who I admire greatly. I like to think I have been helpful to them over the years.
From time to time, I have taken on a variety of other projects as a consultant. I did some work for Bear Sterns in New Y ork a few years back, advising them about the rental car industry, which had piqued their firm’s interest. I did some limited strategic consulting for a sizable interior design firm in Naples, Florida. I negotiated the acquisition of controlling interest in an insurance company in Pennsylvania for an old friend, who paid me with a 5 percent interest in the company. I did expert witness testimony on a couple of occasions. One other interesting project was the negotiation of a complex, high-pro file divorce se ttlement for Pam and Steve
Berrard. These and other projects have run the gamut of financial matters, industry expertise, negotiation and, in some cases, just plain common sense. All have been enlightening and rewarding for me personally.
The third leg of my post-Alamo career was the role of college professor. In about 2003, Randy Pohlman, dean of the business school at Nova Southeastern University, approached me about teaching in the graduate school as an adjunct professor. I really had no interest in such a thing and politely declined. I was later talking with
T om Tworoger, a long-time friend and a full-time professor there.
T om leaned on me hard and insisted that I just try it. He promised I
would love it. I consulted with Dot, who encouraged me. So I took the plunge and agreed to teach one course in Entrepreneurial Finance.
After one semester, I was hooked —just as T om had predicted. I
began teaching the same course twice a year and continued that for ten years. It was one of the more enjoyable things I ever did, much to my surprise. I loved it! I was at home in the classroom, delivering to a diverse cadre of students a combination of academic knowledge and practical experience from a lifetime of business activity. And diverse the students were! They came in all shapes and forms. Over the years,
I had two dentists, two pharmacists, a 747 pilot, a minister, an Army colonel, a Pratt & Whitney plant manager, a farmer, a Saudi prince—
you name it—all ages of men, women, many nationalities and ethnic groups. The common thread among this melting pot was a desire to own and operate one’s own business. Most were paying for the course with their own money (well, maybe not the Saudi prince!)
and were genuinely interested. Over the ten years, I taught over five hundred students. I received plaudits and excellent ratings from the students, and several have stayed in touch with me. A few have become good friends, and I have watched them become successful business people. I have now retired from teaching regularly, but have agreed to serve as a guest lecturer at Nova from time to time.
Throughout my career, I have been a staunch believer in continuing education to keep “sharpening my saw” , as Stephen Covey put it in his best-selling book, The Seven Habits of Highly E ffective
People. I have attended as well as led numerous KPMG seminars throughout the years; have attended several concentrated executive leadership programs at Harvard University, Stanford University and the Wharton School of Finance; have attended numerous corporate governance seminars and programs; have served on panels for
Corporate Boardroom programs; have attended NYSE-sponsored peer exchange programs; and have participated as an expert panelist on programs sponsored by the National Association of Corporate
Directors. Invariably, I have learned something every time I
participated in such programs.

## Principles

- Judge founders and leaders by intelligence, energy and integrity.
- Accept that external market forces can make outcomes unmanageable despite sound planning.
- Maintain professional conduct when disagreeing or exiting roles.
- Use failures as teaching tools and case studies to pass lessons to others.
- Leverage personal and professional networks to create opportunities and solutions.

## Decision heuristics

- Screen potential founders/partners for intelligence, energy and integrity before committing.
- When market liquidity disappears, recognize limits of control and stop assuming expansion will continue.
- If a compensation or governance decision conflicts with personal principles, vote your stance and be prepared to resign.
- Accept short-term, fee-based consulting assignments with a clear success metric and act quickly to exploit serendipitous leads.
- Serve as an independent sounding board for clients who want objective testing of judgment and be willing to maintain long-term retainer relationships.

---

# P1_S27 – Lessons on Business Ethics

# Lessons on Business Ethics
**Story ID:** P1_S27
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1960s–1990s, public accounting / auditing, Whether to investigate and escalate suspected financial misconduct despite professional, interpersonal, and client-related risks., Maintain professional skepticism and vigilance, Investigate anomalies thoroughly before concluding, Escalate material misstatements to appropriate aut, Value confidentiality when counseling individuals, Question the fairness of punitive actions and cons

## Summary

A career auditor recounts multiple instances of fraud and ethical breaches encountered over decades, describing investigative actions, escalation choices, and reflections on professional values.

## Full Text

Lessons on
Business Ethics
For some inexplicable reason, I have seen more than my fair share of fraud and corruption over the course of my career. Of course, the public accounting profession naturally exposes one to more than normal, but I somehow seem to have stumbled upon far more than most.
Fortunately, I was raised in an environment where the difference between right and wrong was clear. My parents, my school, my church, the Boy Scouts—all of them supported an admirable set of values. Again, fortunately, and unlike so many people, I never found myself in critical straits where I was severely tempted to breach the values I had been taught.
I provide this background not to imply that my values are better than average or be tter than anyone else’s for that ma tter. But I do believe that the background described keenly sensitized me to the mores of society and may have caused me to draw a brighter line when observing the behavior of others.
Also, the accounting profession trains one to be skeptical and vigilant. In fact, that’s what auditors really do: evaluate the integrity of financial information.
My observations on business ethics deal primarily with financial matters, inasmuch as that is the world in which I have mostly dwelled.
I have been less involved with behavioral standards and ethics in the areas of human resources, sales and marketing, production and other aspects of business. The following anecdotes illustrate some of the fraud and corruption that I have seen and had personal involvement with. Several are stories of cunning and ingenuity. Some are stories of desperation. Others are, I think, merely stories about people with fundamental character flaws.
My earliest recollection of an embezzlement dates back to the
1960s, when I was about twenty-four years old working for Peat
Marwick in Mississippi. One of my clients was a physicians group,
and I got a call one day from one of the doctors, Dr. Raymond Martin,
asking me to come out and take a look at things. He was concerned because the bank had been calling him about overdrafts. I went to his office and began reviewing the records with his bookkeeper, Billie
Pullen. There were some easily discernable discrepancies in the bank deposits. I asked Billie if she knew what might be wrong, and she said, “Sure, I know. ” Thinking she was going to rat on a nurse or someone else, I said, “Well, Billie, if you know, I think you should tell
Dr. Martin or me so we can get to the bo ttom of it. ” She looked me squarely in the eye, not flinching a muscle, and said, “Oh, he knows
I took the money —or he wouldn’t have you out here checking my books!” I was unsure how to react. As it turned out, she had stolen about $6,000. Dr. Martin called the police and they hauled her o ff to jail that morning. I was struck by the boldness of her actions,
and how calm and nonchalant she was about it. Apparently, her husband was not, because a newspaper article a couple of weeks later said that he had commi tted suicide out of humiliation and family embarrassment. We could never determine this woman’s motivations. Her family apparently was not suffering from financial pressure or hardship. Did she take the money because it was there and because she could? This unanswered question has haunted me for forty years. One thing the experience did for me was to set the stage for me to realize early on that fraud and corruption are real and one should be alert for it.
A couple of years later, I discovered another embezzlement.
This one was at Jackson Packing Company. While reconciling the bank accounts used for livestock purchases and flipping through a stack of cancelled checks, I happened to notice the recurring signature of Ham Stocke tt, the livestock buyer, as a secondary endorser on a number of checks. Curious, I went over and asked
Ham about it. He provided a perfectly logical explanation—at least it sounded perfectly logical at first. But I continued to have a nagging feeling, a sixth sense, that something was awry, so I flowcharted the number and weight of the cattle, from the buying lot to the kill floor,
for a month. I tried to reconcile the ca ttle yard purchases with the slaughterhouse numbers. Lo and behold, I discovered an elaborate scheme where the ca ttle buyer was manipulating weights and numbers and embezzling funds, something he had apparently been doing for years. I reported my findings to Ernie Jenkins, the ultra-tough-guy president of the company, who snorted, “Let’s get that bastard over here and see what he has to say!” Stocke tt arrived in a few minutes, confessed and was fired on the spot. Jenkins gave me no credit for uncovering the fraud; he just asked why it took me so long to find it! This was an early lesson about the old adage that “no good deed goes unpunished. ”
Along about the same time frame, I was auditing Alvis &
Company, a small municipal bond dealer in Jackson. I knew nothing about the investment business and found bond trading to be quite complicated. Julian Noel, a career manager at Peat Marwick, came to the office to provide some tutelage. While there, he picked up a stack of cancelled checks and thumbed through them. Suddenly he said,
“Hmmm, these checks feel funny. ” Upon close scrutiny, we observed that several checks were a bit shorter than the others by about a quarter of an inch. A fter digging around for some time, including ordering duplicates from the bank’s microfiche files, we uncovered another intricate scheme. The bookkeeper was preparing bogus checks with a customer and herself as joint payees (a normal practice in that trade). She would then cash the check herself, and when the bank statement and the processed check was returned (we still used paper at that time!), she would clip o ff her tiny endorsement on the back of the cancelled checks and forge the customer’s name as the endorser. This lady had pilfered a lot of money over a period of several years. Had it not been for Julian’s uncanny instincts and years of experience, I would never have noticed this embezzlement.
There were several other instances in those early years, each with its own set of peculiarities, but with a common thread of irregularity or dishonesty. I saw dummies on payrolls, employee self-dealing,
customer/supplier kickbacks, tax dodging and abuses in all shapes and forms. Sometimes the picture was clear, sometimes fuzzy.
Murray Underwood and I came across a $410 shortage in a surprise cash count at the Fine Brothers Madison department stores one year. The cashier’s name was Nora Cook and she had worked at the store for forty-six years. She was a very nice lady, so Murray and I went back later and recounted her fund, just to be sure. The result was the same, so we had no choice but to report the shortage to the president, Harold Madison, who called her in immediately.
Mrs. Cook had no explanation, so Madison fired her without any further investigation. I calculated that this shortage amounted to about $9 for every year this lady had worked. I still don’t know exactly what happened, but I have always questioned the fairness of the firing. I felt badly, but I didn’t know what to do other than report the findings. How was I to know he would be so arbitrary?
I have already chronicled the massive frauds I was involved with at Commonwealth Savings and Loan, Flowerland, and Sahlen and Associates. Each of these cases involved tens of millions of dollars. They all represented outright criminal behavior, but differed from the embezzlements described above, as they were deliberate schemes to defraud investors. I was deeply involved in each of them,
helping to root out the fraud and unravel the complex cover-ups. In each case, the perpetrators went to jail.
Being an auditor, I o ften found myself as the arbiter of ethical behavior in the reporting of financial information. I became accustomed to the stress this causes, but occasionally it became tough to handle. In the 1980s, we had a high- flying client called
Documation, Inc., based in Melbourne, Florida. Documation had developed the fastest impact printers in the U.S. marketplace and the business had taken o ff like a rocket ship. However, the li ft-off apparently was not fast enough for Ray Halbert, the CEO. Halbert began to aggressively push the envelope in the company’s quarterly financial releases. Before long, he was orchestrating deceptive practices and reporting financial information that was materially misleading, in my view. During the annual audit, our team came across several bogus transactions that signi ficantly distorted the company’s reported results. The transactions involved fictitious sales to fictitious customers. I worked for days trying to get them to adjust the numbers, but Ray staunchly refused. So, I attended a meeting of Documation’s board of directors and presented our findings. I
spilled the beans, so to speak. I will never forget Halbert’s reaction.
He stood up at the end of the long mahogany conference table and snorted, “Gentleman, this man is a lying son of a bitch! As a board you have a decision to make. Are you going to believe me or this itinerant numbers geek?” I froze in my seat. I cautiously gazed around the table to judge the reaction of the solemn board members. They stepped out of the room for a while and then returned. A designated leader told Halbert, “Ray, we have made a decision on the challenge you posed. We have opted to believe Peat Marwick. Y ou are hereby terminated as CEO of the company, and we’re asking you to leave the premises immediately. We also recommend you secure personal counsel, because you’re going to need it. ”
There was an interesting sequel to the Documation story.
Shortly after that haunting board meeting, I received a call from Dick
T esta, who was appointed chairman of the board following Halbert’s termination. Dick was the senior partner of a major Boston law firm and also on the board of T extron, a major technology company.
He advised me that the Documation board had voted to terminate
Peat Marwick’s services. I was flabbergasted. Here I had saved the company from perpetrating a massive fraud on the marketplace,
and this was my reward? Dick acknowledged the unfairness of the decision, but implied that they were acting out of a perceived need for some kind of legal protection. I didn’t get it, but the decision was irrevocable. Then Dick called me aside and said in hushed tones,
“Keith, I know this isn’t fair, but I’ll make it up to you. That’s a promise. ” I swallowed hard and thought to myself, “Y eah, yeah. Sure you will!” Then, about four years later, out of the blue, I received a phone call from Dick T esta. It was a short call. Just a few minutes. He said, “Keith, do you remember a private promise I made to you a few years ago? Well, I’m calling to deliver today. ” He had joined the board of a major company in New Y ork and had persuaded them to change auditors from Price Waterhouse to Peat Marwick. No proposal, no interviews, no fee haggling, nothing. His only request was for me to provide him the name of a partner in New Y ork to contact. Now,
there is a positive case of business ethics. An honorable man, living up to his word!
The patterns of ethical and unethical behavior I observed in the business world remained fairly static over the fifty years of my career.
The numbers just got bigger.
Not long after arriving in Philadelphia in 1991, I met the managing partner of one of the major law firms in the city. We seemed to hit it off and soon became friends, having lunch together from time to time and discussing business and political affairs. After about a year, I received an urgent call from him asking me to meet with him on an extremely con fidential matter. I o ffered to go over to his office and he said, “No, no. Can you meet me in the next half hour at a park bench on Ri ttenhouse Square?” I agreed and found myself si tting with him —in heavy overcoats —on a cold blustery day in a deserted downtown park, evocative of some spy movie.
He began to lay out the most bizarre scenario I had ever heard. He had learned that one of his senior partners had been laundering fraudulent funds through the law firm for about three years and had been skimming personal cuts for himself along the way. This man said he had accidentally stumbled upon the scheme and was the only individual in the firm who had any knowledge of it, as far as he knew.
The dollars in this case were enormous. Moreover, the protagonists were prominent individuals with high-pro file national reputations
(hence the vagueness in this paragraph more than twenty years later,
including withholding the attorney’s name). There was not much for me to do except listen to the story and provide some commentary.
The attorney wanted to talk con fidentially with someone he trusted, as he was trying to decide how to react and still protect the reputation of his prestigious law firm. We agreed that he should personally contact the U.S. Attorney General and describe what he knew. He made the phone call right then and there. He and I then walked directly to the train station, several blocks away, where he caught the next train to Washington, D.C. We never spoke of the event again, except for laconic periodic expressions of appreciation for my confidential counsel. I learned through this unusual situation that fraud has no ceiling and no boundaries. I also came to recognize how good it feels to know that a friend trusts you enough to confide something as large and sensitive as this case.
On a Saturday afternoon in 1994, I received a call from Bill
Hannon, our financial services leader in New Y ork, advising me of a large trading loss at Kidder Peabody (Kidder Peabody, a prominent investment banking firm, was our client; it was owned by GE
Capital). Kidder Peabody had called an emergency meeting for
Monday, and Bill asked me to join him there. So, bright and early on Monday morning, I found myself si tting across the table from an irate Jack Welch, chairman of General Electric, and a livid Gary
Wendt, CEO of GE Capital. A rogue trader from Kidder Peabody named Joe Jett, a clever, highly educated guy, had found a loophole in Kidder’s computer systems and had covered $200 million in trading losses with bogus profitable trades, thereby producing huge bonuses for himself. It was the largest investment banking fraud in
U.S. history. The case received intense scrutiny by all levels of GE
and KPMG, and the media a ttention seemed never-ending. An internal investigation evolved into a highly publicized document called the “Lynch Report” . The scandal also found its way into Jack
Welch’s autobiography, Straight from the Gut . It’s one of the classic stories in the annals of American business. I was there and saw it unfold as it emerged.
I have often wondered how I became witness to so many ethical aberrations. Or were they aberrations at all? I was never one to turn a blind eye to problems—does that explain my proximity to so many cases? I have seen literally dozens of serious ethical breakdowns over the years and have worked with numerous clients to try to determine the root causes of aberrant behavior and get to the bottom of internal control issues; maybe I have developed a reputation for that. Over the years, I seem to have developed an unusual sensitivity to, and discernment about, abnormal conduct, perhaps honed and developed by working with these cases.
And these peculiar instincts continue to this day. Recently, a neighbor across the street from my home was fined $6 million and sentenced to house arrest for harboring illegal Swiss bank accounts.
T o be sure, I had nothing to do with this case or the detection of it.
However, I did have a sixth sense about this guy just from talking with him in the street. I had o ften idly wondered what he was up to and had entertained the notion that it was something no good. Why was that? I’m still wondering.
The opening lines of Edgar Allen Poe’s The Raven begins, “Once upon a midnight dreary, while I pondered, weak and weary… ” . I
suppose I’ll keep pondering about business ethics, while keeping eyes open and ears perked for signs of problems out there.

## Principles

- Maintain professional skepticism and vigilance.
- Investigate anomalies thoroughly before concluding.
- Escalate material misstatements to appropriate authority when management resists correction.
- Value confidentiality when counseling individuals facing sensitive disclosures.
- Question the fairness of punitive actions and consider proportionality.

## Decision heuristics

- When numeric or transactional anomalies appear, map the transaction flow end-to-end to reconcile quantities and identify manipulation.
- If physical documents show inconsistencies, obtain bank duplicates or originals to compare endorsements and verify authenticity.
- Persist in requesting management adjustments; if refused, escalate the issue to the board for resolution.
- When a trusted colleague discloses criminal conduct, advise immediate contact with the appropriate legal authority.
- Do not ignore small recurring discrepancies; even modest shortages can indicate long-term or systemic abuse.

---

# P1_S28 – Career Summary

# Career Summary
**Story ID:** P1_S28
**Part:** One
**Tags:** Career spanning decades; references 1993, Accounting / Professional Services and Corporate Leadership, Leaving KPMG to assume leadership of a major company whose problems were not his making, requiring rapid, effective action to resolve them and justify the appointment., Acknowledge and be grateful for institutional deve, Take ownership of problems you inherit and treat t, Act decisively and swiftly when circumstances dema, Leverage broad institutional experience and knowle, Value the career-wide accumulation of experiences

## Summary

A former KPMG partner reflects on a long career, gratitude to the firm, and his transition to lead a major company where he swiftly addressed inherited problems and succeeded.

## Full Text

Career Summary
KPMG was good to me. This terri fic firm took me in, trained me and provided me with unparalleled opportunity to see the U.S.
business community from myriad perspectives. The firm imposed itself on me with the force of its rich history and professionalism. It molded me into a true professional, in every sense of the word. Y ear upon year, KPMG burnished my reputation and took me to places my wildest dreams had never imagined. In the end, it was indeed a lofty perch. I never asked for a raise. I never asked for a promotion.
I never even asked for an assignment. It was all handed to me on a platter. Rising above the hard work and considerable stresses over the years, is admiration for a venerable institution that took a boy out of the country to the pinnacle of American business. Along the way, I had the privilege of working with some incredible people —
the best of the best. In fact, two partners who worked for me went on to later become chairman and CEO of KPMG—Gene O’Kelly and
John Veihmeyer. I am honored and will be forever grateful for the generous experience provided by the firm.
My Alamo experience was truly unique. As I was leaving
KPMG, Chairman Jon Madonna remarked that he could not name another person who had le ft the accounting profession to assume such a lo fty position as CEO of a major company. I was indeed fortunate to have this opportunity. In a sense, however, I think I was bred for the job. I knew the company and its management well, and my breadth of knowledge and experience were in critical demand there. Moreover, I was unshackled. The company’s issues were not of my making; they were mine to solve. And I worked with blistering speed and decisiveness to attack them. Thankfully, it all worked out and we were successful. As with KPMG, I am deeply grateful for this opportunity.
I can think of no better way for someone to wrap up a successful career than to do what I have done for the past nearly twenty years.
I have reached into my personal reservoir of unique experience and been able apply some of my knowledge to corporate boardrooms,
consulting relationships and the university classroom. I have been extremely well compensated for some of these activities. Some have been not so financially lucrative, but I would have done many of them just for the enjoyable experience. It’s all been rewarding.
As I re flect on my career in its entirety, I am reminded of an excerpt from the lyrics of “ A Whole New World” , the song from
Aladdin that I used in that di fficult presentation back in Orlando in
1993. In that song, Aladdin sings, “I can open your eyes, T ake you wonder by wonder, Over, sideways and under, On a magic carpet ride. ” I truly feel as though I’ve been on a magic carpet ride, and I’m truly grateful for the lift!
PART THREE

## Principles

- Acknowledge and be grateful for institutional development and mentorship.
- Take ownership of problems you inherit and treat them as yours to solve.
- Act decisively and swiftly when circumstances demand urgency.
- Leverage broad institutional experience and knowledge when stepping into leadership roles.
- Value the career-wide accumulation of experiences and give back via boards, consulting, and teaching.

## Decision heuristics

- When inheriting organizational problems, accept ownership and prioritize fast, decisive action to stabilize the situation.
- Use deep institutional knowledge and professional training as a primary resource when leading unfamiliar or struggling organizations.
- Maintain gratitude and recognize mentorship as part of leadership legacy and relationship-building.

---

# P1_S29 – What a Wonderful World

# What a Wonderful World
**Story ID:** P1_S29
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1970s–2014, Intellectual or vicarious knowledge versus the deeper understanding gained through first-hand, on-site experience ('seeing is believing')., First-hand observation provides deeper understandi, Independent, Hands-on participation, even if small, Physical mementos and local craftsmanship connect

## Summary

A first-person travelogue reflecting on decades of domestic and international travel, arguing that first-hand observation yields deeper understanding than second-hand accounts and recounting diverse travel experiences and a humanitarian trip.

## Full Text

What a Wonderful
World
As I stood a few years ago in the doorways of cold and desolate barracks at Auschwitz and Dachau concentration camps in Poland and Germany, sharp tingles shot up my spine. I knew the horrid stories and could describe the circumstances intellectually. But honestly, I didn’t really know the stories. As the expression goes,
seeing is believing. Only by walking through those gruesome venues can one actually truly understand the Holocaust that occurred there more than seventy years ago. Only then could I wrap my head around the plight of those six million Jews and what that all means to society today.
So goes the story of my extensive travels over the years. Seeing has been believing —and understanding. First-hand observation has brought our complex world into a sharper focus for me. I am immensely fortunate to have had those first-hand opportunities.
Dot and I have travelled throughout our adult lives. We started early on with domestic vacation trips, primarily exploring the southern United States, close to our home. We gradually expanded our national reach, ultimately accomplishing a goal of placing our feet on the ground in all fifty states.
The last leg of this protracted domestic expedition was an impromptu visit in 2012 to our last two remaining states to visit —
Nebraska and Iowa. While visiting our son Mark in Denver, we spontaneously changed our travel plans. We rented a car and struck out on this diversion. Along the way, as we gazed over the beautiful western plains, we decided to play a road trip game, just for the fun of it. We challenged each other to describe a speci fic memory from each state we had visited. We both performed that feat without a blip as the miles rolled by, recalling years of experiences as if watching a television travelogue.
Wonderful travel memories have lingered with us over all these years. On this short visit, they were as vivid as the sun on a bright summer day. Our conversation became increasingly more fascinating as the recollections poured out. We described the scores of national parks we have visited, from Acadia in Maine to Y ellowstone in
Wyoming to Carlsbad Caverns in New Mexico. We reminisced about Christmas with our family at Williamsburg and memorable
July 4th celebrations in Manchester, Vermont and Nantucket. A
road trip around the perimeters of the Great Lakes was especially unforgettable. We later made another two-week road trip out west,
where we meticulously followed the trail of the 1804–1806 Lewis and Clark Expedition from Bismarck, North Dakota over the Rocky
Mountains to the Pacific Ocean at Portland, Oregon. We recollected our exciting experiences in many of the great cities of America —
New Y ork, Chicago, San Francisco, Boston, Washington, D.C., et al.
We talked about great hotels and quaint bed and breakfast inns we have visited throughout the country. We talked about visits to our children in all the places they have lived (Arizona, T exas, Arkansas,
New Jersey, Illinois, Colorado, Virginia and Washington, D.C.)
as well as all the states where we have lived (Mississippi, Florida,
Maryland and Pennsylvania). We’ve seen so much. We’ve travelled by air, by car, by motor home and by boat, and we’ve seen the mountains, the prairies, the rivers and rapids, the lakes, the parks,
the forests, wine country, nature’s oddities, the cities, the farms, the monuments and shrines, and the highways and byways from sea to shining sea.
With these panoptic travels, we have sensed the rhythm and vibrancy of our country and have come to better appreciate its history and its grandeur. No amount of reading Fodor’s travel guides or taking formal educational courses could have provided a substitute for these learning experiences. Not even National Geographic magazine could reproduce what we have seen and experienced. We thoroughly understand why the United States is the greatest country in the world, largely because we’ve seen it up close and personal with our own eyes. We have touched it and felt it. We have breathed its air from corner to corner. It’s an old friend. We feel like we know it, and know it well. And we like what we have seen.
A similar thing could be said about our international travels, but in a slightly different context. We have now travelled to over seventy-five countries, crossing all seven continents. We rounded out our continental bucket list in 2014 with a memorable expedition to
Antarctica.
Our first international trip was to Switzerland and Austria in the
1970s. I’m not sure why we picked these countries as a starting place for our international wanderlust. But for whatever reason, we laid out our travel plans without the aid of a travel agent (and well before the Internet) and struck out on a two-week journey to Europe,
leaving our children behind with a reliable caretaker. Looking back,
it was a rather daring move for a young couple recently relocated from Mississippi and with virtually no experience in the wily ways of the world. It was an incredible trip, with vivid experiences that are still ingrained in our memories. We visited all the major cities of these two countries and also took a trip to idyllic Zerma tt,
Switzerland, in the Alps. Among our favorite memories are seeing the Ma tterhorn, Oberammergau (in Bavaria, Germany, another side trip), visiting Mozart’s birthplace in Salzburg and a ttending a thrilling performance in the Grand Opera House at Vienna. Dot still has some jewelry that we purchased in Geneva, and some forty years later I’m still telling tall tales about that remarkable adventure.
That first excursion outside the United States whe tted our appetite for seeing more of the world. It ignited a fire within us for the next four decades. We would read or hear about a place or event and say to each other, “Let’s go!”
Another early trip was to China. My interest was piqued while reading about President Richard Nixon’s visit to China in 1972,
breaking down international barriers and normalizing relations with the outside world. I read the reports and mused, “I would like to see that for myself. ” Not too long after that, we booked a cruise with
Norwegian Cruise Lines and visited Beijing, Shanghai and a number of smaller cities and locales along the eastern seaboard. What an eye opener for us! We stood on the Great Wall in total amazement,
wandered through the Forbidden City, visited a common working family in their modest apartment in Dalian, saw thousands of terracotta soldiers at Xian, ate the crummy food and gawked at the millions of bicycles (only to be gawked at in return by the Chinese people). It was truly a world that we had never imagined. Until we saw all of these things with our own eyes, China had been a veritable dreamy fairytale to us. Not actually real. But now, suddenly, we had the picture in vivid T echnicolor. It was indeed real. And tangible.
And fascinating, to say the least!
Exactly twenty years later, we made a return trip and were equally amazed. The changes in the country were incredible beyond description. Glass and steel skyscrapers had magically replaced the old terracotta brick buildings in Beijing. Shanghai looked like Dallas on steroids, with buildings that were even bigger, taller, shinier and more luxurious. Cars, trucks and busses jammed the overcrowded streets. The bicycles were nowhere to be seen. High technology abounded and the public infrastructure was remarkably advanced.
Most people who interfaced with tourists spoke English—excellent
English. All of the school children even seemed to speak English.
What an amazing transformation in twenty short years! Despite all we could read about China, we would never have understood and appreciated the progress and momentum occurring there without these two polarizing visits. The indelible impressions will be with us forever.
In stark contrast, there are other wonderful places in the world that have not been tarnished by such progress and modern development. They have stood virtually unchanged for centuries.
Seeing those places and standing in their shadows caused history to come alive for me. Examples of these timeless places are the pyramids of Egypt, Machu Picchu in Peru, Angkor Wat in Cambodia and Petra in Jordan.
They are venues that originated thousands of years ago and where time has seemingly stood still. As I stood before these and other great monuments to human ingenuity, I scratched my head in bewilderment. How did this come to be? Why? How did such beauty and scale develop without the aid of modern equipment and technology? Who was responsible for leading such developments?
How did they do it? How many lives were sacrificed in the endeavor?
How has society managed to preserve it? And so on with an endless series of unanswered questions. Somehow history texts and colorful coffee table books do not capture the answers to these questions.
Nor do television documentaries. Although answers to my nagging questions were not always forthcoming, just being there in the midst of such history left deep impressions.
Dot and I are o ften asked about the best place we have ever visited. Both our answers are always the same, without hesitation:
Israel. We have been there twice —once on a two-week religious/
cultural tour and another on a three-day trip, where we were just passing through and enjoying the countryside. A fter spending a virtual lifetime thinking I was learning about the land where God planted his seed, it was only by going there and visiting the sites did it actually begin to take on a real and physical meaning. It is hard to imagine an agnostic spending any time there and not scratching his or her head at the wonder of all the imbedded history. The ground is hallowed; there can be no doubt about it. There is no other place on earth like Israel.
Much of our worldwide travel has been independently arranged by us or spent on cruise ships. Organized tour groups have never appealed to us, due to what we see as con fining rigidity and structure. We have found cruising to have a lot of advantages. The accommodations are always comfortable, and one covers a lot of
“ground” with little itinerary management and luggage schlepping.
As has been said, you check in and the destinations come to you!
We have travelled from time to time with friends, sometimes with mixed results. We have done several cruises with the Weils —
Sherra and Michael —our terri fic friends and marvelous travelling companions. Like us, they enjoy flexibility and independence when exploring the world. Who could ever ask for two more compatible and fun people with whom to share the sites of the world?
Our cruises have taken us to Scandinavia, the Mediterranean,
Europe, South America, China, Southeast Asia, Africa, Alaska and of course various venues in the Caribbean. We’ve travelled with Crystal
Cruises, Silversea Cruises, Royal Caribbean, Princess Cruise Lines and other minor lines. They’re all great! We have strolled the streets of virtually every signi ficant city in Europe over the years, enjoying the special charm and character of each. My personal favorites have included the usual high-pro file venues —Paris, Rome, Florence,
Madrid, Vienna, et al. But just as interesting and educational have been some locales that are not as famous, among them Hamburg,
Munich, Geneva, Krakow, Budapest, Lisbon, Copenhagen, Oslo and Brussels. We spent five weeks in Italy in 2014, roaming Tuscany and the Lake Como areas and enjoying the marvelous Italian lifestyle. Europe is truly a special part of the world. So rich in history and culture. So much to learn. We have enjoyed the architecture and scenery, wonderful museums, great food, unusual customs and charming people. With all that said, I do believe we have seen enough Catholic churches to last us for the rest of our lives!
Michael and Sherra Weil, the world’s best travelling buddies
We have also visited several quaint, o ff-the-beaten-path places all over the world. Examples would be Estonia in the Baltic, the
Seychelles Islands in the Indian Ocean, San Miguel de Allende,
buried in the highlands of central Mexico, and T ogo on the west coast of Africa.
One of my choice experiences was a casual two-week trip to
Ireland and Scotland. Dot and I rented a car in Dublin and spent ten days meandering around the countryside with no pre-established plans other than to implant ourselves in the everyday lives of the Irish. We navigated the narrow roads from village to village,
spending our days enjoying the scenic beauty of the emerald countryside and its people. Most of our evenings were spent in small bed and breakfast inns that we booked day by day as we moved along the byways. On a couple of occasions, we stayed in old converted castles. We o ften stopped on the roadside and talked with local people and wandered into classic Irish pubs and cha tted with the customers, actually experiencing first-hand the conviviality and gift of gab for which the Irish are so famous. The experience was authentic, in every sense of the word. Real people living real lives.
Unpretentious and genuine. We le ft Ireland on a high note and then migrated over to Scotland to do a similar thing. The climate and atmosphere in Scotland were different, but we felt the same way about the culture. We gained a new appreciation for these people—
their history, their spirit, their attitudes, essentially their love of life.
One doesn’t learn about such things by staying at home.
And then there have been several ventures with just the guys—
simply fun trips. Many of these were deep sea fishing expeditions,
some productive and some not so successful. I’ve owned several boats over the years and have fished the waters of South Florida and the Bahamas. My good friend Pat Kelley and I signed up for a big fishing tournament in Bimini in 1982. We chartered the Happy
Day T oday, out of Fort Lauderdale, with a captain and mate and got ourselves all fitted out for the occasion. We even had a writer and photographer from World Magazine on board. On the last afternoon of three days’ fishing, we hooked up on the big one —a huge blue marlin estimated at six hundred pounds. Massive jaws lurched out of the ocean just behind our boat and gobbled a large red artificial lure.
Pandemonium set in immediately. Our large Penn 80 reel squealed away as the giant marlin spun the line almost down to the core. After battling for about a half hour, our line mysteriously snapped and we lost it. It was a huge disappointment. Aside from the thrill of it all,
we would have won the $15,000 grand prize in the tournament. But
I have some fabulous photo memoirs of the event, including several with our giant marlin’s tail dancing in the ocean!
I have also made several trips to Costa Rica, fishing the bountiful waters of the Paci fic. My son Mark and I went there together once and had an incredible experience. We landed several sail fish and rare roosterfish. One day, fishing in a dilapidated twenty- five-foot charter boat, we hooked a 450-pound black marlin. We fought it together for two and a half hours, finally bringing it alongside our boat to share in the exhaustion. We released him, of course. What an incredible moment—both catching and releasing this giant animal!
Those were the days! I have now sold my last boat and fish only occasionally these days.
A short domestic trip in 2013 has added even additional spice to the diversity of my travels. The trip was short —five days—but the experience was up close and personal. I joined a group of twelve people from our church on a humanitarian expedition to Moore,
Oklahoma to assist in the disaster cleanup following a record-shattering tornado. There I came face-to-face with the incredible destruction that nature’s wrath can bring on a community.
Unfathomable devastation. At the same time, I witnessed the faith,
determination, generosity and loving spirit of the American people.
All in one place. A local church transformed itself overnight into a disaster recovery center, and people miraculously appeared from all over the United States with helping hands and generous donations.
Our group rebuilt a fence and a front porch, and collected tons of debris from destroyed neighborhoods. Relative to the magnitude of the ruin, the products of our labor were miniscule. However, we felt wonderful knowing that we were able to help, if only in a small way.
I had seen all of the television coverage of the ravages of the tornado.
But…the point of including this trip in my travelogue is to once again underscore the fact that no amount of description or news coverage is a fitting substitute for being there live and in person.
There is a colloquial expression in the business world called
“MBWA”—management by wandering around. This, of course,
refers to knowledge that comes from first-hand observance of a business operation as contrasted to memos, meetings and manuals.
That thought captures much of what Dot and I have experienced in our travels over the years. We have learned so much about our world simply by wandering around, stumbling about in nooks and crannies everywhere, and absorbing what we saw. It has allowed us to be connected to our wonderful country and the world in ways we never could have achieved if we had remained closeted in our cocoon. We have witnessed the trappings of the rich and the poor of the world (e.g., the Palace of Versailles contrasted with the slums of Cairo) and we have seen the spectacular dichotomy of the old and the new (e.g., the ancient temples of Cambodia’s Angkor Wat vs. glistening modern Singapore —not so far apart, these two). We have gazed in wonder at the grandeur of the natural and the man-made (e.g., the Grand Canyon and Mount Rushmore). We’ve seen mountains, rivers, canyons, oceans, beaches, forests, deserts and glaciers. Y ou name it; it seems as though we’ve seen it.
Along the way, we have carted home a lot of stu ff. Most of it we have classi fied in the category of “J&S” (you can guess what that stands for) and it has gradually disappeared. But a few things remain with us as prize remembrances of our travels and tangible linkages to the places we have been. We have a set of two beautiful white marble fish on our patio that we purchased spontaneously in a marble quarry in Vietnam. I stood in the hot sun and watched a frail old Vietnamese man toiling away on the fish with a crude hammer and chisel. I was moved both by the beauty of the fish and the raw talent of this old man. We cherish an incredible piece of Chinese embroidery that hangs above our grand piano; it is a meticulous copy of a famous Chen Yifei oil painting, and reportedly took a Chinese woman twenty-two months to weave. These mementos remind us of the talents and skills that are tucked away in remote places, places we don’t always think of with respect. They serve to keep us connected to our experiences. I’m not sure why I ever thought I needed a didgeridoo carved and painted by the Aborigines in Australia, but I
have one sitting in my study!
So, where do we go from here? There are still a number of choice places on our list. These include Brazil and India, the two largest countries that we have not visited. Both have a lot to offer in education and enchantment, and we’ll probably make it to both of them sometime in the future. I also have a hankering to revisit some of our earlier destinations, such as Switzerland and Austria. Other than London, where we’ve been several times, we haven’t been to many places more than once. We think it might be time for a few revisits.
Of all the things we’ve experienced in our more than fifty years of marriage —places we have lived, people we have known, jobs,
organizations, trials and tribulations, etc.—our travels are among the most memorable and rewarding experiences, providing insights into our world and enriching our lives immeasurably. They have allowed us to stand in the unique doorways of history and the contemporary world, and to view them through special lenses. Besides, we have enjoyed ourselves immensely.
I think o ften about the lyrics of that famous Louis Armstrong recording that we have heard many times on Crystal Cruise ships, as they sailed out of every port:
I see trees of green, red roses, too,
I see them bloom, for me and you.
And I think to myself,
What a wonderful world.
I see skies of blue, and clouds of white,
The bright blessed day, the dark sacred night.
And I think to myself,
What a wonderful world.
The colors of the rainbow, so pretty in the sky,
Are also on faces of people going by.
I see friends shaking hands, saying, “How do you do?”
They’re really saying, “I love you. ”
I hear babies crying, I watch them grow.
They’ll learn much more than I’ll ever know.
And I think to myself,
What a wonderful world.
Y es, I think to myself,
What a wonderful world.
Yes, I indeed think to myself, what a wonderful world!
Checking o ff Australia
Living the good life in Italy
Keith and Dot, the last continent
Sunset at the South Pole

## Principles

- First-hand observation provides deeper understanding than reading or media.
- Independent, flexible exploration yields more authentic experiences than rigid tours.
- Hands-on participation, even if small, is valuable in humanitarian response.
- Physical mementos and local craftsmanship connect travelers to places and people.

## Decision heuristics

- When wanting true understanding of historical events, visit relevant sites in person rather than relying solely on accounts.
- Prefer independent travel or cruising to maximize flexibility and reduce itinerary and luggage burdens.
- When responding to community disasters, join hands-on local recovery efforts to provide practical help and firsthand perspective.
