# P1_S30 – Famous People I’ve Met

# Famous People I’ve Met (None of Whom Would Ever Remember Me)
**Story ID:** P1_S30
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1950s–2000s, Accounting/Professional services, How to interpret and learn from brief encounters with famous people without idolizing them — turning fleeting moments into lasting personal and professional lessons., Minor setbacks do not derail long-term career mome, Serendipity combined with skill can produce transf, Direct engagement with people reduces intimidation, Observe others' grit and sacrifice as motivation t, Humility and selflessness are powerful leadership

## Summary

The narrator recounts brief encounters with various famous people across decades and the practical, personal lessons gleaned from those meetings.

## Full Text

Famous People I’ve Met
(None of Whom Would Ever
Remember Me)
I have had the pleasure of meeting many celebrities over the years. Most of these encounters were brief and many were not particularly substantive—surely, they were memorable only to me,
not to the people I met. Y et they le ft indelible impressions on me,
life lessons that have lighted my pathways for years and probably will continue to do so in years to come. A summary of a few of these follows.
My earliest recollection of meeting a celebrity was an encounter with Elvis Presley in about 1956. This is when I was a fifteen-year-old clarinet player in a Dixieland jazz band that won a spot in the annual Mid-South Fair T alent Competition in Memphis, T ennessee.
Elvis was twenty-one years old and at a break-out point of his career,
having just recorded some his first songs and signed a contract with
RCA. Colonel Parker, his agent, was managing part of the talent contest as well as promoting Elvis at the fair. He tried to get Elvis out on stage to showcase him to our audience, but for some reason,
Elvis refused to make an appearance. I vividly remember him out back in the alleyway behind the stage, throwing a wristwatch back and forth to a friend. He chatted it up with the five of us in our band as we stood and watched them. He bragged about winning a teddy bear as a prize at one of the carnival booths and ridiculed Colonel
Parker for being an unbridled promoter. I also remember being struck by his obstinacy, and thinking what a fool he was to miss such a publicity opportunity. And, of course, I remember the numerous times I have recalled this miniscule incident and thought about what little difference it made to his spectacular career. T elling people that
I once met Elvis Presley has been fun. More importantly, I learned a lesson from the encounter—that minor blips do not sidetrack the momentum of a career trajectory.
Somewhere near the same time, I had the opportunity for a trip to the National Boy Scout Jamboree at Valley Forge that I
wrote about earlier. The itinerary included a side trip to New Y ork,
where our scout troop attended a Brooklyn Dodgers-Chicago Cubs baseball game at Ebbets Field, truly a memorable experience. I was a diehard baseball fan and this was my first Major League game. I
still remember the score: Dodgers 6, Cubs 3. Even more memorable was a locker room visit after the game. Our small group had the opportunity to talk with Roy Campanella, the legendary Dodger catcher. He was pleasant and patient with us, answering all of our questions and encouraging us to diligently practice whatever interested us. He showed us his left hand, which seemed to me at the time to be the most gnarled example of human flesh I could imagine.
The hand re flected years of ba ttering behind the plate. He talked about the constant pain, but how it was worth all of it for the glory of Major League baseball. His grit inspired me. Many times over the years since then, I have pictured that gnarled hand as I contemplated interferences with my own ambitions.
In about 1990, I had a chance to meet and talk with T om Clancy,
who by happenstance was seated next to me at a business luncheon in his hometown of Baltimore. He was at the early stages of his notoriety and naturally was peppered with questions. He told the people at our table about how he managed to write The Hunt for Red
October, his first blockbuster novel. He described his school days at
Loyola University and his earlier career as a humble State Farm agent.
He related his numerous misguided attempts at writing and then his fortuitous meeting of some retired admirals at an Annapolis pub.
He confessed how he surreptitiously played their stories one against another and eventually pieced together the intricate submarine details contained in the book. He then described how he gave a copy of the book to an Argentine lady who had been seated next to him on an airplane and how she, in turn, passed it on to her friend President
Ronald Reagan. The president was interviewed on TV not long after and was asked what he might be reading for fun. He turned, picked up the book and said, “Well, I’m reading this excellent book called
The Hunt for Red October. ” After that little slice of free promotion, no further marketing was necessary! This anecdote has often reminded me how serendipity, coupled with a little skill, can dramatically change one’s life.
During the 1990–1995 era when I was with KPMG in
Philadelphia, I was responsible for the firm’s Washington, D.C.
office and travelled there regularly on business. I would take the early-morning Amtrak train to D.C. ’s Union Station and, usually, a late-afternoon train back; there was a stop in Wilmington, Delaware each way. Occasionally, Senator Joe Biden would be on the train,
and I had a chance to sit with him several times and talk (or more accurately, listen!). He had recently su ffered a brain aneurism,
similar to my close friend Hy Indowsky, from the Fort Lauderdale
Peat Marwick office, so we had at least a common starting point for a conversation. I learned from Senator Biden that he chose to commute to Washington from Delaware daily, rather than take up residence in the Capitol. He had lost his wife in an automobile accident and was committed to being home with his family whenever possible. He also told me why he was a teetotaler and had resolved to never drink, as well as stories about how a stuttering impediment had impacted his youth. I told him about my son Mark, who was dealing with speech issues. He struck me at the time as an everyday
Joe—almost a blue-collar poster boy—and I had to pinch myself to realize that he was a powerful United States senator. It never crossed my mind that one day he would be vice president of the United
States. Along with his discourses about political philosophies and personal journeys, his family devotion le ft a big impression on me,
influencing my thoughts about family and commitment for the next twenty-five years and more.
In Philadelphia in the early 1990s, General Colin Powell spoke at a large business banquet; it was shortly after his resignation from the military. KPMG was one of the sponsors for the event, and Dot and I represented the firm at a private pre-banquet meeting of about a dozen community leaders with him. General Powell spent almost an hour in a question-and-answer session with this small group. I
was in awe listening to him talk extemporaneously on a wide range of subjects. His command of the world stage was nothing short of astonishing. It seemed as if he was intimately familiar with every place on the globe. Moreover, the name of every world leader, regardless of how remote or obscure, was on the tip of his tongue. I asked him a couple of questions. In his responses, he looked me straight in the eye and gave me an eerie feeling of sincere connection. T ruly a most impressive man! I watched him carefully as he displayed a genuine respect for the country and its government, and proposed solutions for world problems—all with the calm aplomb of a world-class professional. He caused me to yearn for a more comprehensive understanding of my world and con firmed my respect for the professionalism of our military. I really liked this man and will never forget this special hour of enlightenment. A photograph of this event sits in my study today.
In about 2005, Dot and I travelled to London for an RHR
International board meeting. Our hotel was sponsoring a classical concert and offered us some complimentary tickets, which we gladly accepted. As it turned out, the concert was a highbrow event at an old Christopher Wren church in the Chelsea district. As we sat all prim and proper in the small audience, only two hundred people or so, Dot pointed out Margaret Thatcher in a seat just across the center aisle from us. Following the concert, we attended a nice reception in another part of the church. We again noticed Margaret Thatcher.
She was standing along the perimeter of the crowd, alone except for her bodyguard, and on an impulse, I walked over and struck up a conversation with her. Although I should have logically expected her to be an engaging conversationalist, I was nevertheless surprised and stricken by her charm and the ease of the conversation. I spent about twenty minutes talking about everything, from Boynton Beach,
Florida to the French rioting in Paris. One of my curious friends later asked, “Just what the hell would you have to talk about with
Margaret Thatcher for twenty minutes?” I don’t recall my answer, but
I do recall how many times since then I’ve been reminded that there is no good reason to be intimidated by anyone. My self-assurance,
admittedly never altogether in the background, was rea ffirmed by this experience.
Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher
And then there was Miss Oceola McCarty. While perhaps not as famous as some of the others, Miss McCarty gained notoriety and national publicity in the late 1990s. She was a poor African-American washerwoman in Hattiesburg, Mississippi, who at age eighty-seven had donated her life savings of $150,000 to the University of Southern
Mississippi for a scholarship fund. The national media latched onto her story, and she became a national emblem of sel fless charity.
She was awarded the Presidential Citizens Award by President Bill
Clinton in 1998. Around that time, the USM Foundation escorted
Miss McCarty to New Y ork to visit potential donors who might be interested in matching her generosity. Knowing that I had business contacts in the New Y ork financial community and was an alumnus,
they invited me to accompany them. The University hosted a nice luncheon at the Metropolitan Club, and I had the privilege of sitting at the head table with Miss McCarty, the USM president, Senator
T rent Lott and his wife, Dan Rather and a couple of other journalists who had covered her story. Of course, this remarkable little lady was the star of the show at our table. Even in the midst of the splendor of the New Y ork glitz, her humility and selflessness matched the bright lights of the city. For an uneducated and lowly Southern lady, she displayed remarkable wit, self-con fidence and clever conversation.
Senator Lott asked her if she had any secrets about ironing a man’s shirt, to which she responded by explaining in great detail how one should start with the cu ff and finish with the collar. He replied,
“Well, that’s interesting. I always start with the collar and end with the cuff. ” She quickly quipped, with a wry grin, “Well, Mr. Lott, it’s just like some of the things you folks are doing up in Washington.
Y ou’re just doing it wrong!” I loved this little lady. She’s now gone,
of course, but I think of her o ften, often when they pass around the offering plate in our church. What an inspiration!
Over the years, I have met numerous other well-known personalities, but the above stories illustrate some of the timeless inspiration and guidance that I have gleaned from these brief experiences.
Many people tend to either worship or despise famous people.
I have tried to parse out some of the characteristics that make celebrities special and to learn from these people without wholesale adulation, judgment, or contempt. And I indeed have learned quite a lot from them. I am, in part, a product of these experiences; they shaped my thinking for years to come.

## Principles

- Minor setbacks do not derail long-term career momentum.
- Serendipity combined with skill can produce transformative opportunities.
- Direct engagement with people reduces intimidation and builds confidence.
- Observe others' grit and sacrifice as motivation to persist in your ambitions.
- Humility and selflessness are powerful leadership virtues regardless of fame.
- Family devotion informs leadership priorities and commitment.

## Decision heuristics

- When you encounter a notable person, consider approaching them directly to have a personal conversation.
- Treat small setbacks as temporary; maintain momentum in your craft and career.
- Prepare your skills and be ready to act when chance presents an opening; serendipity favors the ready.
- Use examples of others' grit and sacrifice to sustain your own commitment during hardship.

# P1_S31 – These Are a Few of My Favorite Things

# These Are a Few of My Favorite Things
**Story ID:** P1_S31
**Part:** One
**Tags:** mid-20th century to present, education / music, Monotonous, disciplined study-hall environment versus occasional bursts of musical beauty that awaken and sustain a lifelong passion for music., Find and acknowledge beauty in mundane settings, Use music as a tool for emotional regulation and m, Preserve meaningful artifacts to maintain connecti, Internalize beloved works by learning and practici

## Summary

The narrator recounts how moments of music in a monotonous school setting sparked a lifelong love of music and lists favorite compositions that shaped emotions and memories.

## Full Text

These Are a Few of My
Favorite Things
Study hall periods for seventh and eighth graders at Calhoun
City School were monotonous and uncomfortable. They were held in the auditorium on the second floor of the old school building.
On either side of the stage was a small dressing room that was used for piano lessons —one, on the le ft, occupied by Libba Jane
Harrelson for beginning levels and the other by Freddie Cox for more advanced students. T eachers proctored the aisles of the study hall with stern expressions, trying to maintain discipline and ensure that “studying” was taking place. However, there was no keeping the noise level down, due largely to the constant competing cacophony penetrating the thin walls of those little dressing rooms. There was much hunting and pecking, repetitious musical scales, dissonant chords and fumbling around with strange melodies. Y et in the midst of all this monotony and noise, there were occasional bursts of beauty. A few talented students practiced and learned musical scores that radiated sonorously throughout that small auditorium.
My young ears always perked up when these exceptions seeped through the walls. My love of music was hatching.
Music has played a pivotal role in my life for as long as I can remember. The impact of music is difficult to explain, but it has had a profound influence on my personality and my cultural foundation.
Music has served as a trigger for memories of key events. I have looked back over the years and tried to formulate a list of my T op
T en songs. That task has proved daunting because there are so many candidates, in such a wide variety of styles and genres. Nevertheless, I
have struggled and constructed such a list. The musical compositions described in the following nine paragraphs have special places in my heart—places occupied for a variety of reasons. A brief overview of each follows, ranked in no particular order.
“Somewhere Over the Rainbow” is an incredibly beautiful song that belongs near the top of the list. Harold Arlen and E.Y. Harburg wrote this song in 1939 for The Wizard of Oz movie starring Judy
Garland as Dorothy, who sings it to her little dog, T oto. The melody and lyrics are equally touching. I was first drawn to the melody, which left an unforge ttable tune plastered inside my brain, as a teenager.
Then I began to focus on the lyrics, which somehow echoed much of my yearnings in those quixotic days —“Somewhere over the rainbow, blue birds fly, and the dreams that you dreamed of, dreams really do come true. ” I loved that line, which seemed to li ft me up and take me out of Calhoun City. It also seemed to me to express a universal hope. Then I realized that the music and lyrics matched perfectly. I learned to play “Somewhere Over the Rainbow” on the piano, and I have played it probably more than a thousand times over the past fifty years. It still raises goosebumps on my arms, and I
love it as much now as I did the first time I heard it.
There were times when Freddie Cox would fill in gaps in his lesson schedule by playing the piano himself. Identifying those occasions was easy. Freddie, a somewhat odd character otherwise,
was an absolute piano virtuoso with truly unique talent and skill.
He played “Claire de Lune” like no one I have heard since. Now,
bear in mind, I was twelve or thirteen years old at the time and knew nothing whatsoever about formal music. So perhaps my views might have been a bit exaggerated. However, I came to simply adore
“Clair de Lune” , the Claude Debussy masterpiece. The mathematical precision, the hills and valleys of the melody, and the odd chords all blended to create a haunting theme that resonated in my soul and has lived with me for a lifetime. It’s been said that Debussy was a musical Impressionist; he and his interpreter Freddie Cox most definitely left an impression on me. I never learned to play “Clair de Lune” on the piano; it is above my skill level. But the everlasting impression this magnificent work left inside me is the reason I now place it in my litany of top favorite musical compositions.
Van Cliburn skyrocketed to international fame in 1958, when he won the first T chaikovsky International Piano Competition in
Moscow, playing with impeccable perfection T chaikovsky’s Piano
Concerto No. 1 in B Flat Minor, a classic composed in 1875. Somehow
Libba Jane Harrelson knew about this remarkable and historical event and presented me with a gi ft of the soundtrack. I still have it stored away with my other relics. Of course, it’s hardly playable now, since the format is the old 33 1/3 rpm disc, but I managed to download it into my iTunes library and still play it from time to time,
along with other artists’ renditions. In my view, the concerto is one of the most powerful pieces of classical music ever composed. I love it, and I love the memory of Libba Jane, who surely had no idea how much I would enjoy it for the rest of my life.
Shifting to American in fluences on the classical scene, while in high school I became addicted to George Gershwin’s Rhapsody in
Blue. Less structured technically than the European classical works,
Rhapsody is probably be tter characterized as classical jazz. The melodies and pace of the music demand one’s full a ttention from beginning to end. It has demanded my attention for more than fifty years, from the moment it opens with the clarinet glissando to the haunting blues melody throughout.
The Eddy Duchin Story , filmed in the mid-1950s, starred
Tyrone Power and Kim Novak, with piano music performed by
Carmen Cavalaro. I saw the movie and absolutely adored the music,
particularly the theme song, “T o Love Again” . This theme was based on Chopin’s “Nocturne in E Flat” , another one of the beautiful pieces from the golden age of European composers. This one song has also remained by my side for more than fifty years. I now have the original soundtrack of The Eddy Duchin Story in my iTunes library and play it regularly (over and over again) on my iPhone and home Sonos system. The “Nocturne” has essentially become my personal theme song; it effectively defines my love for piano music.
When I played clarinet in the Dixieland band in high school and came to love Dixieland music, I tried my best to emulate Pete
Fountain, the eminent clarinetist of that day. Pete had a fluid and woody tone that I simply could not replicate, no ma tter how hard I
tried. Anyway, the most famous song among the almost one hundred albums he recorded over the years was “Just a Closer Walk With
Thee” . It was his theme song, played at virtually every performance over a five-decade career. I loved it as a teenager and I still do. It defines New Orleans music and a segment of Dixieland jazz for me.
“Just a Closer Walk” is an easy member of my T op T en.
My music education and familiarity basically originated in the church. Dozens of favorite hymns and religious compositions have touched my soul over the years. However, one stands out singularly—“The Lord’s Prayer” , written by a little-known composer named Albert Malo tte. My first recollection of this masterpiece was a stunning performance in the First Baptist Church by Ada
Gwyn Prior, a remarkably talented soloist, in Calhoun City. It still stirs incredible emotion within me whenever I think about it.
Absolute beauty! Probably the best contemporary rendition of this magni ficent music is a recording by Andrea Bocelli with the
Mormon T abernacle Choir. It can’t be beat!
Moving along, the 1950s and 1960s decades were a time of inspiration and experimentation in the world of music. My choice of the best song of that era is “Y esterday” , performed by the Beatles with Paul McCartney as the lead singer. While the music is unique and exquisite, the lyrics spoke to me in the 1960s. “Y esterday, all my troubles seemed so far away… ” . Like a mirror, they re flected a lot of my inner thinking at the time. That thinking, interestingly, has now morphed more into “tomorrow”! “Y esterday” stands out as one of the most famous songs in music history. I fully understand that notoriety.
Broadway musicals and the movies have produced dozens of candidates for my T op T en. The magic works of some of the great composers live through the decades —Richard Rodgers, Oscar
Hammerstein, George Gershwin, Cole Porter and on and on. From this long list of worthy candidates, I have chosen Burt Bacharach and his flagship song, “Raindrops Keep Falling on My Head” . Bacharach’s music has often been classified as perhaps the most complex among the works of American composers —dizzying arrays of melodies,
chord changes, modulations and key changes, making the songs extremely difficult for musicians to play. Y et, the tunes and rhythms are catchy and seem to stick like glue in one’s memory. “Raindrops”
is like that —complicated and infectious. This might seem an odd choice of a favorite from this genre, but it reflects my admiration for a true musical genius.
My last Top Ten selection is an American tradition for the past century. It’s “ The Stars and Stripes Forever”, composed by
John Philip Sousa in 1896. It’s the o fficial march of the United
States. It brings people to their feet and triggers applause whenever and wherever it is performed. The composition features the entire marching band, from tuba to piccolo. Its brisk pace imbues the space with a powerful sense of patriotism. I am inspired every time I hear it. It’s an easy T op T en pick.
I can think of dozens of others —perhaps hundreds—that stir up my emotions. “ Are Y ou Lonesome T onight” (Elvis Presley),
“That’s What Friends Are For” (Burt Bacharach), “How Great
Thou Art” ( The Gaithers), “I Will Always Love Y ou” (Whitney
Houston), “Battle Hymn of the Republic” (Mormon T abernacle
Choir), “Hey Jude” (The Beatles), “Hallelujah Chorus” by George
Frideric Handel, “Y ou Light Up My Life” (Debby Boone), “What A
Wonderful World” (Louis Armstrong), “The Way We Were” (Barbra
Streisand), “Moon River” by Henry Mancini and Johnny Mercer,
“White Christmas” (Bing Crosby), “1812 Overture” (T chaikovsky),
“Don’t Cry For Me Argentina” by Andrew Lloyd Webber and Tim
Rice, “ Amazing Grace” and on and on. Heck, maybe I should have set out to list the T op 100! I can’t seem to stop.
The common theme running through all these compositions and recordings is how they have touched me over time. They all fall into a category that I think of as magic potions, casting all sorts of different spells. Music has incredible power. It has brightened my bleakest days. It stirs up wonderful memories in my life and can change my mood in a flash. So, I rely on my favorite music to keep my spirits alive and healthy. As Julie Andrews sang so beautifully in
The Sound of Music: “I simply remember my favorite things, and then
I don’t feel so bad. ”

## Principles

- Find and acknowledge beauty in mundane settings.
- Use music as a tool for emotional regulation and memory triggers.
- Preserve meaningful artifacts to maintain connection to formative experiences.
- Internalize beloved works by learning and practicing them.

## Decision heuristics

- When a melody moves you, learn and practice it to internalize its emotional effect.
- Preserve significant recordings and convert obsolete formats so they remain accessible.
- When feeling down, recall favorite things to improve mood quickly.

# P1_S32 – For the Love of Words

# For the Love of Words
**Story ID:** P1_S32
**Part:** One
**Tags:** spans decades from early career to the present (references to 'years ago' and recent website activity), professional services / accounting (Peat Marwick referenced), No high-stakes conflict; main tension is how to cultivate and sustain a personal avocation for words alongside other activities., Regular, Curate a personal, Learning driven by intrinsic enjoyment sustains lo, Use playful competition to reinforce learning and, Sharing work publicly can create durability and us

## Summary

A reflective first-person account of a lifelong passion for vocabulary: influences, habits (collections, subscriptions, games), and the pleasure derived from words and their clarity.

## Full Text

For the Love of Words
I step into the subject of vocabulary with trepidation, lest I leave an impression that I visualize myself as some sort of English scholar.
Clearly that’s not the case, but I will confess to being an aficionado.
Not a connoisseur, not a cognoscente; but simply someone who has admired and observed expressive language over the years—perhaps one who could be characterized as an esthete. Not quite right, but close.
I can’t be sure when or where my focus on words originated.
It probably all started early in my career with Bill Norton, my first managing partner at Peat Marwick. Bill was indeed a connoisseur.
Despite all of his peculiar qualities and idiosyncrasies, he used extremely descriptive language, often spiced with arcane words and phrases that induced a trip to be dictionary. He appeared to delight in confounding his audiences with his unusual lingo, and for some reason I was just as delighted to be confounded.
Bill’s expressions were challenging. And so we began our strange word riddle game that continued for more than forty years. It was a private game without formal rules and without scoring. Part of the fun was that neither of us ever outwardly acknowledged that the game was going on. Bill died a few years ago. I miss him and I miss the game. But I take pleasure that he improved my vocabulary, even though it took a long time to do it.
Bill Safire was one of my favorite word mavens for many years.
He wrote a column “On Language” for the New York Times Magazine for twenty years. I subscribed to the Sunday Times for most of that time and read his column religiously. Safire was a smart guy with an unusual gi ft for word cra ftsmanship. I loved his writings and have missed him since his death a few years ago. Another interesting journalist was Edwin Newman, whose excellent descriptive vocabulary fueled his humor. He was a master punster and linguist.
I remember reading two of his best-selling books, Strictly Speaking:
Will America be the Death of English? and A Civil T ongue. I miss him, too.
Y ears ago I started a collection of words that I happened to like for one reason or another. At first, the word collection consisted of scraps of miscellaneous notes. Then, somewhere along the line I
began an official list on my computer. The criteria were: (1) words that we have read or heard, but might not be able to clearly define if called upon; (2) words that are extremely descriptive of a speci fic abstract concept; (3) words that some people occasionally de fine or pronounce incorrectly (often, the opposite of the true meaning);
and (4) words that simply sound pleasant or have a crisp, descriptive tone. Just a few examples are: callow, didactic, heuristic, panoply,
truculent and obdurate. I really don’t have a cogent reason for this avocation. It was just a labor of love that I found enjoyable.
A few years ago a friend tipped me o ff to a website called
Thinkmap Visual Thesaurus. I subscribed and through it receive a
“Word of the Day” each day of the week, with an accompanying paragraph chronicling its origin and evolution. It has been fascinating to begin each day with this short lesson. As I am writing this chapter,
for example, the word that popped up today was “garbanzo” . That’s a new one for me, one I have never seen before. It’s actually a legume—a chickpea relative. So now I know!
The Thinkmap website and a corollary website called Vocabulary.
com regularly publish handy articles on vocabulary usage and grammar, and a compendium called “Featured Word Lists” , where hundreds of readers have submitted all sorts of word collections —
words from famous documents, books, speeches and writings of famous people as well as various topical collections. About five years ago, I submitted a portion of my word collection and labeled it “93
Eclectic Words” . For quite a while this list ranked number one on the website. T oday, even after five years, it remains ranked number nineteen on the website’s published “T op Fi fty” ever submi tted.
It has been fun watching the durability of my list and reading the dozens of comments submitted by readers.
Related to this attraction to words, I have worked on crossword puzzles for years. In one sense, it is a silly waste of time every day—
sitting around filling in little le tters in little boxes. Nevertheless,
I continue to find it a challenging and enjoyable exercise. As is commonly known, the daily newspaper crosswords begin the week with easy puzzles and get progressively harder every day until the next
Sunday, when I find full completion almost impossible. Whenever
I try to tackle the really di fficult ones and get stumped early on, I
wonder to myself, “Who on earth is smart enough to breeze through something like this?” But I suppose those brains are out there. Not me. By mid-week, I’m struggling. However, my struggle continues on week after week. Hope springs eternal!
Many studies have confirmed a correlation between vocabulary and real-world competence, yet there seems to be confusion as to the most e ffective way to enhance vocabulary. T oday, numerous computer programs are designed to improve vocabulary, and some actually seem to be e ffective. One interesting computer game that is both educational and enjoyable is called “Words with Friends” .
I play this competitive game every day with Dot, Polly Y ork, one of Dot’s sisters, and my sister, Sandy. The competition is fun, but it is also an excellent learning experience. New words continue to pop up regularly. Dot and I also play a game called “Upwords” and keep a running score that has reached into the thousands now. Our cumulative scores are less than one percent apart.
There are also a lot of computer and smart phone apps to test and assess the use of words. Many, for example, will count the words in a document and a few will actually calculate the number of unique words and develop other useful statistics. For example,
according to this technology, this memoir contains approximately
85,000 words, with more than 10,000 of those being unique —that is, 10,000 different words in the document. There are more than 800
paragraphs and 5,000 sentences. For a numbers guy like me, that’s all very interesting.
T o summarize, I love words. They have life. They have power.
They deliver clarity. I wish I knew more of them.

## Principles

- Regular, small exposures build vocabulary over time.
- Curate a personal, persistent list to deepen familiarity with chosen words.
- Learning driven by intrinsic enjoyment sustains long-term practice.
- Use playful competition to reinforce learning and maintain engagement.
- Sharing work publicly can create durability and useful feedback.

## Decision heuristics

- Capture interesting words immediately in a personal list on your computer.
- Subscribe to a daily 'Word of the Day' service to ensure regular exposure.
- Use competitive word games daily with peers to learn new vocabulary through play.
- Publish a curated selection to public forums to test durability and gather feedback.

---

# P1_S33 – Houses, Cars and Boats

# Houses, Cars and Boats
**Story ID:** P1_S33
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1960s–2010s, Accounting / professional services (and later business/executive roles), Balancing the desire for homes, cars, and boats as identity and family-enriching assets against financial prudence and the risk of overextending resources., Learn from financial mistakes and take corrective, Do your own research when external advice is vague, Use major assets (homes, Seek trusted counsel before making large expenditu, Mitigate operational risk by hiring expertise and

## Summary

A personal memoir tracing decades of the narrator's choices about homes, automobiles, and boats, showing how material desires, research, advice, and learning from mistakes shaped family life and investments.

## Full Text

Houses, Cars and Boats
Many families de fine their identify by their assets —their houses, cars and toys. Materialism is one of the basic and powerful facts of human behavior. I suppose it has been somewhat of a factor for Dot and me as we have stumbled through the years. Therefore, I
likewise suppose a memoir would be incomplete without describing some of these important faceplates in our lives.
During our first couple of years of marriage, Dot and I rented apartments. The first was a tiny one-bedroom co ttage on Green
Street in Jackson, Mississippi, tucked behind the large Calvary
Baptist Church. The apartment was modestly furnished, but clean and neat. The rent was $75 a month. The location was convenient for us because the church had provided Dot a full scholarship at
Mississippi College in exchange for her service as a soloist on Sunday mornings. We later leased a more modern one-bedroom furnished unit in a complex on Je fferson Street. This complex was brand new,
and we were excited about our upscale surroundings. However, we were anxious and eager to own a home of our own, so much so that we rushed into it faster than we should have.
We pinched pennies until we accumulated about $1,200 in our savings account. Then in 1965, we found a three-bedroom home at 1249 Springdale Drive and made a deal for $19,000. The downpayment was $900, and we executed an FHA mortgage for
$18,100. Payments were $116 per month, including taxes and interest. It was a nice home in excellent condition, situated in a pleasant, tree-lined neighborhood. It was a typical tract house, with the only oddity being the unusual pink kitchen appliances. We took great pride in this home over the next six years.
We had no furniture for this new castle except for a TV and a bookcase. The $300 left over in savings after the downpayment was not going to reach far, we realized as soon as we began to set up housekeeping. Acute stress set in as I came to realize all the things we thought we needed—a washer and dryer, bedroom furniture, a sofa,
lawn mower, etc. For example, I remember coming to the realization that we needed a mailbox; then, after going to the hardware store and purchasing one, I realized I needed a shovel and cement to install it. And so it went, as we sank into debt and wound up with a stack of installment payment coupons. Only a year earlier, I had passed the CPA examination and was ostensibly a financial expert.
Now this self-acclaimed expert was finding himself up to his ears in debt and stretched to the limit financially. Ultimately, we hunkered down and waded our way through this crisis. But I vowed then and there to never again find myself in such a position.
We sold the Springdale Drive house for a modest profit when we moved to Orlando in 1971. There we settled on a nice brick home at
138 Variety T ree Circle in Altamonte Springs. This was a larger four-bedroom home and was listed for more than double the selling price of our Jackson home. More cautious this time, I remember calling
Norm White, the regional Peat Marwick partner in Atlanta, and asking what he thought about me spending that much money for a house. He actually laughed at me and said, “Go for it. Y ou can afford it. ” It was a nice, trouble-free house in a beautiful neighborhood with terrific neighbors. The property’s twelve Valencia orange trees and two tangerine trees produced tons of fruit annually. It was an idyllic place for our family at the time. We called it home for four years.
Leaving Orlando in 1975, we purchased a home at 3060 NE 47
Street in Fort Lauderdale. We once again upped the ante and paid more than double the selling price of our Orlando home. The Fort
Lauderdale home was a beautiful, four-bedroom house with a large
Florida room in back overlooking a swimming pool and a canal running off the Intracoastal Waterway. It was conveniently located in the prestigious Coral Ridge area inside the city. We loved this home and thoroughly enjoyed living there for fourteen years. Our three boys spent their formative years there and consider it their original homestead. It was a wise investment, too; when we left Fort
Lauderdale, we sold it for exactly three times what we had paid for it.
In 1989, we landed in Baltimore, Maryland. Before we le ft
Florida, I made some inquiries of the KPMG partners there, asking about what section of the city we should consider. Their answers were all over the page, mostly focused on tract developments in outlying suburbs. Not being satis fied with this guidance, I went to the Broward County library and did some research. I managed to find a listing of the fifty top executives in Baltimore. Still at the library, I located a Baltimore phone book. I then plo tted the home addresses of these executives on a city map. Voilà! I had my answer.
So we concentrated our search in the Ruxton/Malvern area, not far from the center of the city. We located a beautiful, two-story
Georgian home atop a steep hill, at 1428 East Joppa Road. The house was under construction and about 95 percent complete —the only brand new home we have ever owned. We had the chance to tailor most of the final details to our tastes, and the result was arguably the all-around best home we ever owned. It was meticulously built by a perfectionist contractor and was finely detailed in every respect. It had a huge 1,500-square-foot walk-in finished basement that we fitted out nicely with an oak bar, pool table and recreational furnishings. Another unique feature was a music room near the front entrance, where we placed our grand piano and some of our musical collections. When Dot finished decorating the home, it was a showplace. We used it liberally for entertainment. The only disappointment was that we had the opportunity to live there for only two years. Then, on to Philadelphia in 1991.
The Philadelphia Main Line has a well-deserved reputation as one of the most beautiful suburban communities in America.
Winding, tree-lined roads meander through hills and valleys, with large lots and a wide variety of beautiful brick and stone homes set back among lush vegetation. Dot and I scoured this landscape,
looking at fifty or more houses before settling on a beautiful property at 840 Mt. Moro Road in Villanova. This was a recently renovated and expanded house with a French motif, nestled on a well-landscaped one-acre lot. The renovation developer was living in the house at the time. It was a large home, with a combination of historic-type styling and modern appointments. It had terrific curb appeal. Interestingly,
practically all our furnishings fit the house to a tee. We had to acquire practically nothing to furnish the place. Again, we used the home liberally for entertainment. This was our home for four years.
In 1995, we returned to Fort Lauderdale. We had loved the
Coral Ridge area, where we had lived before, and were predisposed to return to that neighborhood. However, as we began our house search, we gravitated to the south part of the city, where some of our prior friends were now living. It was also a bit more convenient to downtown, the airport and major roadways. Following a thorough search, we landed at 2521 Del Lago Drive. The home was a
5,600-square-foot single-floor structure situated on a canal just off
Philadelphia home for four years
Home base in Fort Lauderdale, Florida the Intracoastal Waterway near the port. Reportedly, the home had once been lavishly decorated in Laura Ashley style, but after a bank repossession it had been neutralized in order to facilitate a sale. Therefore, it was plain vanilla and required some extensive remodeling. We hired an interior decorator, and she and Dot did a beautiful job of converting this terri fic house into a showplace. We have lived in this home now for twenty years. We have continued to update and maintain the property over the years, but the essential design, look and feel is much the same as it was following our initial occupancy.
And then there is the Mallory House, our respite in Calhoun City that we have enjoyed for the past many years. We purchased the home in 1998 from our friend Fred Nabors, a local developer who had meticulously restored the property. Our beloved high school English teacher, Miss Frances Mallory, had lived in the home practically her
The Mallory House, Calhoun City entire life, so we named it after her. Built in 1905, it is the oldest house in Calhoun City and exudes the harmony of the history of the community. The Mallory House has been our home away from home, where we visit about four times each year. It is fully decorated and stocked, and is just as comfortable as our home in Florida. James
Pittman has been our caretaker since we have owned it, and takes great care to keep it in top flight condition. He takes enormous pride in the place and treats it as his personal asset. We will be eternally gratefully to James for his loyalty and his excellent service at the
Mallory House.

James Pittman, caretaker

Shifting from houses to automobiles, Dot and I started our marriage with two cars—a 1957 Ford Tudor Sedan and a 1961 Ford
Falcon carried over from our college days. Neither was in great shape,
but the 1957 Ford was particularly dilapidated. After struggling with it for a couple of years, I eventually parked it in our driveway and replaced it. Dot, ge tting tired of seeing in there, sold it to a scrap dealer for $50. When we later had serious engine problems with the
Falcon, we traded it in for a brand new maroon 1965 Ford Fairlane.
We bought the new car from Dot’s brother Charles Hill, who was working as a salesman at Hawkins Ford dealership in Calhoun City.
It was a beautiful car and we were justifiably proud of it.
In those days, cars were not nearly as well built as they are today,
and their designs changed o ften. Therefore, we found ourselves trading one of our cars every couple of years. Over the next few years,
we owned a yellow Pontiac, a two-tone brown Oldsmobile and a white Buick Riviera. In about 1972, I surprised Dot at Christmas by driving home a gorgeous white and maroon Cadillac and parking it in the driveway. It was our first of several Cadillacs, and we were both excited. Later on, we shi fted our tastes to foreign models. I
purchased a snazzy BMW 633 sports car and got Dot a beautiful dark green Jaguar sedan. We then shifted to Lexus and owned both sedan and sports models of that luxury brand.
When we returned to Fort Lauderdale with Alamo, we dumped our cars and began driving company-furnished Cadillacs for the next two years. Because of our General Motors connections, we traded these cars for new ones about every six months.
Upon the sale of Alamo, Mike Egan arranged a dinner and awarded me several gi fts in recognition of my leadership of the company. One of these gi fts was an option to pick out any car
I wanted and send the bill to him. So, I chose a sleek BMW 840
CI. This was one of the coolest cars ever built. It retailed for about
$85,000 in 1997. BMW only sold about seven thousand of this model over a five-year period. People gawked at it everywhere I
went. I loved the look and feel of this car and kept it for about ten years, before I eventually sold it on eBay to a retired executive in
South Carolina for $24,000.
For our family car during this time, we had Mercedes, BMW
and Lexus sedans at various times. Our latest Lexus was a hybrid,
a new and di fferent twist for us. For a while, we owned three cars.
Somehow, we reasoned that we needed a convertible (since we lived in South Florida), so we started this craze with a BMW Z3, a cute sports car that we used for tooling around town. We kept it for ten years as well, and also sold it on eBay. Continuing the convertible interests, we replaced the Z3 with a Lexus 450 hardtop convertible and then replaced that with an Infiniti G37 hardtop convertible.
While at Alamo, I became interested in electric automobiles and placed an order in 1996 for ten General Motors experimental electric cars, to test them in the rental market place. Following hybrid and electric car developments over the next several years, it seemed to me that T esla was cracking the code. So, in 2014 I took the plunge and purchased a new T esla Model S sedan. It is clearly the most remarkable vehicle I have even seen, much less driven. It is as close to perfect as one can imagine for an automobile. It is fast, quiet,
technologically advanced and simply fun to drive and own. I have been significantly impressed and have enjoyed owning it.
Looking back, we have owned over thirty different automobiles over the past fifty-plus years. I have enjoyed each and every one of them (except for the starter Fords) and never was so unfortunate to draw a lemon.
We have owned not as many boats. But the ones we did own were a source of great pleasure and fun. We acquired our first boat in the early 1980s, a thirty-two-foot T rojan sedan that I bought from the estate of a deceased partner in KPMG’s Jacksonville office. I
knew little about boating at the time and hired a captain to assist me in bringing it three hundred miles down the Intracoastal Waterway.
I supplemented this experience with a course sponsored by the
U.S. Power Squadron, and before long became adept at navigation,
operation and maintenance of our boat. We renamed it “Happiness” ,
thinking the name might prove providential for our family, and we thoroughly enjoyed our many marine outings together. A fter a few years of wear and tear, I decided to sell it and look for a better one.
As it turned out, another Jacksonville partner had a thirty-six-foot Chris Cra ft sports fisherman that he was interested in selling.
He had acquired it following a heart a ttack and had spent three or four years tinkering and improving the vessel as therapy for his health condition. It was in pristine condition—better than new—so
I jumped on the opportunity and made another three-hundred-mile journey down the Intracoastal. We renamed this boat “Happiness”
also. It was a significant improvement over the T rojan, and our family enjoyed it even more. We took several extended trips on it —to the
Bahamas, through the cross-Florida waterway, and down into the
Keys on several occasions. I also used it o ften for deep-sea fishing.
Paul and John were teenagers at the time and helped me maintain
“Happiness” and keep it in top condition. Eventually, as the boys got older and their interests changed, our usage of the boat diminished.
So, we sold it shortly before we moved to Baltimore. I remember the sad day when we all stood on the dock and watched the new owner head away down the canal.
Our family thoroughly enjoyed these boats. They were a catalyst for family unity during some important years for our children. And we had some unique experiences as we vacationed and travelled around Florida and the Bahamas. Once, on our way to an outing in the Keys with my good friend Allan Arch and his family, we came upon a dead man floating in the Intracoastal Waterway near the
Sunrise bridge in Fort Lauderdale. Allan and I reported the sighting to the Coast Guard and waited for help to arrive. Unfortunately, we then spent most of the rest of that day in interviews with the local police. We never learned the man’s identity or the reason for his death. On another occasion, our family was in the Bahamas when we encountered two fishermen stranded in open waters after their boat had sunk. One was floating out of sight of land, clinging to a red gasoline can. The other was spo tted about a half-mile away,
swimming in the wrong direction. We literally saved these guys’
lives.
Along the way, we owned three Boston Whalers. Back in the 1980s, we had a fifteen-foot Super Sport that we kept on a li ft behind our house. This boat was used almost exclusively as local transportation around Fort Lauderdale’s miles of waterways. I can’t begin to estimate how many hours John scooted around town on it with his friends and enjoyed water sports with it. By the time we sold it, it was almost worn out! After returning to Fort Lauderdale in
1995, I felt like we had to have a boat, so I went back to Lauderdale
Marina and purchased a twenty-four-foot Boston Whaler Outrage.
It was a great boat. As time went by, however, it became a little dated,
so I traded it for a somewhat similar twenty-three-foot Boston
Whaler Dauntless. This essentially became a local water taxi for us,
and we used it primarily to drive around town sightseeing and going out to dinner at waterside restaurants. As our usage diminished, I
finally decided to bite the bullet and become “boatless” for the first time in many years.

## Principles

- Learn from financial mistakes and take corrective action.
- Do your own research when external advice is vague or inconsistent.
- Use major assets (homes, boats) to create family experiences and social value.
- Seek trusted counsel before making large expenditures.
- Mitigate operational risk by hiring expertise and acquiring skills when entering a new domain.

## Decision heuristics

- If expert local guidance is inconsistent, collect primary data (phone books, maps) and plot target peers to identify desirable neighborhoods.
- When lacking operational experience, hire a knowledgeable professional and undertake formal training before full responsibility.
- Before making large discretionary purchases, consult a trusted senior colleague for perspective on affordability.
- After experiencing financial strain from overextension, 'hunker down'—prioritize paying obligations and avoid repeating risky purchases until stabilized.
- When evaluating new technology for business or personal use, pilot/test in a controlled way before full-scale adoption.

# P1_S34 – My Three Sons

# My Three Sons
**Story ID:** P1_S34
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1968–2000s, Accounting/Consulting, Raising three very different sons to adulthood while ensuring they received strong education, developed core values, and became independent and responsible men., Prioritize providing the best possible education f, Treat children with respect and as autonomous indi, Be consistent in parenting and dealings with child, Support independence by stepping back when a child, Act promptly on credible external observations abo

## Summary

A father reflects on raising his three sons, describing their personalities, education, milestones, and the values and choices that shaped their development and families.

## Full Text

My Three Sons
Of all the memories sca ttered over of my life, among the most joyful are re flections on our three sons, Paul, John and Mark. I am a very fortunate father to have been blessed with such wonderful children—indeed a proud father, in every sense of the word.
Paul arrived in our lives on May 1, 1968, at St. Dominic Hospital in Jackson. I have a vivid memory of standing beside his tiny bed for the first time, picking him up and holding him, and asking myself,
“Can this be real?” It felt like a sort of mirage, or a miracle, the realization that this was really and truly a new life that Dot and I had created and brought into the world.
John was born on October 29, 1971, at Orange Memorial
Hospital in Orlando. I was attending a KPMG partners’ meeting in
Boca Raton and received a call from Dot late in the evening to tell me she was on the way to the hospital. I immediately jumped into my car and raced the two hundred miles, arriving at the hospital just before John arrived in the world in the wee morning hours. Once again, I stood in a brightly lit hospital nursery with wonder and amazement. Another miracle!
Mark was our third gi ft, delivered on April 11, 1977, at Holy
Cross Hospital in Fort Lauderdale. I shall never forget seeing him for the first time —a beautiful doll-like figure with a sprinkling of red hair. It seemed as if he had a faint smile on his face —perhaps a foreshadowing of the fun-loving personality that was later to develop. I remember a pair of twins, the Fetch twins, who were born prematurely at almost the same time and were side by side with him in the nursery. One died almost immediately and the other was born blind. That made Mark’s miracle even more dramatic in my mind.
Dot and I have thousands of wonderful stories about these guys over the last several decades. We also have dozens of albums of photos to go along with the stories —thousands and thousands of pictures. T o try and capsule their lives in a short memoir chapter is actually hopeless. Nevertheless, a few highlights and themes do emerge. The early school days are memorable, as are their varied activities and interests over the years.
From the get-go, Dot and I focused sharply on making sure our guys received as good an education as we could provide for them.
Paul was three years old when we moved to Orlando. He later entered the first grade at a public school at Altamonte Springs. He then went to the second grade at Bay View Elementary School, after we moved to Fort Lauderdale, where he was quickly singled out as a gifted student. We enrolled him in the third grade at the venerable
Pine Crest School, which he attended through his graduation in
1986. Paul was always an excellent student, but perhaps a bit of an underachiever academically. Invariably, he tested higher than his grades. He had lots of fine friends and was a working teenager at
Beach Hardware. He swam on the swim team at Pine Crest and was active on the debate team. Somewhere along the way, he became interested in military and public a ffairs and seemed drawn to the
U.S. Air Force for some reason. He applied and was accepted by a number of universities and also applied for an appointment to the
Air Force Academy. All service academy admissions required a formal appointment by a member of the U.S. Congress. I was friends with Congressman Clay Shaw and told Paul that I would be pleased to speak to Clay about his application. He said, “Dad, please don’t do that. If I’m going to do this, I want to do it on my own. ” So I
backed off and let him proceed. He was accepted by the Academy and received his congressional appointment from U.S. Senator Paula
Hawkins, one of only two Florida appointments allocated to her that year. So off he went into a completely new and different life.
John was only three years old when we moved to Fort Lauderdale.
He followed Paul at Pine Crest and attended school there all twelve years, graduating in 1989. He struggled a bit in school in the first year or two, until we stumbled upon the fact that his eyesight was an issue.
On the advice of an alert teacher, we took him to an optometrist and he emerged with the most amazed look on his face. He could actually see clearly for the first time in his life! He never had a problem in school after that day. John was always smart and a quick study, so he never spent much time hi tting the books. Nevertheless, his grades were always acceptable, despite his insatiable social appetite.
One of his first jobs as a teenager was working at Skippy’s, an ice-cream restaurant. It was a great experience for him, and he learned some valuable lessons there. T oday, we all know of John’s generous nature and his appetite to share with others. This admirable trait was noticeable even at an early age, when we learned that he was sharing part of his wages at Skippy’s with a homeless man who slept in the back of the restaurant. He later wrote a poignant college essay about this man that became a pivotal part of his collegiate application process. As John neared high school graduation, he and I made a tour of potential colleges for him to a ttend. We landed at Lehigh
University in Bethlehem, Pennsylvania, which turned out to be a perfect choice for him.
Mark attended Pine Crest through the sixth grade, when we moved to Baltimore. He attended McDonough School in Baltimore for two years and then finished up his high school career with four years at Shipley School in Philadelphia. Changing schools in mid-stream in junior high and high school is always tough on young students, but Mark’s vibrant, fun-loving personality served him well.
He never broke stride and adapted marvelously. Mark is extremely intelligent, but often drifted to the underperforming side, much like his brothers. Nevertheless, his grades also were always acceptable.
He’s been the instant life of any party and has been a virtual magnet for friends. His boundless sense of humor makes even the most serious people laugh. From as early as I can remember, Mark has been a sports fanatic. When he was only ten or eleven years old, he insisted on playing the football pools in my office and would o ften win the pot, to the amazement and consternation of my associates.
I think he must have been born loving the Dallas Cowboys! Like the other guys, he worked at several jobs while in high school. Mark considered a number of colleges on the eastern seaboard and settled on James Madison University in Harrisonburg, Virginia. JMU fit him well and was an excellent choice for him.
As these three guys completed their education and began their own careers and independent lives, they each set out in different directions. But, notwithstanding their stark di fferences in appearances and personalities, Dot and I began to notice a central theme with them —traits that had been important to us as we had nurtured them through the years. Of all the ways I could describe them and single out their a ttributes, the common trait that stands out prominently is that all three were paragons of character and competence as they matured to manhood. They had solid, well-developed core values —internal compasses by which they have steered their lives.
Keith, Mark, John and Paul in Washington, D.C.
Those same core values emerged for us to admire when they began to choose their spouses and life mates. Second only to our love for our sons is our love for their wives. They all managed to find and marry women who appear to be perfect matches and who have fostered their happiness for many years. Of course, they had an exceptional role model in their mother to form a pa ttern or road map! Seriously, I can see many of the same traits in Dawn, Sarah and Sue that resemble so many of Dot’s core values and personal characteristics. Each of the guys chose well and, as a result, have happy marriages and wonderful families.
Paul, John and Mark are widely different in so many respects—
different careers, di fferent interests and di fferent general outlooks.
One illustration comes to mind. Some time ago, Dot and I sat down and worked up a detailed list of all of our family a ffairs—financial,
assets, records, access codes, passwords, etc. We thought such a comprehensive document might be useful to our children once we are gone. So I typed it all up and sent copies to the three guys. Their responses were interesting —and telling. Paul sent back an email saying, “Dad, thanks for doing this. I have done something similar whenever I have been deployed to the Middle East. Dawn and I
are going to put this document in our safe and hope we never have to use it. ” On the same day, I received an email from John saying,
“Dad, is this all you have to do?” Then Mark followed with his usual banter: “Dad, please don’t send us any more crap like this. Just send money!” These comments are truly emblematic of the three personalities—Paul, the serious one; John, short and to the point;
Mark, who couldn’t resist the comedian opportunity.
Y et, the three share so many common traits. For example, all three have turned out to be exemplary fathers, dedicating a huge part of their lives to their children. It’s almost embarrassing to me as I watch their parenting and look back and wonder about my own investment in my children. As we observe our grandchildren, we notice glimpses of the same abiding characteristics —character and competence—sprouting as they grow up. It’s all so interesting.
Through the years I have enjoyed many special times with my boys, sometimes as a group and at other times one on one. One especially memorable time was a trip to Washington, D.C. with
Paul and John when they were about ten and seven, just the three of us wandering around the historic sites, staying at the Capitol
Hilton, eating at fancy restaurants and soaking up experiences and knowledge that have lasted for decades. On another occasion, I
remember taking Mark, the sports nut, to a Boston Red Sox game when he was about ten. It was a complete surprise to him until we entered the stadium, and I have indelible memories of his shock and excitement. I worked with Paul building a model railroad in his early teen years (some of which he still has today). I worked with John on boat maintenance so he could tool around with his friends in the
Fort Lauderdale waterways. And I tried hard to keep up with Mark’s obsession with sports trivia so that I could at least communicate! We have taken several “guy trips” or excursions over the years, including a couple of fishing trips in Costa Rica, where all four of us bonded together. A few years ago, we reunited as a foursome and went back to Washington, D.C., for a delightful weekend together.
With the guys scattered around the country—Denver, Chicago and Washington—it has been di fficult getting everyone together at the same time. But we’ve managed to do so a few times, and it has always been wonderful. The last such gathering was a family cruise,
when all thirteen of us spent a week together in the Caribbean celebrating Dot’s and my fiftieth wedding anniversary.
One thing I have particularly enjoyed over the years has been counseling with the guys about their careers. I suppose that may be partly because I devoted so much of my life to my own career and have a natural interest. Their careers vary greatly, but each is interesting to me, and I thoroughly enjoy counseling them as they travel along in their career journeys.
I recently was asked what I thought was the secret in raising three such upstanding and successful young men. I fumbled through some words. Sitting out on our dock that evening, Dot and
I pondered this question and came up with three basic things. First,
we insisted on thevery best education we could provide. Second,
we always treated them like real people, as contrasted to wards or subordinates. Third, we always tried to be consistent in our dealings with them. But actually it was a lot more than that. I think that once again serendipity played a major role. Time and again, we found in them traits and characteristics beyond what we were looking for.
They each are just fundamentally good guys and, to a large extent,
we have been along for the ride as they developed.
Paul, John and Mark truly have been God-sent blessings for Dot and me. They have been the source of immense pride and lots of fun over the years. We love them, and it’s so nice to know that the love is reciprocated. It is a wonderful feeling to know we will be leaving behind a legacy in three fine men who have incredible families,
admirable personal values and successful careers. Without a doubt,
each of them will easily eclipse anything I have ever done.

## Principles

- Prioritize providing the best possible education for children.
- Treat children with respect and as autonomous individuals.
- Be consistent in parenting and dealings with children.
- Support independence by stepping back when a child requests ownership of a decision.
- Act promptly on credible external observations about a child's needs (e.g., teacher concerns).

## Decision heuristics

- When a child explicitly requests to handle an important opportunity, step back and let them lead the process.
- Act on reliable external alerts (teachers or professionals) and obtain appropriate evaluations promptly.
- Provide the best available educational resources early and persistently to nurture potential.
- Document important family affairs clearly and share them with adult children to reduce future uncertainty.
