# P1_S35 – The Gift of Family

# The Gift of Family
**Story ID:** P1_S35
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1995–2012 (events described), Maintaining close family bonds and support across geographic distance and life changes., Sustain emotional closeness despite physical dista, Trust and rely on capable partners to manage famil, Expose children to varied environments to build ad, Use periodic family gatherings and celebrations to, Leverage modern communication technology to mainta

## Summary

A narrator reflects on family as the bedrock of life, describing children, spouses, grandchildren, and how love and regular contact have sustained close relationships despite geographic distance.

## Full Text

The Gift of Family
“ An ounce of blood is worth more than a pound of friendship.” —Spanish proverb
As I have reflected on my life in developing these memoirs, I have come to realize that family has been the bedrock pillar supporting my existence for the overwhelming majority of my lifetime. Much has been wri tten about family values and relationships, and I
suppose there is not much wisdom for me to add to the library. I will simply devote a few words describing the members of our family and the loving relationship we have developed.
Our family has always been close in spirit, yet sca ttered geographically. Therefore, unlike many families we know who live near one another and spend large chunks of their time together,
our face-to-face contact over the years has been sporadic. However,
distance has not seemed to us to be a stumbling block for our love and affection for one another.
Physical separation from our children began when Paul finished high school and le ft for the U.S. Air Force Academy, two thousand miles away from our home in Fort Lauderdale. Dot and I still remember seeing him walk down that ramp to the airplane. A while later, John left for college at Lehigh University and Mark at James
Madison University. They never returned home to live and have been scattered around the country ever since.
Paul’s first Air Force base after flight school was in Tucson,
Arizona. It was there he met a beautiful young woman named Dawn
Garrison. They fell in love and married there in Tucson. Dawn had dozens of wonderful qualities and proved to be the perfect mate for
Paul. T ogether they began to build their own family and have lived in six di fferent locations since their wedding. Having retired after twenty-three years as a pilot in the Air Force, Paul is now working as a project manager for Google. Dawn is a terrific, diversified woman.
Of all her a ttributes, I think I most admire how she took charge of their affairs and managed their family when Paul was deployed on several extended tours in the Middle East. She was a real trooper during those times. She is a cheerful, energetic and loving person who is a wonderful wife and mother. We love Dawn!
Miranda was the first of our grandchildren, born January 24,
1995. What a joy it has been to watch her grow and develop over the past twenty years. She has always been a good student and hard worker. She was also an excellent athlete in high school. She finished high school at the top of her large senior class and chose the University of Alabama for her college education. T oday, she is a junior and has been recognized as one of the most outstanding students on that large campus. She has truly developed into a most distinguished young woman. Dot and I love the fact that she stays in regular touch with us and visits us here in Florida from time to time.
Jordan was the next addition to the family, born May 18, 1998.
From the get-go, it was clear that Jordan might be destined to be most precocious of the Cobb family. She is naturally extremely bright and has always been at the top of her class. She has scored off the charts on all of the academic measuring sticks. She’s now a senior in high school and is in the process of choosing from a large sample of elite universities that all undoubtedly will be anxious to admit her. Currently she is interested in a career in nursing. Aside from her unique intellect, she is a charming and energetic young woman who is just as delightful to us and as fun to be around as her older sister.
She seems to have plucked the most important virtues of both her mother and father in the gene auction.
We believe that both Miranda and Jordan have actually benefitted from moving every three or four years. Changing venues so many times has made them more adaptable, independent and self-confident young women, in our view, than they might have been otherwise.
John, after working in Pennsylvania and Florida for a short while,
moved to Chicago, where he has settled for the past fifteen years. He now is a senior executive with Chicago-based Ventas Corporation.
He was most fortunate to meet Sarah Meier, who was living in the same apartment building when he arrived there. What a discovery for John! They married and began their lives together, both working in professional careers. Sarah initially worked as a consultant for
PricewaterhouseCoopers and later moved to Goldman Sachs. After receiving her MBA from Northwestern University, she served for a few years as the CEO of a large non-pro fit organization there in
Chicago. She is a delightful young woman, with a heart of gold, and she has been a remarkably wonderful mate for John. Sarah quickly became a certified member of the Cobb clan!
Avery was the next gi ft to the Cobb family. She was born in
Chicago on January 27, 2007. It’s been so much fun to watch Avery grow and develop in their urban environment over the past eight years. John and Sarah have been fabulous parents and have exposed
Avery to a world of diversity. She now is a student at the prestigious
Latin School and is performing exceptionally well academically.
Avery is an exceptionally bright child with a loving disposition. She is also quite skilled in a variety of activities, including ice skating,
gymnastics, dance and sailing. Dot and I have thoroughly enjoyed the opportunities we have had to visit with her from time to time in
Chicago and their weekend place in Lake Geneva, Wisconsin. Avery is a winner, no doubt about it!
Mark began his career with Enterprise Rent A Car in Washington,
D.C., later transferring to Colorado to live out his outdoor dreams.
After a while, he shi fted employment to the online education industry, where he still works as an executive. In the process, he had the extremely good fortune to stumble upon an extraordinary young woman named Sue Nickerson, who was working in the same company at the time. Mark and Sue were kindred spirits with interests that fit like a glove. They soon found themselves married and building a life together. Like our other daughters-in-law, Sue is a beautiful, smart and loving young woman. She is always cheerful,
upbeat and likeable—a genuine pleasure to be with. Dot and I have enjoyed a wonderful relationship. Sue is a winner, just like the others!
Allison appeared in our lives as our fourth granddaughter on
September 22, 2008. She was a most beautiful child from thevery beginning. T oday, she is in the first grade at school and growing like a weed. Ally, as we all call her, has an indescribable lovely disposition.
She is cute, sweet, upbeat and seemingly always happy. There doesn’t appear to be a single negative bone in her body. I suppose some of her obvious goodness is taken from her mother, who shares many of her lovely traits. Dot and I get a kick out of visiting with Ally on
FaceTime, seeing her perpetual cheerful smiling face and listening to her peppy voice!
And then along comes Ethan Keith Cobb on March 15, 2012.
Our first grandson! I was touched by the selection of his middle name, of course, but even more touched seeing him emerge as all
The entire family celebrating our fiftieth wedding anniversary in the
Caribbean boy, so di fferent from his sister and cousins. Although he’s only three years old now, he is already showing remarkable athletic skills and a keen interest in sports. Perhaps he may be just as precocious athletically as Jordan has been academically. Ethan is an unusually handsome little guy and is an incredible ball of energy. He never stops moving. Ethan is lots of fun to be with!
That rounds out the thirteen members of our immediate family. We are physically spread out from Florida to Washington,
D.C. to Chicago to Denver, but we manage to stay in regular close touch through the beauty of modern communications technology.
Occasionally, we are able to assemble everyone at the same time for a family gathering. The last of these times was a Caribbean cruise celebrating our fiftieth wedding anniversary, a wonderful time together.
Like so much of our lives, Dot and I marvel at how trouble-free our family has been. Everyone is healthy, happy and financially comfortable, and we’ve all been spared so much of the misery and problems that plague so many families. I doubt there is a formula for our happiness. Perhaps it is just the luck of the draw. Whatever the root, the vine is healthy and happy and the fruit is abundant. We are indeed overjoyed to have such a wonderful, loving family.
I am also extremely grateful for my relationship with Sandy,
my terrific sister, who has been a loyal supporter and con fidant for my entire life. I love her and cherish the great relationship we have shared.
I recently saw a Desmond Tutu quote that resonated with me:
“Y ou don’t choose your family. They are God’s gift to you, as you are to them. ” I truly regard my family as an extraordinary gift.

## Principles

- Sustain emotional closeness despite physical distance.
- Trust and rely on capable partners to manage family responsibilities during absences.
- Expose children to varied environments to build adaptability, independence and confidence.
- Use periodic family gatherings and celebrations to reinforce connections.
- Leverage modern communication technology to maintain regular contact.
- Express gratitude and regard family as a precious gift.

## Decision heuristics

- When family members live apart, maintain frequent contact using modern communications to preserve closeness.
- When raising children, introduce relocations and new environments periodically to build adaptability and independence.
- When a partner is deployed or absent, empower the other partner to take charge of family affairs and trust their management.
- Organize occasional full-family gatherings or milestone celebrations to strengthen bonds and create shared memories.

---

# P1_S36 – Charles T . Brumfield

# Charles T. Brumfield
**Story ID:** P1_S36
**Part:** One
**Tags:** autumn of 1959 onward, higher education / academia, Maintaining a deep, enduring friendship and mutual respect despite different personalities, political views, life choices, and physical distance., Cultivate enduring friendships through mutual resp, Leverage complementary strengths within a team: vi, Place trusted people in roles of responsibility, Respect divergent viewpoints while holding to shar, Prioritize experience and independence over materi

## Summary

A reflection on a fifty-year friendship between the narrator and Charles Brumfield, tracing their college collaboration, long correspondence, pranks, and Brumfield’s adventurous, cosmopolitan life.

## Full Text

Charles T . Brumfield
Two unlikely kindred spirits stumbled onto the campus of
Mississippi Southern College in the autumn of 1959, neither of them realizing the presence of the other. One was from the Biloxi-
Gulfport area on the Gulf of Mexico, the other from the red clay hills of Calhoun County in the northern part of the state. They hailed from di fferent backgrounds and sported di fferent personalities.
Both, however, were naïve to many of the ways of the world, but eager to learn. Somehow their paths crossed during that freshman year, just how and when is unrecorded or unremembered by either.
Their friendship has lasted for more than a half century.
Charlie Brumfield was a quiet sort of guy who kept pretty much to himself in those early days, but had a familiarity with a few other freshmen from the Gulf Coast area, which gave him a modicum of a built-in network. I, on the other hand, was a lone import from the north, knew no one, and had to quickly begin to make friends in my dormitory and on campus. We both were assigned to live in Forest
Hall, a three-story brick dormitory that was one of the original buildings on the campus. As time passed that freshman year, it turned out that some of our friends were the same. Whatever the catalyst, Keith and Charlie somehow befriended each other. Along the way, we joined forces working in the Student Government
Association and eventually found ourselves taking courses in the business school. By the time our senior year rolled around, we were rooming together in Bond Hall.
As I wrote earlier, I was a busy guy in college, active in numerous organizations and heavily engaged in student politics. Charlie was less busy and involved than I was. However, he was considerably more thoughtful and studious, and he served as a guiding compass for me much of the time. We both found ourselves involved in student politics and we shared similar outlooks on the welfare of the student body. When I was elected president of the SGA, I
immediately appointed Charlie as my administrative assistant. We made a great team. It seemed as though I was always on the stage and he was always in the wings, keeping my head straight. He seemed to like it better that way than if he had been in the spotlight. We werevery different individuals in many respects, but we shared a mutual admiration for each other.
When Dot and I began to plan our wedding, my choice for best man was a slam dunk. It was to be Charlie Brumfield, my best friend.
I’ve never had a better friend. We have remained close and stayed in touch, despite the physical distance between us.
Being best friends did not preclude a bit of horseplay and practical joking from time to time. I recall one example of a time when I probably stepped over the line. Charlie was still in graduate school at USM. He had been the president of the Y oung Republicans
Club in college and was a fervent Republican devotee. (One would never recognize that radical conservative leaning today!) When Paul
Johnson was a Democratic candidate for governor of Mississippi, I
sat down and wrote a letter to his campaign office, pledging support for his campaign and forging Charlie’s signature. I volunteered, on
Charlie’s behalf, to work on the Johnson campaign and requested literature for distribution. Charlie was immediately bombarded with cases of bumper stickers, brochure mailers, signs and other campaign paraphernalia. Indignant and smelling a rat, he called me to protest and complain. In response, I wrote a follow-up letter to the Johnson headquarters saying that I had used up all the materials and requesting another supply. The box arrived at Charlie’s place promptly. I’m still laughing. And I am sure Charlie is still complaining!
After receiving his master’s degree in economics at USM, Charlie went on to achieve a doctorate in economics at the University of
South Carolina. Y ears later, he wrote a book about economic theory,
Basic Macroeconomic Equilibrium Analysis . Although I don’t think he ever published it, I seem to recall that he did submit it to some publishers. He sent me a copy of the manuscript, which I still have stowed away somewhere. The book’s contents were well over my head, and I never did anything with it except scan it and gloss over the chapters—and I did take it upon myself to write a faux New York
Times Book Review-style critique lampooning it. If I must say so, the review was well done. Satirizing the book, I likened it to an a ttempt to prove Fermat’s theorem and wrote, “Brumfield seems to be trying to prove scientifically that nature will take its course —if not in the short run, then certainly in the long run. Wow!” I even went to the trouble of printing it with standard New York Times Book Review section columns and fonts to make it look authentic. Although he never admitted it, I think I might have had him going for a short while. I still have that book review in my bulging file of Charlie mementos.
Such was some of the fun we had together over the years.
While working in Germany in the early years, Charlie met and married a woman named Margot. That relationship foundered after a few years, but in the meantime they had a terri fic son they named
Chuck. Chuck became the joy of Charlie’s life. Charlie moved to
California to be with him, and essentially raised him over a period of about twenty years; Margot was around in the background, but it was
Charlie who provided the critical parenting, it seemed to me. Chuck spent some time as a radio disc jockey in the San Francisco area and is now an executive in the music industry and quite successful.
Charlie taught at the university level in South Carolina and
California. He also had two long stints in a relationship with the
University of Maryland’s overseas extension service. In that capacity,
he lived and taught all over the world. I have lost count, but he has probably lived in twenty different countries over the years and most likely has visited well over one hundred.
Sometime during Chuck’s teenage years, Charlie and he acquired a dog they named Dum Dum. Somehow Chuck managed to teach Dum Dum to “talk” , muttering phrases that were intelligible,
albeit barely so. They gained some notoriety with this talking dog and eventually finagled an appearance on the Johnny Carson Show in Hollywood. So here they were, with Dum Dum talking on nationwide television. It was hilarious! Eventually, Dum Dum died.
Charlie, being the eccentric he was becoming by that time, had Dum
Dum freeze-dried and preserved in a natural state. I actually saw the frozen dog in the trunk of his car during one of my trips to California.
Spooky. I’m guessing the dog is still sitting, all posed and relaxed, in some storage facility somewhere in California.
Following college, Charlie and I launched a correspondence that has lasted for more than fifty years. Sometimes the communications were quite regular; at other times they were spo tty. Usually,
particularly in the earlier years, the letters back and forth were filled with repartee. Our political views were normally at opposite ends of the spectrum, and we reveled in the opportunity to parody each other’s views. The spoofs were o ften sharp-tongued, but we both understood the good nature of the exchange. At one point, Charlie crafted a compendium of cut-and-pasted excerpts from my le tters over the years and bound them up in a book-like folder. As could be expected, the excerpts were taken out of context and cleverly constructed to present me in a ridiculous light. I still have copies of all of his letters stored away in my attic. Perhaps someday I’ll do my own cut-and-paste job!
Charlie and I have sparred over the years about politics. Generally speaking, I have followed a traditional conservative pathway, and
Charlie has leaned to the left. Notwithstanding the polarized politics and in a more serious vein, we have always shared a common set of fundamental values, which has probably been the bedrock of our friendship. We have both valued honesty and integrity and have respected the people we have known. My sense of right and wrong has almost always dovetailed with Charlie’s.
Our face-to-face visits over the years have been short and infrequent. Charlie has visited our home a few times. He drove clear across the country once, in his old beat-up Volkswagen van, to visit with our family on vacation in Nantucket. He also was kind and thoughtful enough to a ttend Paul’s wedding in Arizona. He always brought gifts on his visits, which drove me crazy. I didn’t need any gifts. But that was Charlie! Dot and I visited him once in California.
Once when I was in Berlin on business with Alamo Rent A Car, I
notified Charlie, who travelled all the way from somewhere in the
Netherlands to enable us to spend a couple of days together. Dot and
I met him in Brussels once and spent a few hours visiting, before we had to head back to London.
Charlie has been a full-time vagabond, wandering the globe for more than fifty years. He is the consummate cosmopolitan.
Germany, Belgium, South Korea, Okinawa and Thailand are just a few of the many places he has lived. He has visited every major edifice in the world. He’s climbed Mt. Kilimanjaro, has dived in waters everywhere, ridden horses through Mongolia and done so many other adventurous things that they are too numerous to recite. Indeed, it has been a full and glamorous life, at least from my perspective. He never seems to tire of adventure. A few years ago,
well past age sixty, he fell in love and married a young Thai woman in her twenties. I hope she loves adventure! What a guy!
For as long as I have known Charlie, he has never been focused on material things. He lives for the moment and seems to be interested in accumulating only what he might need for the next moment. He occasionally grumbles about being poor and financially inadequate,
but I have always known that his grumbling is just a front. He is secretly proud of his independence and his ability to survive amidst all the adventure of his fun-loving existence.
Charlie claims that he admires my life and all of the things I have done over the years. I certainly have admired his. He has been the
Indiana Jones that I, like many men, have only dreamed about. He has marched to his own drummer and done his own thing, with little or no ambition for wealth and fame, or even personal security. He’s just been Charlie, roaming the world in search of adventure and the personal satisfaction that come from being a professor and teacher. I
have truly admired those characteristics and count myself extremely fortunate to have known him as a friend. Members of his family refer to him as T ommy. Most of his friends and associates call him
Charles. T o me, he will always be Charlie, best friend forever.
Charlie has indeed been an unusual kindred spirit for a very long time. Whenever I think of all the things in the world that I am grateful for, I put Charles T . Brumfield at or near the top of the list.
Charlie Brum field, college days
Charlie Brum field, after fifty years of adventure

## Principles

- Cultivate enduring friendships through mutual respect and shared values.
- Leverage complementary strengths within a team: visible leader and steady behind-the-scenes support.
- Place trusted people in roles of responsibility.
- Respect divergent viewpoints while holding to shared ethical foundations.
- Prioritize experience and independence over material accumulation.

## Decision heuristics

- Appoint people to roles that match their temperament and let them operate behind the scenes.
- Sustain long-term relationships through regular correspondence despite geographical distance.
- Use humor and parody to test, challenge, and strengthen bonds across ideological differences.
- Choose life experiences and personal independence over seeking wealth and fame for fulfillment.

# P1_S37 – T o God Be The Glory

# To God Be The Glory
**Story ID:** P1_S37
**Part:** One
**Tags:** mid-20th century through early 21st century (1950s–2000s), religious / church community, How to live out, sustain, and express a lifelong Christian commitment through personal choices, family decisions, and public leadership responsibilities., Spiritual formation is rooted in early, Mentorship and pastoral presence can catalyze deci, Stewardship entails both time and money as obligat, Active participation in religious programs deepens, Gratitude frames lifelong spiritual perspective

## Summary

A personal testimony recounting lifelong Christian formation beginning with childhood VBS, a pivotal conversion at age eleven, sustained service in church leadership, exploration of other faiths, and a conviction of stewardship and gratitude.

## Full Text

T o God Be The Glory
The First Presbyterian Church of Fort Lauderdale has a special dinner for elders and trustees in January of each year. At this dinner, the newly elected members are expected to stand and tell the group about their religious experience. The charge is to present one’s personal testimony, as it is called in Baptist churches. It is an emotional time for most people —both the presenters and the audience members —and most of the speakers are nervous and anxious. I have sat through several of these evenings during my years as a church elder, and invariably come away with a warm glow after hearing so many beautiful stories. My story has been presented twice, and the following paragraphs re flect much of the message I
have attempted to relate.
I have been associated with the church all of my life. My Christian faith has been a cornerstone of who I am as a person. From as early as I can remember, my mother hustled me o ff to the First Baptist
Church on Sundays and to all of the special programs sponsored by the church—Sunday School, T raining Union, Royal Ambassadors,
etc. We were usually there when the doors opened.
A highlight of every summer was Vacation Bible School (VBS).
It is uncanny how the messages of those summer programs remain lodged in my mind. We attended classes, sang songs, played games and involved ourselves with various cra fts. My favorite cra ft was building birdhouses. I always painted mine bright red. But VBS was not all fun and games. We also learned to recite the sixty-six books of the Holy Bible and memorized scripture verses. I can still visualize the colored cards —green, purple, yellow, orange, etc. —with printed memory verses and rote memory exercises. I remember once memorizing the entire chapter of Ephesians 6, all twenty-three verses. I still think of those days whenever I hear words or phrases like “the armor of God” or “breastplate of righteousness” . I remember maps of the Holy Land, pictures of Christ and the apostles, and larger-than-life stories of Bible characters —King Solomon, King
David, Samson, the Apostle Paul and many others. I remember learning and reciting the pledge of allegiance to the flags—both the
American flag and the Christian flag. The moderator would intone
“Attention, salute, pledge,” as we all lined up at the front of the sanctuary. There was a lot of meat in that VBS sandwich!
In 1952, when I was eleven years old, all of those messages from Sunday School, T raining Union and VBS began to come together and take shape at VBS during an intense week of Bible drills, scripture memorization, music, learning projects, etc. I can remember going home near the end of that week and talking with my mother and father about believing in Christ as my savior and what that meant. At the time, my father was not a Christian and,
accordingly, he was not much help. However, he seemed unusually interested, and he involved himself in the discussions. My mother,
who also felt ill prepared for the discussion, invited our minister,
Brother Clarence Cutrell, to come by our home and counsel with us.
As I re flect on this event, I can visualize Brother Cutrell’s piercing eyes, hear his gentle voice with so serious a tone, and feel his firm hand on my shoulder. He seemed to understand me. He made me feel so special that day, and I sensed that he had been sent by God to see me. Perhaps he had. The upshot of this experience was that my father and I both prayed to receive Christ into our lives at the same time. We were baptized together the following week. That day has been an abiding special memory for me for the past sixty years, for it was at that time that I formed an eternal bond with Christ and also a special bond with my father.
Despite being a callow eleven-year-old, I clearly recognized my profession of faith as a life-altering experience. I regarded my action as a firm commitment. No turning back. T oday, I’m pleased that my life is still guided by that pivotal commitment and the knowledge that it will always be so directed.
Dot and I were exposed to the same Christian environment and were grounded in the same fundamentals. We exchanged our marriage vows at the altar of the First Baptist Church. Throughout our marriage, we have struggled mightily to keep those vows, as well as the fundamentals that we learned in the church at a young age.
We have attempted to provide the same Christian leadership to our children that our parents and our church gave to us. God has blessed our lives immensely.
Much has been said and written about standing on the shoulders of giants, and I can name a number of giants in my spiritual background. Brother Cutrell stands at the head of that line. It was through his tutelage and leadership that I began to take spirituality seriously. There have been many others —some I knew personally and some I knew only from a distance. Some of my favorite pastors whom I have known and admired personally were Reverend J.C.
Mitchell of First Baptist Church in Winter Park, Florida; Dr. D.
James Kennedy of Coral Ridge Presbyterian Church; and Dr.
Douglas Brouwer of First Presbyterian in Fort Lauderdale.
I should also mention a special place in my heart for Reverend
Joseph Sellinger, a Jesuit priest who I came to know when I lived in
Baltimore. Father Sellinger (he was also called Father Joe) was truly an ecumenical man —the apotheosis of a servant of God. I believe he was closer to God than any person I have ever known. Sitting with him in his living room on the Loyola University campus, where he was president, was like being lifted out of reality and into another realm.
It was truly a “thin place” , an expression in early Celtic Christianity describing a mesmerizing place where the distance between heaven and earth collapses. This man le ft an enormous impression on me.
Even though I moved to Philadelphia, I travelled back to Baltimore in 1993 to attend his funeral, along with a thousand other admirers of this great man.
Descriptions of other Christian giants I have admired and respected, people on whose shoulders I have stood, would fill an entire chapter in this memoir.
I have participated in a wide range of Christian service areas over the years. These include teaching Sunday School, singing in choirs,
serving on commi ttees, completing intense Bethel Bible study programs, attending men’s Bible studies, making educational trips to Israel and participating in scores of other programs and spiritual growth activities over the years. At First Presbyterian, I have served two terms as an elder and chaired the personnel commi ttee for a couple of years. In the 2005–2006 era, I chaired a successful capital campaign that raised over $16 million for new facilities. Every single one of these activities has enriched my life and served to enhance my faith.
I have always been curious about organized religion and occasionally have spent time trying to learn what the other major religions are all about. Y ears ago, when we were on vacation in
Hawaii, a Mormon missionary stopped me on the street and engaged me in a polite discussion about the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter Day Saints. He gave me a copy of the Book of Mormon. On the long trip home, I read the entire book, cover to cover, and found it fascinating. While I thought some of it was questionable, I came to realize that most of the message is sound and makes a lot of sense.
Another time, when I was in upstate New Y ork and paid a visit to a Buddhist temple, a monk sat down with me for two hours trying to explain the fundamentals of Buddhism. For some odd reason, I
was surprised at how reasonable Buddhism is, too. I have also read a number of books on comparative religion. One interesting scholarly study was a comparison of Christianity, Judaism and Islam in a book called From Moses to Mohammed by Jean Pierre Isbouts. I spent a lot of interesting hours with Isbouts on a cruise in Africa. One of the main points of this book is that much of the Christian Old T estament shows up almost verbatim in the Koran. All of this has enriched my understanding of the world’s religions and made me more respectful of different points of view. It has also helped dispel my notions that religions other than mine are somehow kooky or spooky. However,
none of it has ever pried me away from my Christian fundamentals.
Those are my roots, and I draw my strength from them.
We have found the Holy Land a fascinating and absorbing place to visit. As we toured the countryside with an expert Israeli guide, all that I had learned in those Sunday School and Vacation Bible School days suddenly sprang to life. But the realities of these places were often different from what I had read in stories, heard in sermons, and seen in paintings and illustrations.
I had imagined the countryside to be much larger that it actually is, and the landscape to be much different. I learned that Bethlehem was a suburb of Jerusalem, only six miles outside the main gates of the city. Shocking. Likewise, Jericho was only seventeen miles away. And I never realized that Jericho was simply a lonely oasis, a green spot poking up out of a desolate desert, surrounded by barren sand as far as the eye can see. I had heard so much about the Jordan
River and had always visualized it as a major navigable waterway. In fact, it turned out to be a stream that one can wade across in places.
In Mississippi, we called those creeks! And the Sea of Galilee is a relatively small lake, about one-tenth the size of Lake Okeechobee in Florida. Notwithstanding these physical revelations, the spiritual significance of the Holy Land was very moving. I have deep and abiding memories of standing on the shores of the Sea of Galilee,
walking along the Via Dolorosa, si tting on a bench in the Garden of Gethsemane, visiting the Mount of Olives and seeing so many other remarkable sights. For any Christian, these are unforge ttable experiences.
For more than fifty years, Dot and I have been keenly conscious of the unusual blessings God has bestowed upon us. This awareness has been a potent reminder of an obligation we have both felt to stay grounded in our faith. We also have a conviction that we have a stewardship responsibility to others. Throughout our adult lives,
we have tried to go the extra mile in supporting our church and the community at large. And we have enjoyed every minute of it. We believe we are called upon to be contributors of both our time and our money —payback—and we a ttempt to consistently live up to that core belief.
Although my personal commitment to Christ has never wavered,
I must acknowledge that the level of my personal Christian service has vacillated over time. We have belonged to several churches as we moved around the country, and some periods of my life have been more active than others. I have sometimes likened my activity to an oscillating fan moving from side to side. Occasionally the air faded away a bit and sometimes it blew strongly right in my face. But the soft, cool breeze of God’s presence in my life has always been there—a steady, comforting feeling for me.
The basic story of my spiritual experience is one of gratitude. I am incredibly grateful for so many ways in which God has blessed my life.
Through the leadership of many Christian mentors, I have learned about the immensity and greatness of God. Through his grace, I have been blessed with so many gi fts, some of which I admi ttedly have managed to squander along the way. I have matured as a Christian over the years as I studied and participated in organized religious programs. But my roots are still planted in the fundamentals drilled into me at Vacation Bible School at the Calhoun City First Baptist
Church more than sixty years ago. The message is the same. The fundamentals have not changed. I just believe I understand them a little better. Simply put, I am grateful.
“T o God be the glory. Great things he hath done. ”

## Principles

- Spiritual formation is rooted in early, repeated practices and rituals.
- Mentorship and pastoral presence can catalyze decisive commitments.
- Stewardship entails both time and money as obligations to community and faith.
- Active participation in religious programs deepens faith and personal growth.
- Gratitude frames lifelong spiritual perspective.

## Decision heuristics

- When a trusted spiritual leader engages, allow space for counsel and follow through on commitments.
- Solidify pivotal commitments through public ritual to mark and sustain the decision.
- Accept leadership responsibilities when motivated by stewardship and ability to contribute time and resources.
- Learn about other faiths through direct reading and conversation to increase respect and correct misconceptions.

---

# P1_S38 – For Good. For Ever.

# For Good. For Ever.
**Story ID:** P1_S38
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1963–2013 (examples span 1963, 1980s, and 2013), Accounting (Peat Marwick) and nonprofit/community organizations, Whether and how much to commit time, money, and leadership to community organizations—balancing personal/professional life with sustained, escalating public service., Sustained, Volunteer leadership roles are opportunities to ap, Combine monetary support with time and leadership, Adopt an ethic of reciprocity—'pay it forward'—as

## Summary

A personal account of lifelong commitment to community service through sustained financial giving, board leadership, volunteer work, and the ethic of "paying it forward."

## Full Text

For Good. For Ever.
“We make a living by what we do, but we make a life by what we give. ” That sage quotation by Winston Churchill fairly well sums up my fundamental philosophy about community service. From our earliest days, Dot and I have made community service a cornerstone of our lives. Wherever we have lived, we have tried to be generous in the giving of both our money and our time for the bene fit of the better good.
I suppose the concept of community service goes all the way back to my Boy Scout days, when service to others was a basic part of the learning. As one early example of this, I remember dressing up in my brown long-sleeved uniform and accompanying the local troop on a rickety bus to Mississippi State University football games,
where we walked up and down the stadium steps in the sweltering heat, serving as ushers for the fans a ttending the game. Granted,
there was a quid pro quo in this service; we got to watch the game.
Nevertheless, it was voluntary work and it was a service —helping people find their seats. And I saw it as just that.
All through college, I was deeply involved in all sorts of public service organizations, many of them centered on the Student
Government Association and student a ffairs. Upon joining Peat
Marwick, it became clear that management of the firm expected everyone to be involved in the community in some way. Oddly,
despite the clarity of this message, most just ignored it and went on with their personal lives. Having been preconditioned, I saddled up and finagled my way into a few things. I knew that such things could be interesting and quickly realized that they were an excellent way to meet people and make friends. They also generated a feeling,
important to me, of having done something worthwhile.
The most continuous charitable endeavor for us over the years has been the United Way. Our financial contributions to this organization started with a modest payroll deduction from my first paycheck at Peat Marwick in 1963 and have continued uninterrupted ever since. The United Way of Broward County recently recognized us, with a degree of local hoopla, for over fifty consecutive years of support to various United Ways where we have lived.
We gradually escalated our United Way contributions over time, trying to do a little be tter every year. And we’ve had a few nudges along the way. Once, back in the 1980s, Bob Elmore, a local businessman, called on me at my office and suggested an increase in our annual contribution. I responded, “Why Bob, we’ve been giving
$1,000 a year!” He said, “Keith, I already know that, but it’s just not enough. We both know you can do more, and I’m asking you to consider doing that. ” So, I doubled our pledge. A few years later, in
Philadelphia, with a similar nudge by Pete Musser, a client, we again bumped up our pledge, this time to $10,000. We have contributed at least $10,000 every year since. We have belonged to the United
Way’s T ocqueville Society for more than twenty years now.
Our involvement, however, has been more than just monetary contributions. We have contributed our time and energy as well. I
served on the board in Broward County for several years and was chairman for two years. Dot served on the executive commi ttee of the T ocqueville Society and founded a United Way agency in
Baltimore. We have hosted a number of United Way events in our home and attended countless events to support the organization.
We like to believe our personal support of United Way has made a difference in our community.
We have also supported our local church during that same period of time. We gradually increased our contributions over the years as our financial position improved. At one time or another,
we have been involved in all aspects of the various churches that we have attended. Most of my involvement has been focused on financial and administrative ma tters, while Dot has concentrated on music programs and women’s activities. We’ve spent many, many hours behind those church doors and, again, we like to believe we have made solid contributions to the various congregations.
Along the way, I was also involved in other community organizations, usually at the behest of friends. I served as chairman of the Museum of Science and Discovery board when the beautiful downtown museum in Fort Lauderdale was conceived and constructed. I also served as chairman of the Broward Library
Foundation during its formative stages. Following that gig, I
was asked to join the board of the Community Foundation of
Broward and ended up as chairman for a couple of years. I was a charter member of the Broward Workshop, a business coalition of corporate CEOs, and remain a member today, thirty years later. And so the story has evolved over the years, serving as a board member or volunteer in dozens of organizations, which have ranged from the local Kidney Foundation to the Federal Reserve Board. More recently, my leadership roles have subsided a bit, but commi ttee work continues with the United Way, the Community Foundation,
The Performing Arts Center and First Presbyterian Church. I am also called on from time to time to head up citizen commi ttees for the City of Fort Lauderdale. Community service has actually become a way of life for me.
And it has not all been organizational work. In 2013, for example,
I travelled to Oklahoma City following the horrible tornado there and spent a week in the blazing sun helping with the cleanup. I came home physically weary but with a wonderful inner feeling, a sense that I had done some good work for some good people in need.
Along the way, community service has had the e ffect of enhancing my leadership skills. As I slogged my way through all of these organizations and activities, I was able to bring some of my business training and experience to bear on the organizations—but
I learned a lot from them in return. Community leadership roles have taught me a lot about motivating the behavior of people, setting definitive goals and ge tting things done. Many well-intentioned volunteers are essentially inept at organizational work. Accordingly,
working with them is a virtual laboratory for leadership. I learned a great deal about running an e fficient meeting, about compromise and about so many other things that I might have missed otherwise.
In other words, I gained some extremely valuable skills from these associations.
I also gained some wonderful friends and relationships. Many of my closest friends today can be traced back to mutual community service activity. The same is true for many of my business relationships. That has never been a raison d’être for my involvement,
but it has certainly proved to be a fringe benefit.
The term “pay it forward” has been popularized in recent years to characterize the receipt of a good deed and then repaying the gesture to someone other than the original benefactor. As Ralph Waldo
Emerson wrote in 1841, “In the order of nature we cannot render benefits to those from whom we receive them, or only seldom. But the benefit we receive must be rendered again, line for line, deed for deed, cent for cent, to somebody. ” Although it might sound cheesy,
Dot and I have adopted that as our essential creed throughout the years. We completely recognize the good fortune that has rained upon us and see ourselves as privileged indeed. Most of the world is not nearly as fortunate. We have sensed a responsibility to “pay it forward” and, hence, our immersion in so much community service over the years.
My study and my closet are filled with photos, plaques and mementos presented in recognition of our community service. But those are not nearly as valuable as the memories packed inside our hearts and the notion that we have played a role in making the little world around us a better place for others.
The Community Foundation of Broward has a catchy slogan that they use in all their literature: “For Good. For Ever. ” I like that a lot.

## Principles

- Sustained, consistent giving builds long-term community impact.
- Volunteer leadership roles are opportunities to apply professional skills and to learn leadership.
- Combine monetary support with time and leadership for greater impact.
- Adopt an ethic of reciprocity—'pay it forward'—as a guiding motive for service.

## Decision heuristics

- When company culture expects community involvement, participate to build networks and experience.
- When a trusted peer asks you to increase your support, consider promptly raising your pledge.
- Use board service to transfer business expertise to nonprofits and treat it as a learning laboratory.
- When disaster relief needs hands, volunteer on-site even if physically demanding.
- Gradually escalate financial support as personal financial position improves.

---

# P1_S39 – Moments to Remember

# Moments to Remember
**Story ID:** P1_S39
**Part:** One
**Tags:** 1950s–1990s (life events span mid-20th to late-20th century), professional services / accounting, How to organize and make sense of a lifetime of experiences while confronting mortality, commitments, and the need to acknowledge others' contributions., Acknowledge and be grateful for the support and in, Maintain consistent goal-setting to drive long-ter, Use retrospection in later life to clarify meaning, Accept serendipity as a meaningful factor in life, Treat major commitments with seriousness and inten

## Summary

A memoirist reflects on a series of memorable personal moments across his life—marriage, education, family vacations, births and losses—and describes a late-life turn to retrospection, gratitude, and sense-making.

## Full Text

Moments to Remember
When one begins to look back and try to document a lifetime of experiences, the methodology of the exercise sometimes becomes confusing. Does a year-by-year chronology make sense? Should the memories be grouped topically? Should they be associated with particular people or places? Should things be ranked in some sort of priority of importance? What is the best way to organize things? Thinking about this, I have decided that random is as good a methodology as any.
On a long flight one day, I decided to use the idle time to reflect on my life and on this memoir, which was a work-in-progress and to make a list of some of the most memorable moments in my life. The list is still incomplete, but consists of an eclectic mix of experiences that have ground their way into my memory bank and serve as touch points for my emotional nerve center. Many of those moments are chronicled in this memoir. I will attempt to summarize a few examples of the highlights in the following paragraphs. There is no particular order to the moments, just a random recollection of nostalgic pieces stuffed into the corners of my mind.
There is only place to begin the list. It is with my marriage to
Dot. By a huge margin this was the most pivotal point in my life. I
stepped out of a world of independence into a lifetime partnership. I
was indeed so fortunate to have found a soul mate and, ironically, just down the street from my previous existence. As I stood at the altar in the First Baptist Church in 1963, my mind raced a million miles an hour thinking of the transformation that was taking place. Though a bit nervous, I listened to the vows carefully and took them seriously.
I loved this woman with all my heart and realized I was making an irrevocable commitment—possibly the only one I had ever made up until that point. If I should ever contract Alzheimer’s disease and lose my memory, I suspect this will be the last experience of my life that I would forget. It was the most special day of my life.
College life was a special time in my life —so many experiences to recall. Probably the most significant was graduation day. This was a distinctively emotional time for both my family and me. I was the first person on either side of the family to graduate from college.
That observation included parents, grandparents, aunts, uncles and cousins. That uniqueness was not lost on me. I felt both fortunate and proud. I felt fortunate because I was undoubtedly the first one who ever had the opportunity to attend college. I was proud because
I had worked hard and not only graduated, but also graduated with honors.
Aside from graduation day, the other most memorable occasion in college was the day I was elected president of the Student
Government Association. My emotions were all over the place. I felt a sense of pride that five thousand people had elected me as their leader. A duality flooded over me, as I realized that this euphoria came along with a responsibility to live up to their expectations.
There was a strong sense of gratitude for all the support I had in the process. Optimism overwhelmed me as I thought of all the good things I should be able to accomplish with the newfound power and authority. I can still think about that day and feel all those disparate emotions converge inside me.
The day we learned of Dot’s breast cancer was a traumatic one.
If one had asked me, I would have said that I understood illness and mortality; however, the honest fact was that I had never spent any time reflecting on the tragedies that so many people deal with. All of a sudden, we were faced with this looming reality. What were we to do? It was simultaneously confusing and scary. I will never forget that day.
Family vacations are special times in family memory bank.
There were lots of them over the years. One of the most memorable was a motor home trip we took out West in about 1982. We rented a twenty-six-foot motor home from our friend Roy Letourneau in
T exas and spent three weeks touring Colorado, Arizona and New
Mexico. We visited the Grand Canyon, Carlsbad Caverns, Mesa
Verde and all the other famous sites and a ttractions along the way.
Our children have wonderful memories of this trip —the sites, the campgrounds, the campfires, and family songs as we moved over the highways. Such wonderful memories!
There were also several special holidays along the way that trigger emotional tugs. One that rises to the top of the list was a Christmas in Williamsburg, Virginia. There is really nothing else like it in the
United States. T ruly spectacular! We stayed at the magni ficent
Williamsburg Inn, which we had to book a year in advance because of its popularity. We felt like we had actually rolled the clock back two hundred years and were living in colonial times for a few days.
Close to this experience was another Christmas when Dot and
I travelled to Banff, Canada, and spent the holidays at the fabulous
Banff Fairmont Springs Hotel. This was a fairy tale Christmas. The hotel went all out in decorations and activities, and the weather was holiday-perfect. Everything was covered in white snow. We both
Idyllic Christmas in Ban ff, Canada remember a special open-sleigh ride through snow-lined pathways in the countryside. It was Currier and Ives personified!
Our first international vacation was also a memorable time for
Dot and me. We were young and adventurous. Switzerland and
Austria were both beautiful and fascinating. We won’t forget the emotions as we rumbled along the railways and walked the streets of
European cities for the first time.
There have been lots of firsts that stir up emotional memories for me. I can still almost relive every moment of the first major league baseball game I saw in 1957. Likewise, I get goosebumps whenever
I recall my first Broadway musical in 1959 and the stirring overture to My Fair Lady. I remember the first time I voted in an election, my first car, my first home, my first KPMG partners’ meeting, and on and on. Unforgettable experiences.
For some rather odd and unexplainable reason, another unforgettable experience sticks in my mind and creeps out occasionally. In 1990 I was invited to a dinner at the Governor’s mansion in Maryland. It was a cold and rainy evening, and I had to park several blocks away from the mansion. As I walked in the stinging rain along the cobbled streets of Annapolis with my overcoat and umbrella, I was struck by the colonial architecture and gas streetlights of this historic city. Time didn’t stand still; it reversed. I imagined myself a ttending a government function as a founding father two hundred years ago and thinking about the drama of such an event. It was American history brought alive in my consciousness. This was a most unusual and highly emotional fifteen or twenty minutes for me. I’m not sure exactly why. But I have had similar moments at other places, such as following the Lewis &
Clark trail out west. These moments qualify for inclusion in my list of special remembrances.
Births and deaths also qualify. No time travel is complete for me without a mental visit to the births of my three sons. These miraculous events are eternal bright spots in my mind, stirring up emotions about new life and continuation of our own existence.
And the death of my father was a transformative time for me. I saw a good man disappear from view, but at the same time saw his legacy continue in the form of wonderful memories of his character and demeanor.
I’ll round out this rambling list with the inclusion of our fiftieth wedding anniversary celebration, when we gathered the entire family of thirteen for a Caribbean cruise aboard the Oasis of the Seas.
With our family so sca ttered geographically and each having their own employment and other commitments, it is difficult to get them all together at once, but for this occasion we managed it. It was a great time of family sharing and celebration. It ranks near the top of my Hall of Fame of family events over the many years, and I will carry those memories with me to the grave.
There is a seemingly endless list of these special memories. The foregoing random collection is intended to illustrate the wide range of my recollections as I reflect on my life.
Conclusion
As I have pecked away at the stories in this memoir, my consciousness has been piqued by many revelations about myself.
Before this project I had never given too much thought about my historical journey’s impact on my life. I seem to have been consistently focused on the future—or the moment—without a lot of self-reflection or retrospection along the way.
Thumbing through some albums and mementos I have stu ffed away in my relics, I came across a stack of annual goals and priorities that I routinely prepared for many years. The first of these from the early years were crude notes scratched out on yellow lined paper.
Over time, they then progressed to a more comprehensive approach to strategizing all aspects of my personal life. I went through this annual exercise consistently for twenty-five or thirty years. For some reason, I have kept all these private notes. It is interesting now to read through them and reflect on my personal struggle to structure and improve my life. Interestingly, there is not a word in all these documents about past accomplishments. If there is a central theme to all these goals and objectives, it was a forward-thinking focus on improving myself in a wide range of areas and providing a balance in my life. That’s what I was about. I wasn’t looking back.
But now, in my twilight years, it has been cathartic to take a peek into the rear view mirror. I have thoroughly enjoyed the re flection.
I have also been able to re flect on the impact of the world around me. I realize that living in different locales and travelling widely have added spice and substance to my life. Moreover, the variety of these experiences has taught me much about the world I inhabit and the people I have encountered.
I have learned a lot about myself in the process. Before embarking on this memoir, I never had a comprehensive thought about all the help I have had along the way. But as I began to weave these stories, I gradually began to notice a pa ttern of personalities that profoundly shaped my life. T eachers, bosses, mentors, partners,
friends and family began to be seen through a di fferent lens. I don’t believe I ever thought I had been alone in my life’s journey; however,
the level of support received along the way had simply not sunk in.
The new focus on these influences has washed me in a deep emotion of gratitude. I am truly grateful for the enormous leverage all these wonderful people have provided.
I am also immensely grateful for my good fortune. Serendipity has been my strongest ally. Despite all the goal se tting, so much of my good fortune has not been sought; so o ften I merely stumbled upon it. It seems as though I was wearing some sort of magic good luck charm that has inoculated me from most of the maladies that plague so many people. Admi ttedly, there have been a few rough spots for me, but not many. Not even close to the norm. I’m not sure why this is. I suppose I’ll just accept it as one of those queer facts of life and move on. Nevertheless, as a result of these memoirs, I am now acutely aware of my good fortune and extremely grateful for the blessings.
In the introduction, I mentioned a metaphorical jigsaw puzzle box filled with odd-shaped pieces that somehow were supposed to fit together to produce the picture on the cover of the box. Well, my box cover now has hundreds of pieces that include many of the stories I
have recorded. As I gaze at this figurative box cover, I realize those puzzle pieces have taken on a shape and form, roughly similar to the picture I see in the mirror. However, the picture remains a bit fuzzy.
I suppose it will stay that way. But I feel be tter having documented a bit of my life and brought the picture into better focus. Perhaps, as a result, the story of the boy from the red clay hills might be a little clearer for my family as well. I hope so.

Out of the Red Clay Hills is formatted in Arno Pro,
a contemporary typeface, designed by Robert Slimbach and named after the Arno River. The paper is Mohawk Via Satin,
Green Seal Certified acid-free and 30 percent post-consumer waste.

## Principles

- Acknowledge and be grateful for the support and influence of others.
- Maintain consistent goal-setting to drive long-term personal improvement.
- Use retrospection in later life to clarify meaning and legacy.
- Accept serendipity as a meaningful factor in life outcomes.
- Treat major commitments with seriousness and intentionality.

## Decision heuristics

- When unsure how to structure recollections, allow randomness rather than forcing a rigid order.
- Use otherwise idle periods (e.g., long travel) to reflect and document important moments.
- Sustain an annual review practice to guide incremental personal development.
- When making life commitments, state and accept vows with full attention and seriousness.
